Midi Health Becomes The First 'Menopause Unicorn'

Forbes(2.1M) ·16:16 ·665 views · 2026-02-03
Joanna Strober Maggie McGrath
Midi Health has achieved a valuation of over $1 billion, becoming the first "menopause unicorn," following a successful $100 million funding round aimed at accelerating its rapid expansion and addressing critical gaps in women's holistic healthcare.
Here's an executive briefing summarizing the Forbes interview with Joanna Strober, CEO of Midi Health: TL;DR Midi Health has achieved a valuation of over $1 billion, becoming the first "menopause unicorn," following a successful $100 million funding round aimed at accelerating...

Here's an executive briefing summarizing the Forbes interview with Joanna Strober, CEO of Midi Health:

Executive Briefing: Midi Health Achieves Unicorn Status & Rapid Expansion

TL;DR

Midi Health has achieved a valuation of over $1 billion, becoming the first "menopause unicorn," following a successful $100 million funding round aimed at accelerating its rapid expansion and addressing critical gaps in women's holistic healthcare.

Unicorn Status & Funding Achievement

  • Valuation Milestone: Midi Health has officially reached a valuation of over $1 billion, making it the first "menopause unicorn."
  • Successful Funding Round: The company closed a $100 million funding round.
      • Lead Investor: Goodwater Capital.
      • New Investors: Foresite Capital and Serena Ventures.
      • Existing Investor Participation: All prior investors also participated in the round.
  • Fundraising Context: This was an opportunistic round, driven by Midi's rapid growth rather than a pre-planned fundraising cycle.
      • Evolving Landscape: Contrasts sharply with earlier difficulties in securing initial capital, reflecting a growing market recognition of women's health as a significant economic opportunity.

Hyper-Growth and Expanding Care Aperture

  • Exponential Patient Growth: Midi has more than doubled its patient base, now treating over 25,000 women per week, up from ~12,000 per week less than a year ago.
  • Nationwide Reach: Provides care and has insurance coverage in all 50 states.
      • High Demand: Current demand is so significant that wait times can exceed one month in some states.
  • Holistic Care Evolution: While initially focused on perimenopause and menopause, Midi's scope has broadened significantly to holistic women's health.
      • Primary Care Role: Increasingly acts as a primary care provider for many women due to systemic care gaps, addressing issues beyond initial symptoms (e.g., pre-diabetes, connecting to cancer care, migraines, PCOS).
      • Age Range Expansion: Now serves women from their 20s to 80s, driven by patient needs and broader health concerns.
  • Efficiency of Virtual Care: Virtual appointments save patients approximately 3.5 hours per visit compared to traditional in-person care, offering convenient 30-minute online consultations.

Strategic Initiatives and Future Directions

  • Economic Impact of Unmet Needs: Untreated menopause symptoms contribute an estimated $25 billion annually in medical expenses and lost work productivity.
      • Cost Savings Focus: Midi aims to save the healthcare system significant costs by providing appropriate, early intervention and reducing the need for specialist care or unnecessary procedures.
      • Addressing SSRI Overuse: Research will examine the potential to reduce inappropriate antidepressant use, as 20% of women in their target age range are on SSRIs.
  • New Research Institute: Launching to leverage data from its 300,000+ patients to better understand cost savings, patient benefits, and personalize care (e.g., drug targeting based on genetics and health history).
  • Leveraging AI for Quality: Investing in AI technologies to enhance care efficiency, ensure up-to-date treatment protocols, and monitor quality.
  • New Care Lines: Plans to launch new services addressing metabolic health, weight management, musculoskeletal health, and long-term wellness.

Operational Scaling and Leadership for the Future

  • Maintaining High Standards: Acknowledges the challenge of sustaining high-quality care amidst rapid growth.
      • Technology & Training: Investing in AI for efficient provider training and quality monitoring, alongside significant resources for hands-on clinician training.
  • Key Executive Hire: Appointed Jason Wheeler, former executive at Tesla and Google, as Chief Financial Officer.
      • Strategic Rationale: Wheeler was brought out of retirement to help build an "efficient, strong operation" that can scale effectively.
  • Scalability Confidence: Early growth phases felt challenging, but with established infrastructure, Midi is now confident in scaling more efficiently to serve millions of women.
  • Long-Term Vision: The core goal remains to bridge the enormous women's healthcare gap, ensuring access to high-quality care for women across all demographics, from rural factory workers to urban CEOs.

Notable Quotes

"Yes, we're a menopausal unicorn. The first time." — *Joanna Strober, Cofounder and CEO, Midi Health* "The thing that's exciting to me is that women's health is becoming acknowledged as a really big economic opportunity." — *Joanna Strober, Cofounder and CEO, Midi Health* "For many of the women coming to us, we actually become their primary care doctor... we have very much become both a place that women come to get their symptoms taken care of, but also a preventive care company and a primary care company." — *Joanna Strober, Cofounder and CEO, Midi Health* "Honestly, Maggie, I'm just focused on taking care of more women. That's the goal. It really is. I think we can be taking care of millions of women." — *Joanna Strober, Cofounder and CEO, Midi Health*
Hi everyone, I'm Maggie McGrath, editor of Forbes Women. In 2024, Forbes named Midi Health as one of its next billion-dollar startups. The company is focused on women's health and specifically perimenopause and menopause. Today, we're joined by founder and CEO Joanna Strober with an update as to whether or not our prediction about Midi becoming a unicorn is coming to fruition. Joanna, thanks for joining us. Yes, we're a menopausal unicorn – the first time! You're a menopausal unicorn. So, we were correct when we named Midi a next billion-dollar startup company. Today, you are announcing that Midi Health has reached a more than $1 billion valuation. Let's talk about what got you there. It's clearly around funding. Were you spending all of 2025 fundraising? You didn't really seem like you were in fundraising mode the last time we spoke, which was spring of last year. No, actually, we've been very focused on growth, to be honest. We are growing very rapidly, and it's exciting. We are taking care of increasing numbers of women, and they are coming to us for a much broader range of care than we originally started taking care of. So, we're definitely broadening the aperture of care that we're giving. The fundraising actually came together very quickly because we needed capital to keep growing fast. And so, it's a $100 million round. Is that correct? Yes, we raised $100 million led by Goodwater Capital with new investors, Foresight and Serena Ventures. All of our old investors participated as well. It was interesting to me to see this news because I know we've talked about this more than once: that very first round of fundraising you did in Midi's earliest days was difficult. Talk to us about the fundraising landscape in 2025. We've been talking about how menopause has collectively, across your peer set and across culture, become more of a – I hate to use the word – hot topic. Were investors banging down the door, or was this an opportunistic round, or did you go around saying, "We're growing faster than the capital we have can facilitate?" It really was opportunistic. There was a lot of capital that was available for us. Honestly, we probably could have raised a lot more. The thing that's exciting to me is that women's health is becoming acknowledged as a really big economic opportunity. When I first started the company, that was not the case. Just not that many years ago, I don't think people understood what a big opportunity women's health is. Women are using their dollars, and they're looking for better health care, and so they are proactively looking for places that can better take care of them, and they're finding places like Midi and they're spending their dollars there. So, I think society is catching up to the fact that women are looking for better care. It's also, I guess, from Field of Dreams, "if you build it, they will come." It's a Catch-22, right? This opportunity has probably been around for a long time, but women weren't able to raise the capital to build the companies to then prove that there was this demand. So, the fact that we've been able to raise the capital to build something that's really valuable, and now women are coming, it's definitely a circle. But I think now that there is capital, we can build companies that really matter and that can really impact women's lives, and women are getting care from those companies. You've talked about how in the early days of the company, you did a three-month trial period with a certain test group. And then when you were about to end the trial period, these women came to you and said, "No, no, no. We need your nutrition advice. We need the coaching. We need everything we're getting from you." And that was kind of the earliest proof in the pudding. Fast forward to spring of last year, the last time you and I sat down together, you said you were treating about 12,000 women per week. What's the rate of customer acquisition or patient treatment that you're seeing today? Over 25,000 patients a week across the country, in all 50 states. We have insurance coverage in all 50 states. We are hiring rapidly. We're hiring and training clinicians rapidly. In some states right now, you can't get in for over a month. So, the demand is huge. Our job really is to ensure that we have really high-quality care and that we keep up with the demand that we are seeing. The other thing that we're finding is that there's not just a women's care gap in the country; there's a primary care doctor gap. For many of the women coming to us, we actually become their primary care doctor. They might come to us because they have hot flashes or night sweats or anxiety or joint pain, but then we get them a blood test and we find out they're pre-diabetic, and so we start taking care of that. We're getting them mammograms and we're finding out that they have cancer. So, we're taking care of that – not taking care of the cancer, but getting them connected to care. But we have very much become both a place that women come to get their symptoms taken care of, and also a preventive care company and a primary care company. So, we're following the women and we're giving them the care that they're looking for, but the gap in care, I would say, is bigger than we even thought it was. So, we're just taking care of a lot more things because women are asking us to do that. Have you tracked how much time you're saving patients hypothetically? Are they seeing someone through Midi within maybe a week or two of requesting an appointment versus a month or two if they were to go the traditional route, or is that not a calculus that you have yet? I don't know. We absolutely are trying very hard to get people in within a week. That's our goal. We're not always succeeding, but that's our goal. But honestly, do you know that the average in-person visit takes someone three and a half hours to go to? By the time you leave work and you drive and you sit in the office and then you wait and then you eventually come back to your office, it's a half day off of work. Our visits are a half hour online wherever you are. So, the amount of time we're saving people just by doing virtual care is actually quite significant. Joanna, I've had back-to-back dentist appointments in the past two days, so I know that three and a half hour time suck better than I normally would. So, that statistic, I fact-check: true. Now, Midi and you have said that untreated symptoms of menopause can cost an estimated $25 billion annually in medical expenses and lost work productivity. How often does that figure come up in conversation with your investors, or are they more interested in the AI technologies that you're developing or hiring to shorten that wait time that I just referenced? What's the value proposition that you're having in these investor meetings? I want to explain that there are different constituents that we are reaching. I think the savings of cost is incredibly important. When you think about it, if someone comes to Midi, they probably don't need a psychiatrist. They might not need a psychologist. They're probably avoiding a lot of specialty care and a lot of unnecessary procedures. So, we, as a company, are very focused on the cost that we can save the system when women get the appropriate care early in their care journey. A lot of the work that we are doing – we're actually starting a research institute to really better understand the cost savings and the benefits to women that we are able to give by giving them appropriate care early. One of the things I'm actually focused on is the overuse of SSRIs, anxiety and depression medication. 20% of women in our age category are on antidepressant medications, and I think for many of them it is inappropriate care. So, one of the things we're looking at is whether we can take them off of that and use other solutions instead. We're doing a lot of health research to understand better how to take care of women and ensure they get really high-quality care. With regard to our investment dollars, we are absolutely focused on how to make the highest quality care most efficient. So, we're looking very carefully at AI because there's a lot that we can do with AI to make sure the protocols are up to date, to make sure that everything that we're doing is really efficient and really high quality, and it's exciting what we can do with that. I want to go back to that research institute because that's a really interesting business opportunity for you. When did that become an idea, and how much was that affected by what's happening at the federal level with investment in health research, or rather, the lack thereof? We've seen billions of dollars pulled back. Yes, that's right. Billions of dollars have been pulled back, but also people don't have the data. We have learned that we have 300,000 women who are our patients right now. That means we have really good cross-sectional data of what works for women. When you think about a drug company, they're only testing their one drug. They're not testing their drug across five other drugs. So, we're really excited to be able to look across this huge population of women to much better target the right drug to the right person based on their genetics, based on their family history, based on their health history. I think over time, I'm just incredibly excited about the impact that our research will be able to have on improving better care for women. In the release that came out today, there was a line saying that Midi will launch new care lines addressing metabolic health, weight management, musculoskeletal health, and long-term wellness. We've talked about this before. So, a two-part question: One, is it inaccurate to call Midi a menopause company? Is it more holistic women's health? And two, what can you share about those new health care lines that Midi might be launching? Menopause care is holistic women's health. That is my learning. It's not one or the other. Menopause just means you haven't had your period for a year. Taking care of women in perimenopause and in menopause is holistic care, and that really is what we do. What we found is that women are sending their daughters to us saying they're having migraines, asking if that's related to their periods, and if we can help them. They're having PCOS. They're having other health issues, asking if we can help them. So, we are expanding the aperture very much in relation to the women who are coming to us. We have 80-year-old women who are coming to us, and we have 20-year-old women coming to us, and we've been much more proactive in creating care protocols for them too. So, we are much more than just a midlife women's care company, even though that is still the bulk of our audience. The last time we spoke, you talked about how one of your concerns in growing so quickly is maintaining the standard of care, the training of your nurse practitioners and the practitioners who see patients. With this raise, will you be hiring more people? If so, how do you continue to ensure that standard of care remains the same, because that's a challenge for a lot of startup founders, whether or not they're in healthcare, is maintaining that standard of training across a growing employee base. It's key to the success of our company. There's a lot you can do now with AI that can enable us to train better and to ensure high-quality care. I'm very excited that the technological changes are enabling us to more efficiently train people, more efficiently monitor quality. There's just a lot that we can do with technology to ensure high-quality care, but we're also investing in the nitty-gritty of training people. So, we're actually putting a lot of resources into training our providers and making sure that we're only hiring really high-quality providers, because in the end, our value to the world is that we're providing really high-quality care. It is our care providers that are doing that, so we need to really ensure quality, and a lot of our effort is focused there. Speaking of hiring, you are also announcing in today's release that you have appointed Jason Wheeler as Chief Financial Officer. He was a former executive at Tesla and Google. Can you talk about why it was important to hire him and what it signals about Midi's future? Is there an IPO in your future, perhaps? Jason was retired. We reached out to him and we were asking him if he had any suggestions of people who he thought we should hire to be our CFO, and he said, "Me, I might be interested in coming out of retirement to do this." So, we are incredibly fortunate to have him join us. We are building a big operation, and his experience at Google and Tesla of building a really efficient, strong operation is incredibly valuable to us. I am really focused on the fact that we need to build a very efficient, high-quality organization that scales, and he, in his early time with us, has really been helping us to do that. So, no comment on an IPO just yet. No comment on an IPO just yet. But you are growing quickly. I feel like every time we speak, you talk about the rate of growth. As you look back at the past several years of the company, was there an inflection point where that growth tipped from regular growth to hyper-charge growth? One of my investors, I think we were doing about a thousand visits a week, and I was like, "Oh my god, do you think we can get to 2,000?" And then it got to 2,000, and it got to 5,000, and now we're over 25,000. Honestly, it's getting easier to understand how it can be a really big company at this point. Initially, I was nervous if we could do 10,000, then we saw we could, and we can. Now we actually have enough infrastructure in place that we can scale much more efficiently than we could then. So, I actually feel much more comfortable about our growth than I did when we were much smaller because we have a lot of infrastructure that we've built to enable us to scale more efficiently. Today's news is big. Midi is a unicorn company. It has a billion-dollar valuation. That's a lot to report for one day. But I always have to ask Joanna, if we were to speak again later this year or one year from now, what would you hope to report that Midi has accomplished in the ensuing year? Honestly, Maggie, I'm just focused on taking care of more women. That's the goal. It really is. I think we can be taking care of millions of women. I think the women's care gap is enormous, and there is an opportunity to serve lots of women for lots of health issues and ensure that all women have access to high-quality care wherever they're located. One of the things I'm actually most proud of as a CEO is that we have the CEOs of a number of the most important companies in this country as our patients, and we have a lot of factory workers as our patients. Our patient base is all over the country. It is rural. It is urban. Women are just not getting access to care, and we are able to fill that gap. So, that's really where I'm focused on, is how we can most effectively fill the care gaps. Joanna Strober, co-founder and CEO of Midi Health, thank you so much for joining us as always. We appreciate being in the loop on every update you guys put out. Thank you. This was really fun.

Watch: Citadel CEO Ken Griffin at WSJ Invest Live | WSJ

Executive Briefing: Ken Griffin at WSJ Invest Live TL;DR Citadel CEO Ken Griffin outlined his concerns over US fiscal policy, government overreach in business, and eroding public ethics, while defending the dollar's long-term strength and explaining Citadel's strategic shift to...

Executive Briefing: Ken Griffin at WSJ Invest Live

TL;DR Citadel CEO Ken Griffin outlined his concerns over US fiscal policy, government overreach in business, and eroding public ethics, while defending the dollar's long-term strength and explaining Citadel's strategic shift to Florida due to quality of life issues.

Macroeconomic Outlook & US Fiscal Health

  • Dollar Primacy:
      • The US dollar has "lost some of its luster" over the past 12 months but remains one of the world's great safe harbors.
      • US geopolitical strength (e.g., "Mission Impossible"-like military mission in Venezuela) underpins confidence.
      • America's capital markets are "breathtakingly" strong, allowing companies like Anthropic to raise $10 billion in days, a stark contrast to Europe.
      • A strong reserve currency reduces capital costs, lowers interest rates, and enhances living standards.
  • National Debt & Fiscal Discipline:
      • National debt now exceeds $38 trillion, a critical concern.
      • The US is "late in an economic cycle" yet still running a significant deficit, which should be closer to break-even.
      • Warns that deferring decisions on fiscal discipline will lead to "far more pain 20 years down the road," citing the uncertain future of Social Security for younger generations.
      • Advocates for increased fiscal discipline in both spending and revenue generation, calling for bipartisan agreement akin to the "Simpson-Bowles" committee.
  • Inflation Concerns:
      • A 3% inflation rate is "grating to tens of millions of American households" and was a key issue in past elections.
      • Credits the administration for shifting the dialogue to "affordability" and taking steps like appointing a credible Fed chairman (Kevin Walsh) and rolling back some tariffs.

Business Environment & Government Overreach

  • Impact of Regulatory Uncertainty:
      • Frequent changes to "rules of the road" by Washington (e.g., tariffs) make long-term investment decisions (3, 5, 10, 20+ year horizons) extremely difficult.
      • Unpredictability can lead businesses to make "no decision" at all, hindering capital investment and manufacturing growth.
  • Critique of Government as VC (Crony Capitalism):
      • Acknowledges government's important role in consumer protection and preventing externalities (like pollution).
      • However, criticizes current government involvement in corporate America as "distinctly different," particularly government investments in companies (e.g., "green energy companies" that ended in bankruptcy).
      • States the US has a robust venture capital community and "doesn't need the US government to pretend to be venture capitalists."
      • Distaste for Favoritism: Most CEOs find government engagement that "tastes of favoritism" incredibly distasteful, preferring to compete on merit rather than regulatory favors.
  • Silencing the Corporate Voice:
      • The "woke movement" and social media backlash have created "fear and apprehension" among CEOs, causing them to withdraw from public commentary.
      • Argues that the voices of corporate leaders, who create jobs, are "very important" in Washington and public discourse, rather than being "completely extinguished."
  • Ethics in Public Service:
      • Expressed deep concern over "missteps" in the administration leading to personal enrichment and conflicts of interest (e.g., $500 million donation to a Trump crypto vehicle, Supreme Court issues).
      • States he is not optimistic about a return to strong ethics without continued scrutiny from the press, which serves as a "very important check."

Personal Insights & Societal Challenges

  • Investment Philosophy:
      • Portfolio Purpose: Investors must focus on the purpose of their portfolio based on age and objectives (e.g., 20s: equities; 70s: inflation-protected assets like TIPS or commercial real estate).
      • Personal Investment: As the largest investor in Citadel's hedge fund, his capital is deployed across alternative strategies with "dozens of the world's best investors."
  • Citadel's Move to Florida:
      • Pandemic Response: Initial move to Palm Beach during the pandemic was strategic due to science suggesting lower respiratory virus transmissibility in hot, humid environments. Florida government was "110% behind" them in converting the Four Seasons into a trading floor.
      • Quality of Life: A senior partner's question ("Why did we come back?") prompted a re-evaluation, concluding that Florida offered "safer streets, better schools, the weather's certainly better."
      • Chicago Concerns: Chicago was "gripped in violence," schools were "failing," and recruiting talent was hard due to negative headlines.
      • Florida's Vibrancy: Miami is now Citadel's third-largest office globally, thriving with "positive vibrancy and energy." His art collection also moved to Palm Beach.
  • AI's Real Impact:
      • Does not believe AI will be a major election issue this cycle.
      • Suggests AI is often used as an "excuse" for companies to trim workforces, but objectively, "very few businesses are actually seeing productivity gains that come anywhere close to the headline of job losses."
  • Public Service Ambitions:
      • Has a "deep interest in public policy issues" and studied economics and government at Harvard.
      • Believes that "at a future point in my life, I will be involved in public service."
      • Currently, he can effect change through philanthropic efforts (e.g., Operation Warp Speed, internet access for Chicago children) and by influencing policymakers through existing ties.

Notable Quotes

"If you're in your 20s, even though the equity markets is somewhat frothy right now, you're still going to be investing the preponderance of that money in in equity markets around the world. And if you're in your mid70s, obviously you want to... worry about inflation... you're going to have much more of that money invested in tips or in commercial real estate or in other assets that have greater protection from the potential damaging influence of inflation." – Ken Griffin "The fact that we're still running a very large annual deficit does tell you that that too much the economy is being supported by the sugar high of of fiscal spending. We need to dial that back. We need to have more discipline in both both spending and thoughtfulness and how we generate revenues. Like we need to put our fiscal house in order." – Ken Griffin "When the US government starts to engage in corporate America in a way that that tastes of favoritism, I know for most CEOs that I'm friends with, they find it incredibly distasteful. Like we want to go run our businesses and win on the merits of providing a better customer to our products at a lower price. Like that's how we win." – Ken Griffin "The power of social media to persuade millions or tens of millions of consumers to make a product choice is really terrifying to corporate executives. And I think it's it's put them in a very just intrinsically like withdrawn position." – Ken Griffin
Thank you very much, Gunjan. And good morning everyone. And a big thank you to Ken for joining us today for a discussion about who knows where it's going to take us, Ken. We'll see. But it's almost impossible to know where to start. There's so much going on in the markets, in the world, certainly in the news cycle. So I thought I'd start with a very simple question. Somebody's just handed you a suitcase of freshly minted dollars. What are you going to do with those dollars? This sounds like a trick asset. A know your customer ALM question. Sounds like I need to call the FBI and go, I've got a suitcase full of cash here. Okay. After you've done that and they've said it's fine, you can keep them. So, we've got clean money. Clean money.
