Yes, 55 is freezing, but it's not truly freezing. If I was handed a suitcase of money today, this is such a strange way to frame a question. Look, what investors need to focus on is what is the purpose of their portfolio. If you're in your early 20s, your investment objective is very different than if you're in your mid-70s, and you need to always invest your money from the vantage point of what you need to achieve with your investment portfolio. If you're in your 20s, even though the equity markets are frothy right now, you're still going to be investing the preponderance of that money in equity markets around the world. If you're in your mid-70s, obviously you worry about inflation. You worry about downside risk. You worry about the fact that you don't have 20 or 30 years potentially as your investment horizon. You're going to have much more of that money invested in TIPS or in commercial real estate or in other assets that have greater protection from the potential damaging influence of inflation. But let's say the investor is you. It's your money. Where would you put it? I actually know my investment portfolio, and my partners and I at Citadel, we're the largest investor in our hedge fund, and we deploy that capital across a variety of alternative investment strategies. But that's a very different question because I have a chance to work with dozens of the world's best investors, and they get to deploy the capital to partnership across really interesting opportunities from the energy market to equities, and it's a really fun place to be a part of. Good. What he hasn't said is what he said to me backstage, which is he'd put it under his mattress. But... She had way too much fun with that. Right. Okay. On to more serious matters. Debt. You've always made it very clear that you think debt levels in this country have gotten too high. The national debt is now running at exceeding $38 trillion, and off the back of that, there's some evidence of a sell America trade going on. My question to you is, do you think we're witnessing the early stages of a genuine challenge to dollar primacy? Look, the US dollar has lost some of its luster over the last 12 months. There's no doubt about that. I do believe that the United States is unquestionably still one of the great safe harbors in the world. There's a bit of an irony here. The United States, from a geopolitical perspective, is asserting its strength in very profound ways. If we look, for example, what happened in Venezuela just a few days ago, the United States pulled off a military mission that was almost like it was out of a Mission Impossible movie. Yeah. Can you believe we actually extracted the leader of a narco-terrorism organization from his bed in his home country? It's incomprehensible. But the US military today has really reshaped the conception of what the United States is able to accomplish from a national security perspective. At the same time, policies relating to tariffs, some of the rhetoric from the administration has taken some of the shine off of the dollar. At the end of the day, I do believe that when it's all said and done, if you are the strongest nation in the world, you are going to be predisposed to having a strong currency. That strong currency, that reserve currency status, reduces your cost to capital, brings down interest rates, all else being equal, increases the quality of living for those citizens of that nation, and allows us to engage in the global economy on a much stronger footing. Yes, it makes exports a bit more challenging, but the fact that we can amass so much capital and deploy it across corporate America is stunning. If you think about it, there are very few companies in Europe with a market cap of north of $10 billion. Anthropic raised that in a couple days just recently to fuel their continued growth in their AI business. The juxtaposition between the strength of America's capital markets and virtually every other country in the world is breathtaking. And we want to protect that ability for American firms to raise tens of billions or hundreds of billions of dollars. Whether to build hyperscale data centers, or to pursue pharmaceutical R&D, leaves us in an envied position by the rest of the world. So what do you think the administration needs to do to make sure that supremacy is maintained? Is it on the right fiscal track to do that? We need to increase fiscal discipline in the United States. We're late in an economic cycle. We don't know if we're in the sixth inning or seventh inning or eighth inning, but very few cycles run as long as this cycle has run. And we're still running a significant deficit. At this point in the economic cycle, we should be running close to a break-even. If you're not paying down your national debt at moments like this, when will you pay it down? And the fact that we're still running a very large annual deficit tells you that too much of the economy is being supported by the sugar high of fiscal spending. We need to dial that back. We need to have more discipline in both spending and thoughtfulness in how we generate revenues. We need to put our fiscal house in order. I do worry that's lost attention and focus in Washington. I know the president has to be frustrated. His first term, his tax cuts were about reigniting growth in America, to get Americans to in essence be bolder again. How do we increase investment? How do we increase productivity? He's trying to play the same playbook again. But what I think is being missed in this analysis is the incredible amount of spending during the pandemic, that three-year era of profligate, out-of-control spending. We need to deal with the reality that we need to pay that debt down. America did this after World War II, and America should be doing that again here today. And how, politically though, it's a difficult needle to thread that one. How would you, if you were the president, what would you say to him? What should he do to bring that deficit down? Look, in the United States, I'm not sure if I have the exact pronunciation right, but Simpson-Bowles was the committee formed decades ago to address this issue in a prior administration. You need, and I know this sounds almost fanciful, you need to get bipartisan agreement on the steps we're going to take to put our fiscal house in order. Here's the big issue: politicians deferring some of these decisions means that the impact of future decisions will be so much more painful for the American people. That's what we're really doing: we're not deferring some fixed amount of pain, we're going to cause far more pain 20 years down the road. Could you imagine today being in your 20s and seeing social security come out of your paycheck each and every year? Will the government safety net be there for you when it's your turn to retire? That's a legitimate question given the level of deficit spending we have today. Another area of pain, certainly one that you've highlighted a lot, is that of tariffs, which for the last year has been this flip-flopping story. One minute they're up, one minute they're down, one country is up. Only yesterday we learned that tariffs on India are going to be cut to 18%. How difficult is it to come up with an investment strategy thesis when this backdrop keeps changing all the time? I've seen my colleagues firsthand have to grapple with this problem over the last year. All of us do in the money management business. How do you create a portfolio when every single company that you invest in can have the terms of engagement changed by the stroke of a pen in Washington? This goes to, you often hear business people say, "Just don't change the rules." And you sit there and go, "Are they that inflexible? Are they that unwilling to change? Can't they go with the flow?" But the problem is that when you're running a business and you're trying to grow that business, you're making decisions that have horizons often of three years, five years, 10 years, 20 years. We're building a new office building in New York for Citadel. That's a 50 to 100-year horizon decision. If you tell me the rules of the road are going to change every couple of years, you make that decision a far more difficult choice. If you tell me the rules of the road are going to change every couple months, I'm best off making no decision. Yeah. Washington needs to think about what is the pace of change it's trying to create in the economy. Having certainty or a higher degree of confidence what the rules of the road will be will actually help the president achieve his goal of creating more capital investment in the United States and strengthening America's manufacturing base. Talking of certainty, there was a big announcement last week, a new Fed chairman, Kevin Walsh. I wondered what you made of that appointment and also how you think he's going to manage in what's clearly going to be quite a tricky role. Look, the front runners in the choice coming into that decision day were Rick and Kevin. Both were outstanding candidates for the role, and I applaud the president for picking somebody who has, and both choices were great choices, but either choice would have represented choosing a professional with credibility with global investors who understands economics, who will carry on the role of the chairman of the Fed to make the decisions that we need to make to balance the risk of inflation and trying to maximize full employment. I think the president made a really solid choice with Kevin, but I'm more impressed by a process that brought us to two really strong candidates at the finish line. There's been a lot of debate around whether or not this administration was going to undermine the independence of the Fed, and frankly, they did some things that made that a legitimate question. But the choice by the president here, I think, is a powerful assertion that the Fed will maintain needed independence in policy decision-making. On the issue of inflation, you have said that you wouldn't underestimate how grating a 3% inflation rate would be to tens of millions of American households. And you said that unless they get that under control, it could cost them the midterms. What's your message to the White House as we head into the midterms in November? It's really interesting that there are a couple of issues that the president won this election with. One was the border and the chaos that we had on the southern border of the United States. The president has been true to his word. He has secured the border. There is no doubt that there's a huge sigh of relief across our country that we're now in a position to pursue a thoughtful legal immigration policy. That's a huge win by the president. The second area that was important or fundamental to the president winning the White House was the inflation of the Biden administration. Prices in America rose at a stunning clip because of the same profligate fiscal spending we spoke about just a few minutes ago. What we saw was the American voter doesn't like the inflation genie out of the bottle. In particular, if you're in your 50s or 60s or 70s and you're seeing your purchasing power of your savings eroded, that's a very frightening place to be. Now, I give the Democrats real credit. In 12 months, they've changed the dialogue from inflation, where they got killed by this administration in the elections, to the dialogue being affordability. We changed one word. Yeah. But what we're really talking about is the same set of issues, which is that for the American people, prices are a lot higher than they were five years ago. The president is very right to be focused on how to respond to the voters' angst and anxiety over inflation. The appointment of Kevin is a step in the right direction. The rolling back of tariffs on a number of goods that consumers buy day in and day out is a step in the right direction. The focus on housing affordability is a step in the right direction. I really do hope the president is able to make enough moves over the next few months to convince the American voters that he really is passionate about controlling the issue of inflation in the United States. I wanted to ask you about the dangers of crony capitalism. You are a big free market champion. You always have been. But there has been, and you were very clear that you didn't like the regulatory burden of the previous administration, very outspoken on that. But now you've got a situation where the government is taking stakes in companies. You've got talk about a cap on interest on credit cards. You've got all the interference with tariffs and the attempt to reshore manufacturing. Do you think there's a role for that interference given that America has to take a stand against the biggest crony capitalist of all, China, or does that behavior make you shudder? Let's take a huge step back. Government has a really important role to play in the economy. It has an important role in the economy to ensure that consumers have fair and reasonable disclosure. When you're a consumer that picks a credit card, you should be able to get through the fine print pretty quickly and understand the cost and interest rates you're going to pay. Government has a really important role in the economy in preventing externalities, the archetype of that being pollution. You can't just build a factory and dump your waste into the river in the backyard. I think it's important to appreciate that no one when they argue for less regulation is arguing for a reversal of these very important policies that protect the safety and health welfare of the American people. But what we are seeing today in Washington is something different. We're seeing this involvement of government in corporate America in a distinctly different way than 20 or 30 years ago. We saw the green shoots of this in the Obama and Biden administration when the government started to invest into the various green energy companies, and you and I both know a number of those companies ended up in bankruptcy, and all the money just disappeared. And as a taxpayer, that's really frustrating. We have an incredible venture capital community in the United States. We don't need the US government to pretend to be venture capitalists. They're not. You've got Sequoia and New Enterprise and A16. You've got tremendous number of great VC firms in the United States. You don't need the US government to pretend to be something it's not. When the US government starts to engage in corporate America in a way that tastes of favoritism, I know for most CEOs that I'm friends with, they find it incredibly distasteful. We want to run our businesses and win on the merits of providing a better product to our customers at a lower price. That's how we win. When you start to say that you're going to win or lose because you get a regulatory favor out of Washington, does that mean the next administration is going to grant a favor to one of my competitors or take a favor away from me because I don't support them publicly? Most CEOs just don't want to find themselves in the business of having to suck up to one administration after another to succeed in running their business. They want to focus on better products, better marketing, better distribution, more value created for their customers, some of which is shared with their shareholders. These are all great points. We live in a world in a country where people really look up to corporate leaders. Why is it so difficult for the corporate world to voice publicly the thing you're saying now? I think there are a couple of challenges that corporate executives face on this front. Companies that found themselves in the middle of the whole woke movement would find their products either embraced or ostracized by tens of millions of Americans overnight. That's created a level of fear and apprehension amongst the corporate CEO class to insert themselves in any publicly facing issues these days. The power of social media to persuade millions or tens of millions of consumers to make a product choice is really terrifying to corporate executives. I think it's put them in a very withdrawn position. Yeah. I think we want to think about this. Is it really good that the people that are so focused, they create the jobs that we all need in our lives? Do we really want to have their voices completely extinguished? They have very important voices. Otherwise, you're crowding out, and you get the voices of Mandami. He's never built anything. We need the voices of America's corporate leadership in the halls of Washington, in the front page of papers, to talk about the issues that we need to have for domestic prosperity. Social media has been a huge impact on the voice of corporate America. Even just look at the backlash on Elon Musk. We can quibble back and forth, and we can do more than quibble, about some of the choices or things that he said. But the most successful business person in the United States walked away from his companies to commit himself to public service for months. We may not love everything he did, but we should admire that willingness to give up oneself to make our country better. While we're on this subject, I wanted to ask you about a trend of people in the administration using their positions to enrich themselves personally. We did a story a couple of days ago about a half a billion dollar donation that was made from an Abu Dhabi royal to the Trump crypto vehicle two days before the inauguration. Does that behavior matter to you? Does it bother you? Of course it bothers me. One of the things you want to believe is that those who serve the public interest have the public interest at heart in everything they do. I think this administration has definitely made missteps in choosing decisions or courses that have been very enriching to the families of those in the administration. That calls into question if the public interest is being served. I think there's a necessity for us as a society to re-embrace some of the critical concepts of ethics in public service. We saw the same problem with the Supreme Court several years ago. Wherever you see the signs of conflicts of interest, you give rise to concerns about if the interests of the public are being put first and foremost by those in public service. Are you optimistic that we're on a trajectory to get back to a world where these ethics take center stage? No. You're not. No, I'm not. Well, that's very depressing. I think it's more important to be objective about it because that will then create the dialogue that maybe will permit us to effectuate change. But if we say, "Of course it'll get better," that actually misses the big picture, which is, for example, the work that you did at the journal in exposing this story is the very work that we need to see done day in and day out to keep the American public informed about the behaviors of our politicians on both sides of the aisle. Absolutely. And to help create, the press has always been a very important part of the checks and balances in American society. It's a very important check on curtailing these types of conflicts of interest. I haven't got much time and there's so much I want to ask you. Very quickly, so that we get to everything, on AI. Do you think there's obviously so much excitement around AI? This incredible innovative new technology. It's going to change everything. It's going to lead to these huge productivity gains. Quite possibly already is. But there is also a creeping sense that I've picked up, certainly at Davos, that people are beginning to think, 'Have we really thought hard enough about the impact it's going to have on society?' Do you think AI is going to come more to the fore this year as a potentially political issue? That's a great question. I don't think it's going to be a major election issue in this cycle. I will dread these words in nine months. You've caught me thinking through a problem here. One of the challenges that exists is during the pandemic, the labor markets were very tight. It was very hard to hire people, and across corporate America, companies hoarded labor. A number of friends in the tech space would tell you openly that their workforces were 20% or 30% bigger than need be, but they didn't want to let anybody go because no one knew what work from home was going to mean in terms of productivity. Most of the country has gone back to work in the office, but in that transition, there was a lot of turnover of people. Citadel put in place a 'thou must come back to the office' policy very early, and we lost a few percent of our workforce over that. For us, it was worth it for the collaboration that goes with that. These were the kinds of issues that corporate America was navigating. The employment market today is still reasonably robust, but it's not as tight as it was two years ago. Companies are now saying, 'I can trim some of my workforce in areas that are not strategic. I can tighten my belt a little bit here at this moment in time.' What a great headline: 'I'm sorry I'm letting you go because we've introduced AI in our business.' It's much kinder and gentler than saying, 'I've kind of employed you for the last three years, but I don't really need you.' I think AI has gotten a lot of very negative headlines in terms of being the excuse that companies have used to trim their workforces down, but objectively, I think very few businesses are actually seeing productivity gains that come anywhere close to the headline of job losses that we have seen. I haven't seen it. To other matters altogether, we're here in this beautiful art museum. You are a very prolific art collector, and I understand you moved a lot of your collection here from Chicago. Why did you make the decision to do that? To move from Chicago, or to move my art from Chicago? Indeed. I think that's actually the more important question. We're in Palm Beach, Florida. At the start of the pandemic, it's a respiratory virus, and most respiratory viruses are less transmissible in hot, humid environments. We opened an office in one of the hottest, most humid parts of the United States that we could find. We rented the entire Four Seasons just down the street. In about five days, we assembled enough technology and infrastructure that we could run the entire US equity market from the ballroom of the Four Seasons in Palm Beach, Florida. It was one of the coolest things I've ever seen done. We had 200 people working three shifts, five days straight. They brought in a portable truck with a generator that used a hurricane to power the thousands of servers. Why am I sharing this? Because the government of the state of Florida was 110% behind us in transforming the Four Seasons into NY South. There was a sense of 'we're in a national crisis and we will be a constructive partner with you' as you literally turn a hotel into a trading floor. The people that came here lived in a bubble. You could not come and go from the hotel property, period. No one was allowed to come or go. Think about the conditions they lived under, which were actually pretty good in retrospect because at least they had the company of a few hundred of their friends and family. When they moved back to Chicago, one of my most senior partners came to my office and said, 'Why did we come back?' I said, 'What do you mean?' He goes, 'Quality life in Florida is better. In particular, the streets are safer. The schools are better. The weather's certainly better. Why do we come back? Why do we come back to a city that is gripped in violence, where the schools are failing the children, where it's very hard to recruit talent today because of these headlines? Why do we come back?' Since that moment in time, Miami has become our third largest office in the world, far bigger than Chicago. Everyone who's moved here has been extraordinarily pleased to be in a community which has so much positive vibrancy and energy. It's really fun to go out with friends at night and talk about how your kids are doing and what you're building and where your life's going. Whereas in Chicago, you frankly often end up talking about violent crime because it touched your life in such a profound way each and every day. Having said that, this being home, this will be my home for the rest of my life. Of course, things like my art collection moved from Chicago to Palm Beach. Okay, fair enough. It's not complicated once you understand the big picture. Good. I will tell you that the Norton's one of the great art museums in the southeast United States. It's been really great to see this museum thrive under its current leadership over the last several years. It's really great to see that. I have got time for one more question. Last night, I went around asking people what question they would like me to ask Ken. It was remarkably consistent what they said. Do you know what that question is that they want me to ask you? I always get asked if the market is going up or down over the next three months? No, it's not that. Want to know? Sure. They want to know if you've ever considered throwing your hat into the ring for public office, even to run for the presidency. That's a bold question. They asked me to ask you. I studied economics and government at Harvard and have always had deep interest in public policy issues. I like to believe that at a future point in my life, I will be involved in public service. I'm very grateful for the opportunities this nation has afforded me. But over the next few years, I love my job. I love the colleagues I work with, and I'm very fortunate to have a number of ties to friends and acquaintances in Washington on both sides of the aisle. I think I've been able to have my voice heard on important issues. And I'd like to think that I've nudged the country in small ways in good directions. The president and I worked on Operation Warp Speed together in the first administration, the flights out of Wuhan. Mike Pompeo and I made most of that happen together. I found that this administration, and for that matter the Obama administration, were administrations that you could make meaningful things happen that benefited the American people. In the Biden administration, we were able to take an idea that we funded in Chicago, a small group of us funded, providing every child in Chicago in the pandemic with internet access. It's incomprehensible to believe that there were tens of thousands of kids in America that did not have access to the internet in Chicago. That concept was rolled into one of the national infrastructure bills in the Biden administration. I'd like to believe that I can continue to pursue philanthropic efforts and initiatives that help to ensure every child in America can still get on that on-ramp to the American dream. That's a very positive answer to the bold question. Thank you very much, Ken Griffin. Thank you so much. Thank you. That was great.

Episode 47: Pete Muller - Founder of PDT Partners

Generating Alpha Podcast(1.1K) ·35:19 ·250 views · 2026-02-03
Peter Muller
Peter Muller, founder of PDT Partners, established one of the most successful quantitative trading firms by cultivating a unique, collaborative culture and disciplined growth over sheer scale.
TL;DR Peter Muller, founder of PDT Partners, established one of the most successful quantitative trading firms by cultivating a unique, collaborative culture and disciplined growth over sheer scale. He balanced this highly successful finance career with a rich personal life...

TL;DR

Peter Muller, founder of PDT Partners, established one of the most successful quantitative trading firms by cultivating a unique, collaborative culture and disciplined growth over sheer scale. He balanced this highly successful finance career with a rich personal life dedicated to music and other diverse pursuits.

Pete Muller: A Quantitative Finance Maverick

  • Early Life & Unconventional Path:
      • Grew up in New Jersey to immigrant parents (Austrian engineer, Brazilian psychiatrist); drawn to math, puzzles, and music, but not finance.
      • Princeton graduate in mathematics, also a jazz musician.
      • Post-graduation, moved to California to compose music for rhythmic gymnasts, intentionally avoiding a traditional 9-5 corporate job.
  • Discovery of Quant Finance:
      • Found his way to BARRA (1985-1992), an early quantitative finance firm, where he discovered his passion for mathematical modeling in finance.
      • Mentored by Richard Bernold and Arjun Devicha.
  • Audacious Pitch & PDT's Founding:
      • In 1992, rejected an analyst role at Morgan Stanley and instead successfully pitched the radical idea of starting a proprietary, quantitative trading desk (Process Driven Trading - PDT) with Morgan Stanley's capital.
      • Conviction stemmed from his experience playing poker; Morgan Stanley gave him two years to prove the concept.

The Genesis and Evolution of PDT Partners

  • Early Success and Growth:
      • PDT launched in 1993, quickly becoming Morgan Stanley's most profitable group, reportedly averaging 20% annual returns.
      • The flagship fund has never experienced a down year since its inception in 1993.
      • Key early team members included Ken Nickerson (Princeton colleague), Kim Elcessor (MIT, MBA, EE), and Frank McQuio (tech).
  • Burnout and Sabbatical:
      • Despite extraordinary success, Muller experienced severe burnout around 1999, his mind overloaded with formulas, hindering his musical pursuits.
      • He took a multi-year phased sabbatical (initially planned for seven years), traveling (e.g., Bhutan), focusing on music (released albums), creating New York Times crossword puzzles, and competing in the World Series of Poker.
  • Return and Independence:
      • Returned to PDT when the firm faced challenges during his phased absence, eventually resuming leadership.
      • A critical decision was to significantly increase hiring of smart, collaborative people, shifting from a cautious, secretive culture. Partner Tar Shaw was instrumental in building the research group.
      • In 2012, post-Volcker Rule, PDT amicably spun out from Morgan Stanley as an independent firm, retaining all 80 employees and transferring its trading book seamlessly.

Culture as a Competitive Edge

  • Core Philosophy:
      • PDT's goal is to be the best place to work in quantitative finance, prioritizing quality over sheer scale.
      • The firm currently manages assets in the mid-teens of billions, with a focus on maximizing dollar profit per employee.
      • PDT charges "3 and 50," typically referring to a 3% management fee and 50% performance fee, and boasts a low 3.5% turnover rate.
  • Hiring and Values:
      • Recruits the smartest individuals, but insists they be collaborative and kind.
      • Maintains an exceptionally warm, nurturing, and trust-based work environment.
      • New hires often express surprise that the supportive culture, which seemed "too good to be true" during interviews, is genuinely maintained.
  • Rewarding Collaboration:
      • Compensation structures reward collaborative behavior, not just individual P&L.
      • A strict "no jerks" policy is enforced; high performers with poor interpersonal skills will not last.
      • The firm fosters a culture where competition is directed towards beating the market, not colleagues, and internal sharing of ideas is encouraged without fear of credit loss.
  • Supportive Environment:
      • The firm operates with a "more like a family" ethos than a "sports team," bending over backward to help struggling employees find suitable roles, even if it's considered "inefficient."

Navigating Risk and Scaling for Quality

  • The 2007 Quant Crisis:
      • In August 2007, PDT faced the "quant crisis," where aggressive leveraging by other firms led to widespread deleveraging and a liquidity crunch, even as the broader market remained flat.
      • PDT made the difficult decision to cut two-thirds of its trading book, but managed to make back the year's returns on the remaining one-third.
      • This painful experience provided crucial lessons in emergency liquidation and market preparedness.
  • Strategic Growth Limitations:
      • Muller consciously limits the firm's assets under management, believing that excessive size (e.g., $20-50 billion more in low-sharp strategies) can kill returns by increasing volatility, lowering Sharpe ratios, and raising trading costs.
      • The firm prioritizes consistent returns and aims for losing quarters/months to be extremely rare.
      • PDT has systematically "kicked out" external capital from its partners fund, making it entirely internal money, and plans to do the same for other offerings.
      • Avoiding massive external growth also prevents distraction from core research and client management, allowing focus on innovation.

Work-Life Harmony and Personal Philosophy

  • Diverse Pursuits:
      • Muller dedicates two-thirds of his time to personal interests in Santa Barbara, including surfing, performing and composing music (seven albums, NPR recognition), creating New York Times crossword puzzles, and competitive poker (made the final table at the World Series of Poker).
      • He believes this breadth of interests makes his life richer and more enjoyable, and that not being *consumed* by markets provides a competitive advantage by fostering broader perspective.
  • Ego and Mentorship:
      • His ego is not tied to being the sole "guy who could figure it all out." He finds immense satisfaction in mentoring others and seeing them surpass his own capabilities in finance.
  • Motivation Beyond Money:
      • Despite being able to retire, Muller remains involved due to the energy and challenge it provides, leveraging success in finance to fuel growth and passion in his musical endeavors where he is still "paying his dues."
  • Core Life Advice:
      • To a 16-year-old, he advises: "Follow the energy." Do what makes you feel alive, and people will be drawn to you; this approach makes difficult things energizing.

Notable Quotes

"The angels were looking down, and started making money." — Peter Muller, Founder and CEO of PDT Partners "The only way to get really good was to keep growing and to keep learning and to keep pushing forward." — Peter Muller, Founder and CEO of PDT Partners "Our utility is our goal is to be the best place to work in quantitative finance... they need to be collaborative and they need to be kind." — Peter Muller, Founder and CEO of PDT Partners "If you're doing something and it makes you feel alive, do it more. If you're doing something and you feel dead or you feel like, okay, I I should do this, I you don't have to do it." — Peter Muller, Founder and CEO of PDT Partners
This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Pete Mueller, founder and CEO of PDT Partners, one of the most successful quantitative hedge funds in history. Pete grew up in Wayne, New Jersey, as the son of immigrant parents. After graduating from Princeton with honors in mathematics, he moved to California to play music for rhythmic gymnasts. He eventually found his way to Barra doing quantitative work and then in 1992, convinced Morgan Stanley to let him start a proprietary trading desk called Process Driven Trading. For nearly two decades, PDT averaged 20% annual returns and was Morgan Stanley's most profitable group. After spinning out in 2012, the firm has continued that track record; the flagship fund has never had a down year since 1993. But in 1999, despite extraordinary success, Pete was miserable. His mind was so overloaded with formulas, he couldn't play music anymore. He took a seven-year sabbatical, trekking in Bhutan, busking in New York City subways, and today he spends two-thirds of his time in Santa Barbara. He has released seven studio albums, creates crossword puzzles for The New York Times, and has made the final table at the World Series of Poker. His firm charges 3 and 50 and has a 3.5% turnover rate. In our conversation, we talked about learning quant finance in the 1980s, the audacious pitch that created PDT, the 2007 quant crisis and institutional constraints, burnout and rebirth, how music and mathematics feed each other, building for quality over scale, and designing a life that doesn't require choosing between the things that matter most. If you enjoy this episode, please follow the podcast and rate it five stars on Spotify, subscribe on YouTube, and share it with anyone who you think might find it valuable. This is an incredible episode, and I really enjoyed recording with Pete. I hope you guys enjoy listening. Thank you. Thanks for joining me, Pete. I really appreciate it. Thanks for having me. I want to start where I always do, at the beginning. You grew up in Wayne, New Jersey, to immigrant parents. If I'm correct, you had an Austrian father who was an engineer and a Brazilian mother who was a psychiatrist. Tell me about your environment growing up and in what ways do you think that shaped you? Although I would say I haven't grown up yet, I was raised in New Jersey. My mom was a small-town doctor in Brazil; she was the only town doctor before coming to the States and so, psychiatry. I was in my head a lot as a kid. I was drawn to both math and puzzles and games and music. I was outdoors a lot, but definitely in my head a lot. I had no interest in finance or markets. You eventually went on to Princeton, studying math, but if I'm correct, also playing in a jazz band. After graduating, you moved to California and played music for rhythmic gymnasts instead of taking a corporate job. I'm not even sure what rhythmic gymnasts mean. Tell me about why you made that decision during your time in college. At Princeton, I was in an eating club and I had a friend who was trying out for the Olympics team. Back then, rhythmic gymnastics, with the hoop, ball, ribbons, it's a branch of gymnastics, and they have Olympic medals. There were two women that made the US team. She asked me to compose a piece for her. She ended up loving it and wanted me to accompany her for Olympic tryouts. I went and did that in Atlanta, played at the Olympic Sports Festival. She came fourth. If she had come first, which was a long shot, I would have been the Olympic pianist. But I met a coach in California who said, if you're ever in California, come play music for my team. I had a job lined up at a German computer firm in New York City. I had worked for them for a couple of summers and I just didn't want to go do a nine-to-five job, just go to an office. I thought, let's go to California and see how this plays out. I went there and realized it's very tough making a living as a rehearsal pianist for a rhythmic gymnastics team, as much as it was fun and I love California. So I went looking for a job. That's where I found a company called Barra, which did financial consulting, started by a Zen Buddhist guy named Barr Rosenberg, a wonderful human. It turns out finance is all math and modeling, and it just suited me. I found I had a couple of really great mentors, a fellow named Richard Bernold, a professor at Berkeley, and a guy named Arjun Devicha. Arjun ended up mainly managing tons of money at GMO, and they took a liking to me and taught me the business. I got really interested, so I started doing talks and writing papers. That's how I got into it. Incredible. I played piano for that gymnastics team for at least a year while I was working with Barra before giving it up. It's a fun overlap. If I'm correct, you were at Barra around the mid to late 80s, which was during the very early days of quantitative finance, from 1985 to 1992. During the earlier days of quantitative finance, what was it like being there while the industry was evolving? It was nascent. People didn't understand what we were doing. There was no such thing as... the technology was tough, the data was tough. But the modeling turned out to be very useful. A lot of what we did was really... we were hired by pension funds who were allocating money to managers, and the managers were being evaluated through our models. So the managers had to hire us for the models and realize that maybe they could help. It was a start, looking at it on a monthly basis. It was early, early days. After leaving Barra, if I'm correct, in '92, Morgan Stanley wanted to hire you as an analyst, as a quant analyst. You said no. Then I'm pretty sure you counter-offered and were like, "Let me start my own trading desk with Morgan Stanley's money." What gave you the conviction to even ask that of them, and why do you think they ultimately gave you that desk? It's a very reasonable question. I had learned how to play poker with a few friends and we got pretty good. I got a little bored at Barra. It was very comfortable, but I just didn't have the edge. The three of us that played poker actually pitched to Barra that we should start doing trading with our own money. But it was a really horrible pitch. If somebody had pitched me with that, I would have never said yes. It was very naive, but I realized it's kind of what I wanted to do. Then a number of other firms talked to me about doing it, but I never found somebody that was interesting. Then Morgan actually wanted me to write papers, travel around, talk to clients, kind of be the face of quant at Morgan Stanley. But it meant putting on a suit and tie and flying around the world, and talking to people about quant as opposed to doing it. I said, "I really want to try to do this." They said, "Can you... have you ever done it?" It's a very valid question. And I said, "Well, I got pretty good at poker. I learned how to do that. I think I can figure this out." They said, "Okay, here's the deal. We'll give you a couple of years. Try it and if it doesn't work out, you'll do what we want you to do." They thought, "You know what, he's going to find it too hard like most other people and then we'll have the guy that we want." I thought, "I'm going to find a way to do this." So, I took a chance. They guaranteed me enough to come to New York and give up my life in California, which was really nice, that I said, "Let me take a risk." Somehow, it took a while to get back to California, but I did. You took that risk. PDT started around 1993 and eventually spun out of Morgan Stanley in 2012 as an independent firm. I'm really interested, from a high level before we get to the more granular questions, in the evolution of the firm over those 20-some years. What milestones do you think were the biggest and shaped the investment that PDT is today? There are a lot of stories in there, and one day, maybe I'll write a book because the journey was fantastic. It took us a couple of years, and I was lucky enough to grab a colleague of mine from college, from Princeton, who had been an ultimate frisbee player with Ken Nickerson, to help build some of those models in the early days. I was lucky enough to find someone who had gone to MIT, who had gotten both an MBA and an advanced degree in electrical engineering, who had been at Morgan Stanley for a while, named Kim Elsesser, and a fellow named Frank McQuillan, who helped us out with our tech. Somehow creating that team, it just worked. After a few years, we actually really figured out how to make money, which was really great because the person that had taken the chance on me and brought me in had a political falling out at Morgan. It was just a question of, if we had not been making money by then, "What the heck is this? Get rid of it." But we were starting to make enough money, and they realized that we were smart, so they kept it going. Then all of a sudden, the growth was exponential until we were by far the most profitable group six years later. It was fantastic. But there was a time when I didn't think I would be there, that I actually went and interviewed with Long-Term Capital Management. The thought was, and they didn't know how to do equities at the time, we were pretty good at statistically doing equities. A lot of people didn't think it was possible. I talked to them really seriously; they had a dinner with all the partners. But for whatever reason, we didn't pull the mutual trigger and airlift the group out. I think if we had gotten a serious offer, we would have, because my position at Morgan Stanley was so unstable. But, we kind of dodged that bullet. The angels were looking down, and we started making money. I ended up working for a foe there named Vikram Pandit, who understood what we did and was like, "You know what, Pete, I get it. I trust you." Initially, he was like, "Wait a second, you guys have all these models and secrets and we have to understand them." And I'm like, "There are a lot of people in the group that understand them." It worked really well. The interesting thing is, around '99, 2000, Kim Elsesser, who was my partner in the group, came to me and said, "You know what? I'm done. I have enough money. I want to move to LA." All of a sudden, I realized I wasn't having that much fun anymore. I had made more money than I ever dreamed of; that was never the goal. I decided I was done too, went through a breakup, started songwriting, and I came up with a plan to leave. I thought I was going to basically be gone. I started another firm with a friend of mine called Chalk Street Capital that was allocating to other hedge fund managers. I thought, "I can't be that unusual. There's got to be a lot of other people that have started hedge funds that I can find and I can figure out." Early days back then, it turns out there are people, but it's really hard to do and really competitive. Chalk has done fine; it's still running. But I eventually, when I went back to PDT, Andrew came and joined us over there. The firm has continued to thrive and evolve and grow with new technologies. I think the strength we have, which is really important, is that for me, our utility, our goal, is to be the best place to work in quantitative finance. We want our people to be having a great time. We love hiring the smartest people we can find, but they need to be collaborative and they need to be kind. So, we have an amazingly warm place to work. People are really nurturing. They like to help people. Those values came from me, and that collaboration and that trust are the fundamental underpinning of our culture, and I think that's so important and that's a real competitive advantage we have. It's based on trust. They trust that if they devote themselves and work really hard that we're going to take care of them, that they're going to share in the success. We trust them that we can share secrets with people and they will stay there and keep them there. That's one of the big things that you have to worry about in trading, if people leave with your models. So, that trust is great, and people are not afraid internally to share ideas with other people because you're not worried about, "Oh, I might not get credit." So I think when it comes down to it, that culture is huge. When we interview people, they come there and they look around and they say, "Oh my God, this seems amazing, but it can't be." And then when we hire them, they join, they're like, "You know what? I thought it was too good to be true in my interview, but it actually really is like this." I would give my partner, Tarun Shah, a lot of credit. He is an amazing physicist. He could be running a research lab anywhere, and he was one of those people when I came back to the firm that I spotted, who was not afraid to hire people and to grow a research group. He has really made a huge difference in building up our research culture. I'm very grateful for that, and I think without it, we wouldn't be where we are. And how do you make sure that the culture is a culture of excellence, but not excellence in the way of people burning out, but rather excellence in the way of really being cordial, being nice, and then delivering great returns? The same way you incentivize behavior. You reward good behavior. So we pay people for being collaborative, not just for making money. If you're a jerk and you're really good, but you're a jerk, you're not going to last. We don't have jerks. And it's worth it. When you have a culture where people don't tolerate jerks, if somebody has lots of jerk tendencies but is basically good, that actually helps decrease those tendencies and their behavior becomes good. You want to kind of fit into a culture and do well. Part of it is, if there's anything we want, we want to make sure that the competitive drive and drive for excellence is still there too. We want people to be ruthless, just not ruthless versus their colleagues. It's ruthless versus building the models and beating the market, figuring that out. You reinforce that, you talk about that, and it mostly works. I think that's the main thing. It kind of shapes human culture, shapes behavior. I think the most important thing is, and you can't fake this, you have to genuinely care about people. So, if we have someone who's struggling, and this could be a junior person that we've hired that's just not getting it yet, or a senior person that kind of did some stuff but isn't in the right role, we bend over backwards to try to help that person find a new position to make it work. A lot of people talk about, "Okay, a company's not a family, it's more like a sports team." We kind of go a little bit more family that way. That's our way of looking at it. It might be inefficient, but I will tell you that it feels a lot better. It's a lot more fun. And for us, it's worked really well. I'm super interested in the aspect of size. You've said that you want to be the best quant on the planet in terms of the people working there and the culture, but specifically not in terms of assets, but in terms of quality of products, both in returns and in the people that you hire. You guys are at around mid-teens of billions of assets under management now and probably could be much bigger. When does size kill returns? We spend a lot of time thinking about that. It's not just returns. The bigger you get, the more volatile you get. Your Sharpe ratio goes down. It doesn't work as well. For the way when we started our partners fund, we told people, and we started with one and a half million, we told people, "Eventually we are going to replace this just with our money, and we will kick you out." That's why we kept the deal with our first investor that we promised we wouldn't kick out for seven years. We've continued to do that. So, our partners fund, we've kicked everybody out of. That's all our money and that's more than half of what we manage. Our other offerings, we will slowly do the same thing and kick people out. The way we look at it is, we want consistent returns. We want the prospect, even for our lower incentive fee fund, for a losing quarter to be very unusual. For our main fund, losing months should happen once or twice a year, and you can kind of figure out about what that target is. We think that that's reasonable. It's an interesting trade-off because things change. You want to make sure the models you're trading are still effective. So when you go through a drawdown period, you always ask the question, "Wait a second, did something change? Is it not working?" There are a lot of rigorous ways to figure that out. But if you have a low Sharpe ratio strategy that has losing years occasionally, and it gets a little harder if you have losing three years in a row, it gets very hard. You start doubting it. So that's how you decide how big you can get. The bigger you get, the more costs you pay to trade in the market and the lower realized alpha is. So there's a formula that trades it off. We could certainly, with our reputation, raise, I don't know, 20, 30, 40, 50 billion dollars more in low Sharpe strategies. We're not doing that. First of all, we'd have to add a lot, we'd have to expand our marketing department from one person, basically. And also, then you have to have a lot of client meetings and conversations about what's going on that's not actually helping you do things. Would it add some revenue diversification to the firm? Potentially. But it would also distract us from our main research mission. So that's the reason we've picked that. We have some competitors that have taken that path, and we have some competitors that have continued to get bigger and bigger. You also, we do have close to 300 people now. Keeping a culture intact when a firm is getting bigger is much harder. I just can't imagine what it would be like running a thousand or two thousand or three thousand-person firm. You don't know everybody, and that's okay. I don't know everybody now, but I know enough people that know people. So there's one degree of separation between me and everyone else, and if we were 1,200 people, that wouldn't be the case. So it seems to work. We think about maximizing dollar profit per employee. I want to take a step back for a second. I think this is a very interesting time in your firm's evolution. A few years after you came back, it was '07 and '08, and if I'm correct, if you didn't cut your positions, it would have been one of your best years ever. Tell me a bit about that period. I'm just super fascinated what it was like for you guys. There was a quant crisis in 2007, near the end of August. Quant, there's low-Sharpe quant and high-Sharpe quant, and the low-Sharpe quant had been doing really well, and people just got bigger and bigger than they thought. When I mentioned before that I made a decision that almost took down the firm, I thought we could make our low-Sharpe books much, much bigger. I went to Morgan Stanley internally, and this is after a period where there was no such thing as risk in the marketplace. So you have leaders whose risk aversion is far lower, that's very natural. I said, "You know what, we've got all these strategies, we can take lots of outside capital, and we should because this isn't something we should do internally." The message I got back was, "No, no, you don't understand, we're really big, we can handle massive drawdowns." I said, "No, you don't understand," but I lost that battle. But I said, "Okay, fine." So we took the lower Sharpe ratio stuff, made it much bigger, and as a result, when we went into that crazy week, what had happened was people started delevering those books, and stuff started going down. The people that were taking too much leverage, the weak hands, needed to start puking, and there just wasn't enough liquidity. So you had a... even though the market was flat that week, it was crazy. We had to make some decisions. We actually ended up cutting two-thirds of our book, which was extreme. We almost went down to zero, but we made back the year on the one-third of our book that we kept. We were ready to unload it that Friday, but it finally... my poker sense felt like it was over. Fortunately, I was right. So we held off for a little bit, but it was a scary time. That was 2007, and I still remember it. There was not a lot of sleep because it was a contagion, it went all around, and it was just a reminder. Plus, we just never thought we would have to emergency liquidate, so we didn't know how to do it. We were trading sloppily. I'm sure we weren't the only ones. All of a sudden, people were like, "Wait a second. Everybody's trying to get out of the way." And so we went out of the way. But it worked out okay. That was the hardest thing, the parallel decision to opening it up and hiring lots of people. The great thing that happened during that week was you kind of saw how dedicated and devoted people were, how much people loved the firm, wanted to stay and do things. Then when 2008 happened, when Morgan Stanley almost went bust, being the weekend that Morgan Stanley almost went bust, when Mack was in Tokyo begging Mitsubishi for the bailout, that's the weekend my son was born. I had moved to California where I live now, and I was there, and I was ready to fly back right away because it was absolutely nuts. Fortunately, that didn't happen, but we were fine. We were making tons of money. We learned so much from that liquidation that when crazy events happen in the marketplace now, we're much better prepared. It seems like a useful learning experience, although a painful one. Indeed. When I write that book one day, the Wall Street Journal had... I made the front page of the Wall Street Journal, which nobody knew we existed, but because it was the end of August and Morgan reported the quarterly earnings right at the end of August, they had to talk about us and do it. So the Wall Street Journal published a story that said, "Quant star battered. No time for music now." Funny. Yeah. And if I'm correct at the firm, you organize researchers into teams by asset class and time horizon. Some work on problems for very short amounts of time, like a couple of weeks. Some work for years at a time. How do you decide what's worth years versus weeks and what gets killed quickly? We don't actually organize them that way. We've changed how we approach it. There's just a lot of judgment that goes into it. I won't go into details in terms of how we think about things, but I think if somebody came in and saw it, they would feel like it was common sense. It's, what's your level of conviction on something and what's your credibility? How good have you been at figuring things out in the past? But typically, if somebody is smart and we trust them and they say, "I want to figure this out. I'm not going to stop till I do," we're going to let them do it. The downside is limited. So that's how we play. I want to touch on a couple of questions about you because you have a very unique background in the sense that you spend two-thirds of your time, if I'm correct, out there in California, most of it surfing. You perform music. You create New York Times crossword puzzles, and I think you made it to the final table of the World Series of Poker event. You've created seven albums as well. Most successful investors that I've interviewed are completely consumed by markets. Do you think not being consumed by markets is a competitive advantage? I think it makes my life much more fun. I think the thing is, I'm pretty good at what I do. I'm pretty smart, and when I obsess over something, I get really good at it. But what I love is being able to have people learn what I do and then do it better. I have a bunch of crosswords that are in the Washington Post, but now I've mentored some people that create them. I can't do that with music. I've got to create the music and do it myself. But in PDT, there are quite a number of people who are, I would argue, smarter and better than I am in terms of building models and making this work. Maybe I have a little more experience and a little more wisdom, but my ego is not attached to being the guy who could figure it all out. And I think that makes for a more interesting life. I love to learn. I love to be challenged. And I love to watch other people learn as well. That's what motivates me. At this point in your career, you could retire tomorrow and just make music. What keeps you going? That's been true forever. But the same reason... well, all those things. Part of it is, when I play music, and I do travel around the country, and we do get shout-outs. This last record, the seventh record, NPR gave us one of the top 10 Americana albums for the week, which was really cool, unexpected. I, when I can go around and play for 90 people in a pin-drop quiet room, either solo or with my band, that's a real turn-on for me. It's energizing and it's great, but I'm a nobody in the music field. It's, and I'm struggling, and a lot of times I'm the opening act. I'm not even changing in the green room, I'm changing in the bathroom. It's cool, and I'm devoted to it, and I've got to pay my dues, and that's cool. It's not like when I travel, it's uncomfortable. It's fine. But when I'm playing, I'm playing. When I walk into the hedge fund world, people know what I did, know what I'm capable of. I feel like it's a nice boost. I'm like, "Oh, good. I can be really excellent at what I devote myself to." And so, I can use that energy and help myself grow in the other area, so the combination actually works great for me. Incredible. And I have one final question I ask every single one of my guests at the end of every episode. I'm 16 right now. If you were to give one piece of advice to a 16-year-old today, and it can be life advice, it can be career advice. Jeff Yass gave me romantic advice. What would it be? I want to know what Jeff told you. That is great advice. The first thing I'm going to tell you is, holy, you're 16. I knew that going into this, but you're incredibly impressive for 16. You're well-spoken, you're thoughtful. You're way ahead of the game, which is fantastic, and you clearly have a great, bright future ahead of you. The piece of advice that I would give, and I would give my kids this, is there is a sure signal that you get when you are operating in the world that you're doing what you're supposed to be doing, and that is the energy. Follow the energy. If you're doing something and it makes you feel alive, do it more. If you're doing something and you feel dead or you feel like, "Okay, I should do this," you don't have to do it. You can make your own rules, but if you pay attention to energy, people will be drawn to you and you'll be able to figure out whatever you need to figure out. It takes a while. Building the Future of Industrial AI with NVIDIA

Dassault Systèmes(88.0K) ·41:40 · 2026-02-03
Pascal Daloz Jensen Huang
Dassault Systèmes and NVIDIA are forging their deepest partnership yet, combining Dassault Systèmes' virtual twin technology with NVIDIA's accelerated computing and AI to power a new "generative economy" and "knowledge factories," enabling real-time,...
TL;DR Dassault Systèmes and NVIDIA are forging their deepest partnership yet, combining Dassault Systèmes' virtual twin technology with NVIDIA's accelerated computing and AI to power a new "generative economy" and "knowledge factories," enabling real-time, AI-driven design,...

TL;DR

Dassault Systèmes and NVIDIA are forging their deepest partnership yet, combining Dassault Systèmes' virtual twin technology with NVIDIA's accelerated computing and AI to power a new "generative economy" and "knowledge factories," enabling real-time, AI-driven design, simulation, and operation across all industries.

A New Era of Industrial AI

  • Shifting Paradigms: The 21st century's industry is moving from producing physical objects to generating knowledge and know-how, which then creates objects, representing the true source of value.
  • Knowledge Factories: Dassault Systèmes' virtual twins and 3D universes are evolving into "knowledge factories" where insights are enriched, know-how is scaled, and results are trusted.
  • Real World AI: This transformation is supercharged by a "real world AI" grounded in industry, engineering, and science, moving beyond generic AI and Large Language Models (LLMs) to create "world models" capable of designing complex systems like aircraft or discovering cancer therapies.
  • Accelerated Computing: The fusion of virtual twin power with accelerated computing is critical to realizing this vision.
  • AI as Infrastructure: Jensen Huang posits that AI is becoming foundational infrastructure, akin to water, electricity, or the internet, impacting every industry and country globally.

Deepening a Quarter-Century Partnership

  • Historical Roots: The collaboration between Dassault Systèmes and NVIDIA spans over a quarter-century, originating during the personal computing revolution with technologies like OpenGL and CGFX (precursor to CUDA).
  • Largest Collaboration: This new phase marks the largest partnership between the two companies, aiming to reinvent the computing platform for an AI-driven era.
  • Integrated Technologies: Dassault Systèmes will integrate NVIDIA CUDA-X acceleration libraries, NVIDIA AI for physical and agentic AI, and NVIDIA Omniverse (NVIDIA's digital twin technology) into its platforms.
  • Unprecedented Scale: This integration promises 100x to 1,000,000x greater scale and real-time capabilities for engineers and designers, enabling processes that were previously impossible.

Generative Power: From Life Sciences to Factories

  • Generative Economy: The partners envision a "generative economy" where virtual twins are infused with accelerated AI to transform design and manufacturing.
  • Life Sciences & World Models:
      • Understanding Life's Language: NVIDIA AI integrates with Dassault Systèmes' Biovia to understand the "language of life" – DNA, proteins, cells – and their interactions.
      • Generative Biology: This understanding enables the generation of new proteins for drugs, novel chemicals, and advanced materials (e.g., stronger, lighter, heat-resistant).
      • Case Study: Bell Group: The Bell Group uses biological world models within virtual twins to automatically generate proteins, accelerating healthier food innovation and ensuring certified decisions.
  • Generative Engineering & PhysicsNemo:
      • Augmenting Simulation: The fusion of simulation and AI allows virtual twins to explore infinite design possibilities and predict outcomes 10,000 times faster using "surrogate and emulation models."
      • Physics-Aware AI: NVIDIA's PhysicsNemo is a physics-aware AI model system, grounded in physical laws but capable of rapid prediction, transforming engineering design.
      • Case Study: Lucid Motors: Lucid embeds complex behaviors like crash dynamics and aerodynamics early in vehicle development, enabling engineers to design behavior and certify performance concurrently.
  • Software-Defined Factories:
      • Virtual Operations: Factories are becoming hybrid virtual and physical assets, simulated and operated entirely within virtual twins.
      • Complex Systems: These virtual factories manage millions of objects, optimize manufacturing lines, orchestrate robot AIs, and ensure safety, all within a software-defined environment.
      • Case Study: Omron: Omron uses virtual twins to engineer "software-defined factories," designing autonomous capabilities from day one, leading to more flexible, resilient, and adaptive production systems.

The Future Engineer with AI Companions

  • AI Factories Infrastructure: The world is undergoing the largest industrial infrastructure buildout in history, with "AI factories" (composed of chip, computer, and AI factories) costing tens of billions of dollars per gigawatt.
  • Model-Based Design for AI Factories: NVIDIA uses Dassault Systèmes' Model-Based Systems Engineering (MBSE) to design, plan, and simulate these complex AI factories in virtual twins before construction, saving significant time and money.
  • Virtual Companions: AI is enabling a new workplace revolution where engineers will work with "virtual companions." These companions can translate unstructured information (like sketches) into precise 3D parametric models automatically.
  • Augmenting Human Creativity: Rather than replacing engineers, AI companions will serve as a team, trained by the designer, to explore and optimize designs, freeing human engineers for strategic, creative, and fine-tuning tasks.
  • Knowledge Protection: These AI companions will codify individual designers' preferences, skills, habits, and domain expertise, acting as private, personalized extensions of their knowledge.
  • Compliance by Design: Virtual companions can automatically ingest requirements and continuously verify conformity (e.g., for aircraft certification with 10,000+ requirements), shifting compliance upstream to become a competitive advantage rather than a cost.
  • World Models for Compliance: Moving from language models to world models means integrating the laws of physics, causality, manufacturability, and societal values directly into the design process, ensuring constant compliance.

Notable Quotes

"In this new century, industry is producing knowledge and knowhow and the knowledge and knowhow are generating the objects. This is where the true value lies." — Pascal Daloz, CEO, Dassault Systèmes "AI is foundational to every single industry, it is going to become an infrastructure. Just as water was infrastructure, electricity is infrastructure, internet was infrastructure, now artificial intelligence will be infrastructure." — Jensen Huang, Founder and CEO, NVIDIA "We can't compute infinity but we can imagine infinity which is the reason why these surrogate and emulation models the fusion of simulation and artificial intelligence is so powerful." — Jensen Huang, Founder and CEO, NVIDIA "The number of users of the soul tools is going to go from biological to biological as well as AI based. And so the number of tool use will ex will explode." — Jensen Huang, Founder and CEO, NVIDIA
Hey, thank you so much for the warm welcome and welcome back. Today is a special day, but before we dive in, let me do a quick summary of what I discussed with you yesterday. In this new century, industry is producing knowledge and knowhow, and that knowledge and knowhow are generating the objects. This is where the true value lies. This is where the power is, and that's why we bring together the virtual twins and the 3D universes. 3D universes are not applications; they are knowledge factories—factories where knowledge is enriched, knowhow is scaled, and the results are trusted. The factories where the robots are orchestrated to build the cars are software-defined, and the cars themselves are software-defined. So everything will be software-defined. Before we go further, if you look around the crowds, it's kind of a ruckus crowd. At Dassault Systèmes, we work with 45 million people around the world, 400,000 customers, more than 15 million engineers and researchers. So I think you have here probably one of the, if not the largest, engineering community in the world. They do more than half of the products surrounding us every day: robots, drones, planes, cars, medical devices, drugs, homes, cities, factories. This is an amazing community, don't you think so? I know they think so. Absolutely. We're all engineers. You are an engineer. Yes, I'm still an engineer. So you belong to this community. I am. If I was to start all over again, I'd choose to use SolidWorks. That's why the world model matters. Because for these communities, success is not about automation; they don't want to automate the past. They want to invent the future. This is the reason why we are announcing this new chapter in our partnership, because together we are bringing the virtual twin factory with the AI factory. This opportunity is really enormous for you guys and for us. To prove the power of this, we have some concrete examples. Jensen and I have selected some use cases we want to share with you. Let's start with research and engineering first. Yes. I remember that almost everything we do together starts with the computing platform. When PCs went into the cloud, Dassault Systèmes reinvented itself again. Now, as we're extending from cloud to AI, we're reinventing again. Today, we're announcing a massive partnership. This is the largest collaboration our two companies have ever had in over a quarter century. Dassault Systèmes is going to integrate NVIDIA CUDA-X acceleration libraries, NVIDIA AI for physical AI and for agentic AI, and NVIDIA Omniverse, our version of digital twin technologies. All of these libraries represent our body of work over a quarter of a century. Now we're going to fuse these technologies into Dassault Systèmes so that all of you will have the benefit of accelerated computing and artificial intelligence, and be able to work at a scale that's a hundred times, a thousand times, and very soon a million times greater than what you were able to do before. What used to be pre-rendered or offline simulations will now be literally the virtual twin vision that you've always had. Everything will be done literally in real time. We'll design products and simulate them in a wind tunnel in real time. We'll interconnect these robots and let them operate in a factory in real time, and they'll be building your products literally in real time. All of this is going to be happening in the next five to ten years; this is going to be extraordinary. Speaking about this, let's start with life. I think life is the most complex system ever created. When you think about it, how much knowledge is encoded in the living world? With our virtual twins, we are learning from life. We are also understanding it in order to replicate and to scale it. This is possible. This is NVIDIA AI integrating with BIOVIA. Yes, BIOVIA, right? We will come back to this. But this is possible because we have this foundation. We call it the world model. The world model, where it's grounded in biology, physics, and material sciences. So the key question I have for you is, what does it take to compute a world model for life? Well, the most important thing, the first thing that we have to do, is understand the language of life. Of course, in the world of physical design, the design started with your imagination, and you represented that physical object using structured information, geometries, and textures that were designed by you. However, life is different. Life existed before us. So we have to go learn the language of DNA, learn the language of proteins, and learn the language of cells, and understand how they interact and their properties. That first stage of learning the meaning of life is what we are in the process of tackling. The second part, of course, is generative. Once you can learn something, learn the meaning of something, we can translate it between languages. We can translate between human language and the language of biology, and interpret it so that we can understand it in human language. Beyond that, you can now translate and generate new proteins that could be used for a drug, or generate new chemicals that could be used for a drug. Then, of course, generate new materials that could be stronger, be more heat resistant, lighter, easier to manufacture, last longer. All of those properties are now within our grasp, and this is one of the reasons why this is likely going to be one of the most impactful areas of engineering in the next decade. Exactly. And it is already happening. In fact, we have a case: the Bel Group. You know them, they make the famous Babybel. Their mission is very simple: they want to produce healthier foods for millions of consumers. But at the same time, they want to consume less water, and they want to progressively change or at least complement dairy protein with non-dairy proteins. That's the reason why they are inventing what we call food science. Before, there were hundreds of physical tests for one single product. Now they generate automatically – and this is what you can see on the screen – they generate automatically the protein from the virtual twins, because it's powered by the biological world model. The result is not only faster innovations, but also certified decisions, because you cannot play when you have the life of people in your hands. That's what we do. Now let's move to something else you started to speak about. You have seen on screen this is changing the daily life of engineers. Now you define the specs, you run your simulations, and automatically the generative experience is producing and exploring the space of possibilities and finding the optimum solutions for you. Actually, the virtual twin is exploring an infinite of possibilities. So the question I have for you is, could we compute infinity? We can't compute infinity, but we can imagine infinity, which is the reason why these surrogate and emulation models, the fusion of simulation and artificial intelligence, are so powerful. Eight years ago, I introduced the idea to scientific computing and simulations: the idea that in the future, not only will we use principled simulations where the equations, the laws of physics, are well understood and well represented (however, the simulation time takes way too long), why don't we augment that with generative methods of predicting the future using artificial intelligence? It's a little bit like the analogy I would give: it's a little bit like are dogs able to catch a ball out of the air? Yet they're not doing physics simulations of balls bouncing or the elastic nature of the ball. They're just literally watching us and predicting where it's going to go, and they snatch it out of the air. The idea that an AI could learn how to predict physics and learn how to predict very accurately how materials would crumble, what happens to a crash—those capabilities are within grasp. We have a technology called Physics-Nemo. Physics-Nemo is essentially a physics-aware AI model simulation system and AI framework that allows us to create these AI models that are either trained by principled simulators or work alongside principled simulators. So it's grounded in the laws of physics but able to predict 10,000 times faster. Now if everything is already running in real time, then you can predict it at 10,000 times greater scale. That's just where we are right now. Imagine where we're going to be in the future. The idea of simulation and emulation coming together to help you design is going to be truly revolutionary. Again, this is exactly what you see here with a customer called Lucid. We know them; they are one of the most innovative car companies in the world. What do they do? They embed the crash behavior, the aerodynamics, and the vehicle performance upstream, early in the vehicle program's developments. Well, the way that people used to think about designing products is they design the product and they build the factory.
