So, it's very likely in the future—well, it will be; in fact, it is now—that every single factory is designing CAD. That's obvious, but it will be simulated and operated completely inside a virtual twin. Operating a factory of these gigantic scales inside a virtual twin is extraordinarily complex. A factory is not just one object; it's millions of objects. We want to also simulate or emulate how these factories will operate in the real world so that we could arrange the manufacturing lines properly, arrange them in the right sequence, space them properly, organize the robots within them, run the robot AIs so that these AI robots could be operating inside the factory, manipulating things, assembling things, moving things, keeping things safe. All of this is going to happen inside a virtual twin. So, the products that Dassault Systèmes is going to help people build and design are going to become gigantic in the future. These are going to be systems of objects, systems of AI, systems of robots all coming together into a giant factory. These are fast computers, is what I'm saying. And again, this is exactly what we do with Omron. You have seen on the screen they don't use the virtual twin only to visualize the factories. In fact, they do much more. They engineer what we call a software-defined factory. That's right. And where the difference is coming from is that they are designing the autonomous part day one. It's not something they come to when the production system is already up and running and then try to infuse the autonomous part into it. As a result, those factories become much more flexible, resilient, and adaptive. But there is another kind of factory, and you talk a lot about this: the AI factories. They are building everywhere; they are extremely complex. What does it take to make them or to build them and to make them a reality? Well, we're going through what clearly is a new industrial revolution, a fundamental technology that impacts the productivity of many industries. That's why it's an industrial revolution. Just as energy did that, just as mechanical energy and power did that, just as electricity and, of course, the internet did all that, we're now seeing artificial intelligence doing that. In order to make this possible, we need to industrialize and really scale three different giant industries. Right now, as we're speaking, these three entirely different industries are all growing incredibly fast so that we could create the infrastructure for intelligence and manufacturing the AIs. While these factories are incredibly complex, a gigawatt AI factory is about $50 billion. And now we're building tens of gigawatts around the world. It's an enormous infrastructure buildout, the largest industrial infrastructure buildout in human history. The amount of technology that comes together inside these factories is extraordinary. We want to make sure that they work the first time. The way we're doing it, we're using MBSE – Dassault Systèmes product, Model-Based Systems Engineering. I wish you would have been something VIA. Not yet. Okay, alright, so MBVIA, that's it, Model-Based VIA. So what goes into these systems are giant data centers with lots of supercomputers. The amount of energy necessary, of course, is a gigawatt – the largest factories ever. And it costs so much money. So we design, we plan, we simulate everything in MBSE before we build it. Our expectation is, and we even run the network and run the supercomputers inside the virtual twin before we even break ground. That allows us to save tons of time and tons of money. And over time, of course, this data center has an AI that keeps it optimal. It's AIs that modulate the performance, modulate the power, modulate the temperature, modulate cooling, and in doing so, if you want to do so successfully, you need artificial intelligence. That operating loop – we're going to have these virtual twins of these AI factories running forever, training and updating our models. So again, this is proving that the virtual twin is not only about 3D. As you say, it's about revealing the architecture, revealing the system underneath, and obviously revealing the knowledge at scale. And it looks real, and it's real. It looks real, right? It looks real. It operates really. It integrates all industrial bill of material. The bill of material comes in from manufacturers and suppliers. We integrate everything physically. Now I'm counting on you to sell it to your ecosystem, right? Absolutely. Well, you're, we're the first customer. You are? You're the first customer. Now the next topic I want to discuss with you is really how do we put the knowledge to work. This is exactly what our virtual companions do. We made a live demo yesterday just to showcase what is coming, and engineers spend too much time looking for information or doing something else. This is not engineering. So now, look at it. In a few seconds, we started from a sketch; we produced automatically, we moved from 2D to 3D. It's a full parametric model simulation already. I think this is revolutionizing everything people are doing, and it's changing the workflows completely. Well, before I answer that, one of the really revolutionary things that AI has made possible for us is the ability to go from an interchange between structured information and unstructured information. Unstructured information is essentially a photograph, or it could be a recording, a video. We want to take that unstructured information and now represent it in a structured way. We need artificial intelligence to go from 2D to 3D, image to 3D. Once you have it in 3D, that information is precise. It's controllable. It's interchangeable. We can enhance it. We can improve it. These agents are going to be companions of ours because we're going to essentially be their manager, their architect, the creator. Whereas most people think that the number of designers, therefore, will be less than in the past, and the number of software tools you will use will be less than in the past, it's exactly the opposite. It is very likely, and I'm certain this is going to happen, that every designer, every SolidWorks designer, every designer in the future, will have a team of companions. You've trained these companions, and you've taught them different skills, and you've helped them coordinate and work with each other and work with you, and they're all going to be using Dassault Systèmes tools. So the number of users of Dassault Systèmes tools is going to go from biological to biological, as well as AI-based. The number of tool uses will explode. This, of course, is going to be great for the software industry. It's going to be great for all the designers because you have so many companions to help you do things. What would be really fantastic is that you're working with your companions, and then it's time for a cocktail, because it's solidly 4:30 somewhere, and so you kick off your team to go explore all these different areas. "I want you to explore this, I want you to explore that, I want you to optimize for these areas and give me three designs. I want to optimize for these years. Give me 10 designs." When you come back, you have all those choices, and then you can go in and fine-tune it specifically yourself because you have the structured data, the 3D data. So I think the opportunities to reinvent how you think about design and creativity are going to be completely revolutionary for everybody. That's also something we can showcase. For instance, take NIAR. NIAR is a national institute for the aviation industry. They are based in Wichita and they focus on research, testing, but also certifying. We know that certifying an airplane is a nightmare. It takes between three to five years; more than 10,000 requirements need to be fulfilled. Now, with virtual companions, what could we do? The regulations could be automatically ingested without having to read millions of pages, and conformity is constantly verified. This means we are moving to compliance by design, and it's not anymore a cost; it could become a competitive edge. So the question I have for you is, how does it take to move from a language model to a world model? Well, the language model has to obviously understand syntax and vocabulary and the structure of language, and it has taste. What's a better way to compose a paragraph? And it has guardrails – what are the things it should talk about and things it should avoid talking about? In the world model, instead of taste and values, it has to obey the laws of physics. It has to understand causality: if you tip over a domino, all the dominoes that are connected to it or nearby it will be tipped over. It has to understand what comes after and what comes before. It understands inertia and friction, understands gravity, of course. It understands contact – all the things that you understand as you're designing things. We have to teach the AI that sensibility that's not necessarily captured in language, and all the language in the world won't capture that. So we have to use laws of physics and simulation and a whole bunch of examples to teach it the laws of physics. Then, of course, one of the things you mentioned is that design for manufacturability today is integrated into the design process, instead of you coming up with the design and then another team decides whether it's manufacturable. Design for manufacturability is really upstreamed, shifted left. We want to shift left basically everything. One of the things that are very hard, as you mentioned, is compliance, because that's where machines meet society and humans. So that language model, where human values could be now integrated into or shifted left into the design process, means you're constantly in compliance. You're constantly obeying the laws of physics because of the world model. You're constantly designing for manufacturability. You're constantly designing to use components that are approved with vendor approval lists and whatever it is, so that by the time you're done with the design, it's good to go. Now, I hope you have a better idea about what this partnership is about. Whatever the size of the company, whatever the industry you are in, I think we help you to certify your decisions. We help you to eliminate bad choices before they become expensive mistakes. More importantly, we need to protect your knowledge as well. So how do you think we can protect the knowledge of all the millions of people using our software? Well, first of all, before I go there, I think that our partnership today and what we're announcing is genuinely extraordinary. The type of things that you will be building in the future are simply impossible without accelerated computing. It's impossible without real-time simulation. It's impossible with artificial intelligence. Instead of thinking about what could be more productive – yes, productivity is going to be enhanced, and you'll be a lot more productive, and you'll be able to do things more quickly than in the past, just as PCs did that for us, just as the cloud did that for us, just as the internet has done that for us – every technology revolution has made us more productive. However, this time, the type of things that you'll be able to do, when you think about the scale, a hundred times, a thousand times, a million times, these are going to be things that are just simply impossible to do before. When people think about AI, they have humans in the loop, and that's important. But remember, you also now have your companion, your AI, in the loop. That AI is going to remember how you like to do your preferences. That AI, therefore, will codify your skills, codify your preferences, codify your habits, codify the domain expertise that you have. That is your companion. That companion sits with you. It's not going to be in the cloud, not going to be public, because it captures your expertise. If you look at my inbox, in a lot of ways, that's captured 33 years of my knowledge, of my expertise. It's not available for everybody. Netflix Co-CEO Ted Sarandos testifies before Senate on Netflix-Warner Bros. deal — 2/3/2026

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·124:40 ·25.0K views · 2026-02-03
Ted Sarandos Bruce Campbell
The Senate Antitrust Subcommittee met to examine Netflix's proposed acquisition of Warner Bros.
TL;DR: The Senate Antitrust Subcommittee convened to scrutinize Netflix's proposed $83 billion acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery, with senators raising significant concerns about market concentration, potential harm to consumers and creative labor, and ideological content...

Executive Briefing: Senate Hearing on Netflix-Warner Bros. Discovery Merger

TL;DR: The Senate Antitrust Subcommittee convened to scrutinize Netflix's proposed $83 billion acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery, with senators raising significant concerns about market concentration, potential harm to consumers and creative labor, and ideological content bias, while company executives defended the deal as pro-consumer, pro-growth, and beneficial for the American entertainment industry.

1. Overview of Proposed Merger & Antitrust Scrutiny

The Senate Antitrust Subcommittee met to examine Netflix's proposed acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery (WBD) for $83 billion, a transaction raising serious antitrust concerns.

  • Transaction Details:
      • Netflix, the world's largest streaming platform (325M+ subscribers, ~1 billion viewers), proposes acquiring WBD's film and television production studios, streaming services (HBO, HBO Max, DC Studios), and extensive content library (e.g., Harry Potter, Game of Thrones, The Wire).
      • The deal would grant Netflix control over WBD's existing catalog and intellectual property for future productions.
  • Context of Hearing:
      • Chairman Mike Lee (R-UT): Stated the transaction is "extraordinary in both scale and in potential consequence," warranting scrutiny due to concerns about competition and its shaping of future entertainment access.
      • Ranking Member Cory Booker (D-NJ): Highlighted a "crisis in our nation right now with a level of corporate concentration like has not been seen for generations," expressing bipartisan concern and noting the absence of Paramount SkyDance, which made a hostile $108 billion bid for WBD.
      • Senator Chuck Grassley (R-IA): Emphasized Congress's oversight role to ensure the DOJ and FTC apply antitrust laws neutrally, cautioning against undermining competition and consumer choice.

2. Senator Concerns: Antitrust, Labor, and Market Impact

Senators raised multiple concerns regarding the merger's potential impact on various aspects of the entertainment industry and wider economy.

  • Horizontal Antitrust Concerns:
      • Direct Competition: Netflix and HBO Max directly compete for subscribers and premium content; the merger eliminates this head-to-head competition.
      • Content Development: Both companies develop critically acclaimed films and series, an area of direct competition.
      • Market Definition: If the market is defined as subscription video on demand (SVOD), the combined firm could exceed a 30% market share, potentially triggering a presumption of illegality.
  • Vertical Antitrust Concerns:
      • Foreclosure Risk: Combining Netflix's dominant distribution platform with WBD's premier content creates a risk of vertical foreclosure. The merged entity could:
          • Withhold marquee titles from rivals.
          • Raise licensing fees for WBD content to competitors.
          • Favor its own content through Netflix's proprietary recommendation algorithms.
      • Data Ownership: Netflix's "unmatched insight into viewer behavior" combined with WBD content could further entrench dominance and raise barriers to entry for new competitors.
  • Impact on Creative Talent (Monopsony Power):
      • Reduced Competition for Labor: Consolidating two major employers (Netflix and WBD) could weaken competition for creative talent (writers, directors, actors, production professionals).
      • Suppression of Wages/Opportunities: Concerns about potential "monopsony power" to suppress wages or limit professional opportunities, citing the DOJ's successful blocking of the Penguin Random House-Simon & Schuster merger.
      • Senator Peter Welch (D-VT): Expressed concern that fewer outlets for creators would "suppress creative content" and "pay me as a creator less."
      • Senator Josh Hawley (R-MO): Presented data suggesting a "massive shift in production overseas" and sought commitments on domestic production and union labor, questioning Netflix's commitment to "fairly compensating your workers in the industry with residual payments."
  • Impact on Theatrical Exhibition:
      • Diverting Releases: WBD is a central force in theatrical distribution, while Netflix has favored streaming. The merger could increase incentives to divert major releases away from theaters to streaming-only on Netflix, reducing consumer choice and threatening movie theaters.
  • Consumer Costs and Choice:
      • Senator Amy Klobuchar (D-MN): Expressed concern that combining the biggest players could lead to further price increases, noting Netflix raised prices last year while adding subscribers.
      • Senator Ashley Moody (R-FL): Highlighted that Netflix costs have doubled in the last 10 years, outpacing inflation.
  • Broader Societal & Political Concerns:
      • Artistic Freedom & Diversity: Ranking Member Cory Booker: Emphasized the importance of art and diverse voices, worrying that consolidation could lead to "less movies and less original story making," and that the trends of consolidation are "disastrous."
      • Content Bias & Political Influence:
          • Senator Josh Hawley: Questioned Netflix's content for children, alleging it "promotes a transgender ideology agenda," and stated "Netflix has no political agenda of any kind" was "inaccurate" given his analysis. He said, "Why does half of your children's program feature this highly controversial, highly sexualized material?" (Netflix Co-CEO Ted Sarandos denied the percentage and the underlying premise).
          • Senator Rick Scott (R-FL): Questioned Netflix's political neutrality, citing employee donations to Democrats (~99%) and past company statements on social justice issues. He accused Netflix of "overtly sexualizing children" and engaging in "very woke programming overwhelmingly."
          • Senator Ted Cruz (R-TX): Expressed concern about "ideological aspects," suggesting the entertainment world has "only one acceptable political view" and could become a "propaganda outlet." He questioned the company's political bias, citing founder donations and Susan Rice's board membership.
      • Political Interference in Merger Review:
          • Ranking Member Cory Booker: Raised concerns about President Trump's reported meeting with Ted Sarandos, his subsequent stock purchases in Netflix and WBD, and allegations of political favoritism, particularly in relation to the rejected Paramount SkyDance bid and its alleged promises regarding CNN. He questioned whether it's appropriate for the President to be involved in a Justice Department merger review or to trade stock in companies undergoing such reviews.

3. Netflix & Warner Bros. Discovery Justification & Benefits

Ted Sarandos (Netflix Co-CEO) and Bruce Campbell (WBD Chief Revenue & Strategy Officer) presented arguments in favor of the merger.

  • Complementary Assets, Not Redundant:
      • Ted Sarandos: Stated WBD assets are "very different" from Netflix's, including a "century of iconic IP," a "world-class theatrical distribution business," and studios that license content to other networks. Netflix currently lacks these.
      • Bruce Campbell: Emphasized Netflix's lack of existing film studio and distribution infrastructure, suggesting the acquisition would build new capabilities rather than duplicate.
      • Netflix aims to "operate those businesses largely as they are today," preserving one of Hollywood's five major studios.
  • Consumer Benefits:
      • More Content, Better Value: Netflix and HBO Max streaming services are "complimentary," with 80% of HBO Max subscribers already subscribing to Netflix. The combined entity could offer more content for less money, potentially through bundled discounts.
      • Improved User Experience: WBD content could be integrated into Netflix's "best technology platform available to consumers."
      • Affordability: Ted Sarandos: Highlighted Netflix's affordability, claiming consumers spend "35 cents an hour to watch Netflix," a third of some competitors, due to the high volume of content for the subscription price.
  • Commitment to Theaters:
      • Ted Sarandos: Committed to supporting theaters by releasing Warner Brothers movies with traditional 45-day theatrical windows, acknowledging prior skepticism due to Netflix's past streaming-first model. He noted WBD's $4 billion box office last year, which Netflix intends to build upon.
  • Job Creation & Domestic Production:
      • Ted Sarandos: Argued the combined company would "strengthen the American entertainment industry," create more economic growth, and "create more American jobs." He cited Netflix's investment in a New Jersey production facility and plans to increase domestic production, arguing this deal would "continue to push up those jobs."
      • Bruce Campbell: Stated Netflix intends to keep WBD's studio operations intact and invest in continued production.
  • Highly Competitive Market:
      • Ted Sarandos: Described the TV and film landscape as "never been more competitive," citing competition from traditional broadcast/cable, other streamers (Disney+, Peacock, Paramount+), and "deep-pocketed tech companies" like Google's YouTube (number one in viewing time, acquiring sports rights, original programming), Amazon Prime Video, and Apple. He argued Netflix only accounts for "about 9% of TV viewing time in the US" and with WBD, it would be "about 10%."
  • Warner Bros. Discovery's Strategic Rationale:
      • Bruce Campbell: Explained WBD's decision to split into two companies (studios/streaming and global networks like CNN/TBS/HGTV) to enhance strategic flexibility. Netflix's offer was deemed "the best opportunity" to combine existing separation strategy with Netflix's strengths, providing WBD production access to Netflix's distribution. The global networks business (Discovery Global) will be spun out as a standalone entity, with CNN as a "centerpiece" maintaining independent editorial policy.

4. Notable Quotes

  • Chairman Mike Lee (R-UT): "The transaction extraordinary in both scale and in potential consequence raises serious antitrust concerns that warrant scrutiny. Uh which is why we're here today."
  • Ranking Member Cory Booker (D-NJ): "With either merger, another corporation will gain significant control over what we see, what we hear, and the news we consume."
  • Ted Sarandos (Co-CEO, Netflix): "A combined Netflix and Warner Brothers will strengthen the American entertainment industry, preserve choice and value for consumers, and create opportunities for creators."
  • Senator Ted Cruz (R-TX): "CNN is already a dumpster fire. There's nothing resembling objectivity... How are people at home to how should they feel even remotely confident that if this merger happens, the combined entity would not simply be a propaganda outlet pushing one particular political view with much greater market power than you have now?"
The hearing will come to order. Thanks to everyone for being here. The subcommittee meets today to examine Netflix's proposed acquisition for $83 billion of Warner Brothers Discovery. The transaction, extraordinary in both scale and potential consequence, raises serious antitrust concerns that warrant scrutiny, which is why we're here today. Over the past few decades, Americans have changed the way that they view movies and television programming – programming that we used to call television programming, but that now doesn't necessarily air over television. There was a time decades before that when movie night meant going to the theater. Then more recently, over the last four decades or so, there was a time when movie night might have meant a trip to Blockbuster or later to a Redbox. But that's no longer the case. Today, millions of Americans will stream their favorite classic movie or bingeworthy show, bingeworthy series, right at home. Netflix has changed with the times as well, and Netflix has been a significant part of that change. They have grown into the largest streaming platform in the entire world. Netflix has more than 325 million subscribers, reaching 1 billion viewers. Premium Netflix streaming plans can cost as much as $300 per year to access Netflix's impressive and steadily growing library of content, both of licensed content and of original content produced by Netflix on its own. Some of which we would describe as full-length feature films, others that we would describe as series, as we would have previously described as TV shows, even though they don't air on TV. With this merger, Netflix would acquire Warner Brothers film and television production studios, their streaming services, and extensive existing content library. These include Warner Brothers Pictures and Warner Brothers Television, HBO, HBO Max, DC Studios, and iconic franchises including Harry Potter, Game of Thrones, Lord of the Rings, and popular series such as The Wire, The Sopranos, and Succession. The deal would not only give Netflix control over Warner Brothers' existing catalog of entertainment content, but also intellectual property for future productions. The transaction raises potential horizontal antitrust concerns. Netflix and HBO Max both offer subscription-based streaming video services and compete directly for subscribers seeking premium content. Additionally, Netflix and Warner Brothers compete to develop critically acclaimed films and hit series content. So, the merger would eliminate these areas where they compete head-to-head in today's market. Not only do Netflix and Warner Brothers compete for subscribers and for content, but they also compete in a different direction. They compete for the employment and retention of creative talent, including writers, directors, actors, and other production professionals that go along with the process of producing this content. Consolidating two major employers within the same market inevitably has an impact and can significantly weaken competition for that labor. The Department of Justice and the Federal Trade Commission have made clear that monopsony power, which here refers to the potential power to suppress wages or limit professional opportunities, is a cognizable antitrust harm separate and apart from any monopoly-focused concerns. The Department of Justice has demonstrated this when it successfully blocked, for example, Penguin Random House's attempted acquisition of a competitor, Simon & Schuster. Second, this merger raises potential vertical concerns. Netflix is the largest global streaming platform, wielding enormous influence due to its size over what audiences watch through its proprietary recommendation algorithms. Warner Brothers controls a portfolio of film and television content for its part. Combining dominant distribution with additional premier content creates the classic risk of what we refer to as vertical foreclosure. The merged firm would have both the incentive and the ability to put rivals at a disadvantage. It could withhold marquee titles. It could raise licensing fees. It could favor its own content with recommendations. On a platform of Netflix's size and reach, even a small set of changes in visibility could impact competition. This deal also has potential downstream implications for movie theaters. Warner Brothers is a central force in theatrical distribution, while Netflix has generally favored streaming over theatrical releases. This consolidation would increase incentives to divert major releases away from theaters to streaming only on Netflix. That would reduce consumer choice and threaten the viability of movie theaters. These risks are amplified by data ownership. Netflix already, due to its innovation and investments in this area, has unmatched insight into viewer behavior. Combining that with the content library of Warner Brothers could further entrench Netflix's dominance and raise barriers to entry, making it harder for nascent competitors to enter the market and compete effectively. Antitrust cases are often decided on the basis of market definition. How you define the market in which the merged entity would make its appearance makes a big difference. In a subscription video on demand market, if it were defined in that way, the combined firm would likely exceed the 30% market share threshold, thus triggering a presumption of illegality established by the Supreme Court and long-standing precedent in United States versus Philadelphia National Bank. Additionally, this merger does not occur in a vacuum. It doesn't occur in isolation. Netflix recently secured an exclusive multi-year agreement with Sony Pictures. This arrangement, announced just in the last couple of weeks, suggests a broader strategy to consolidate even more premium content behind Netflix's own paywall. Even the uncertainty surrounding this merger risks weakening Warner Brothers as a strong independent competitor to Netflix, which it currently is. All told, one might say that Netflix seeks to become the one platform to rule them all, or at least to exercise a significant amount of market dominance. The merger raises numerous antitrust concerns, in a nutshell, consolidating both production and distribution power. If approved, the merger will likely shape in a really significant way how Americans access movies and series for many decades to come. I look forward to hearing the testimony from our witnesses and Ranking Member Booker. We will now proceed to hear your opening statement. Ranking Member Booker thanked Chairman Lee for organizing the hearing in partnership with his team, noting a bipartisan accord on the current crisis of corporate concentration in the nation, which he compared to the trust-busting days of Roosevelt. He acknowledged Senator Amy Klobuchar, to his left, for literally writing the book on antitrust enforcement, expressing gratitude for her leadership and continued focus. He also thanked Mr. Sarandos and Mr. Campbell for appearing, admitting that testifying before Congress is "not fun," and jokingly wondered if it ranked higher than a visit to a proctologist or dentist. The Chairman interjected that "Proctologists and dentists everywhere are outraged by that statement," to which the Ranking Member responded, "I am sure I will get letters. I'm sure I will get letters." The Ranking Member continued, stating that while they would examine the specific facts of the proposed Warner Brothers Netflix merger, he specifically believed that the sale of Warner Brothers to any competitor could have serious consequences for consumers and the television and film industry. He expressed frustration at the absence of Paramount SkyDance, which had made a hostile takeover bid. He explained that he had invited David Ellison, Chair and CEO of Paramount SkyDance, to appear, but Mr. Ellison declined, stating that Paramount "does not believe it would be useful or helpful for them to participate as a witness" because their offer had been rejected. The Ranking Member requested unanimous consent to submit Mr. Ellison's letter into the record. He thanked Mr. Ellison for meeting privately with him and other Senators, calling it a "fruitful conversation," but regretted it wasn't public. He stressed that Paramount remains a player with a $108 billion hostile bid, making their participation highly relevant. He also highlighted concerns regarding President Trump's involvement and allegations of political favoritism, criticizing the exercise of executive power in an unchecked manner. He cited allegations that Paramount promised the President sweeping changes to CNN after Trump's public comments that "the people that have run CNN for the last long period of time are a disgrace. I think that it's imperative that CNN be sold." He also noted that days after the Netflix Warner Brothers deal was announced, President Trump purchased $2 million in stocks and bonds in both companies. The Ranking Member called it "outrageous" for any Senator, House member, President, or Vice President to trade in individual stocks while possessing "extraordinary insider information and influence over policies that move corporate power." He described this as "another example of the corruption that is cancerously attacking our democracy," where President Trump uses government power to reward allies, punish enemies, and enable private enrichment, leaving ordinary Americans with higher prices, suffering consumers, and wealth siphoned to the top. Beyond this administration's alleged exploitation of antitrust enforcement, the acquisition of Warner Brothers Discovery by Netflix, Paramount, or any other competitor would, he argued, result in concerning consolidation in an economic sector already squeezing consumers and artists. He clarified that the sale would significantly impact tens of thousands of Americans in the entertainment industry and consumers. He had heard "concerns, fears, worries" from "legitimate folks" across the industry. This is not an either-or situation; either merger would give another corporation significant control over "what we see, what we hear, and the news we consume." He reiterated that either acquisition would lead to consolidation in an already consolidating sector, necessitating serious questions and discussion about increased corporate control over media. Reflecting on his own life, born in 1969, he recalled his father making him watch *Star Trek* and Nichelle Nichols, one of the first African-American women to star in such a show, and films like *In the Heat of the Night*. He emphasized how deeply American culture is shaped by extraordinary artists who present diverse voices and inspire a future where, for instance, a Black Senator could serve as a ranking member of such a committee. "Art matters. Art is vital," he proclaimed, expressing real concerns that a merger like this impacts art, culture, American voices, and the nation's "moral imagination." For years, he continued, workers, creators, and consumers were promised that media consolidation would bring more choice, unique stories, and lower prices, a promise he felt had not been fulfilled. Instead, each merger has led to higher subscription costs, fewer streaming options, and shrinking creative opportunities. He had personally heard from artists about lower pay and growing instability, with many who once felt they had chances to bring their art to life now worried about dwindling options. Even pre-merger, a few streaming companies control much of the industry, from content production to distribution and the platforms themselves, indicating extensive vertical integration. Acknowledging Netflix's power as a subscriber himself, he admitted to watching an embarrassing number of hours, confirming it as the dominant streaming platform in his household and the nation. Domestically and globally, Netflix has extraordinary scale, with over 325 million consumers worldwide, compared to Disney Plus's 130 million. Warner Brothers, he noted, controls one of the world's most valuable content libraries, including HBO, Harry Potter, CNN, and DC Comics. The Netflix-Warner Brothers transaction would consolidate the largest distribution platforms with one of the largest content producers, raising critical questions for Americans. He voiced concerns about Netflix gaining more power over consumers, reducing alternatives in streaming, and impacting startups and competition, as well as artists and artistry. He expressed anger that antitrust laws have been ignored by both Democrats and Republicans, leading to consolidation that he believes is "screwing Americans from our farms to pharma." He declared a "crisis in America," asserting that antitrust laws are designed to protect independent studios, smaller streaming services, and new entrants from being shut out by companies controlling both content and distribution. The concern, he stated, extends beyond jobs and prices; his late father would worry about "less movies and less original story-making." He celebrated America's vibrant, world-envied entertainment industry, wishing to protect "what is so profoundly American: freedom, the fact that workers are treated fairly, that consumers have choice, that competition exists." He urged federal enforcers to review the merger fairly and without political bias, without a President's "personality and his political opportunism to bear." He believed Americans deserve to see laws, as conceived by past generations, enforced "justly and thoroughly." He concluded by again thanking the witnesses for their presence. Chairman Lee thanked Ranking Member Booker and stated that his offered material would be admitted to the record without objection. He noted the Senate Antitrust Subcommittee is part of the Senate Judiciary Committee and expressed gratitude for Chairman Grassley's presence, indicating that Senator Grassley would give his opening statement next. Chairman Lee also mentioned that Ranking Member Durbin of the Judiciary Committee had a conflict but would arrive later to deliver his opening statement. Senator Grassley thanked the Chairman, observing that the filled hearing room reflected both great interest and controversy. He stated that the committee's interest stemmed from ensuring the Department of Justice and the Federal Trade Commission fulfill their antitrust oversight duties. He thanked Chairman Lee and Ranking Member Booker for holding the hearing on the proposed Netflix Warner Brothers transaction. He acknowledged America's pioneering role in the film industry, from Hollywood musicals to John Wayne Westerns and modern television, emphasizing its enduring cultural importance and the rapid changes in how people watch productions. The proposed transaction, he explained, would combine Netflix, a leading streaming platform, with Warner Brothers Studio assets and HBO Max, another streaming service, potentially affecting prices and choices. He reiterated that Congress enacted antitrust laws to protect competition and consumers, and the Department of Justice and FTC must apply them with a neutral, evidence-based approach. Congress, he asserted, has an oversight role. Opponents, he noted, warn that the deal could reduce head-to-head competition in streaming, leading to higher prices or less innovation. He highlighted that Iowa families already juggle multiple subscriptions and deserve real competition. Opponents also caution that a combined firm could limit access to popular content, restrict licensing, and hinder rivals. Theater owners, he added, worry about shorter release windows and fewer wide theatrical releases, which could harm local cinemas, their employees, and economies. Competition, he argued, depends not only on subscriber numbers but also on high-quality franchises and libraries, which influence what rivals can license and consumer choices. Control of both a large platform and a deep content library affects industry bargaining power, a dynamic regulators must examine carefully. While companies would argue for improved quality, expanded distribution, and efficiencies from the merger, these claims deserve scrutiny. Any claimed benefit, he stressed, must directly result from the merger, be verifiable, and likely make content available to more families. Critically, he warned against using merger review to stall a company's progress by limiting lawful alternatives in a changing market. He concluded by encouraging antitrust regulators to carefully scrutinize the transaction to ensure competition and consumer protection, thanking all witnesses for their testimony and looking forward to discussion. Chairman Lee thanked Senator Grassley. He then introduced Mr. Ted Sarandos, Co-Chairman and Executive Officer of Netflix, and Mr. Bruce Campbell, Chief Revenue and Strategy Officer at Warner Brothers Discovery, asking them to rise and be sworn in. After they affirmed their commitment to truthful testimony, Chairman Lee thanked them again and invited Mr. Sarandos to begin his opening statement. Mr. Sarandos thanked Chairman Lee, Ranking Member Booker, and the subcommittee members, expressing his honor to be there. He stated that a combined Netflix and Warner Brothers would strengthen the American entertainment industry, preserve consumer choice and value, and create opportunities for creators, offering to answer all questions about the deal. He then provided some personal background, recalling his childhood in Phoenix, Arizona, as the son of a union electrician and a stay-at-home mom, where he found comfort in screens. He described his first job at a local video store, where he learned the importance of stories and helped people find movies, noting that home entertainment offered consumers value, choice, and control, despite the burden of physical distribution. This experience, Mr. Sarandos continued, led him to Netflix, which initially sent DVDs via mail. He explained that Netflix solved the distribution burden, broadening choice and value by providing access to a vast library regardless of location, though it still involved waiting for mail. In the late 1990s, recognizing the internet's transformative potential, Netflix built streaming technology, offering fans thousands of choices with full control over how and when to watch, and at a tremendous value compared to traditional TV. Over a decade ago, Netflix began producing its own programming, such as *House of Cards*, *Stranger Things*, *The Crown*, and movies like *The Irishman*, *Marriage Story*, and *Red Notice*. These productions, he said, have created over 155,000 American jobs and contributed $225 billion to the U.S. economy, with filming in all 50 states. While other media companies have been cutting back their content spending, we have increased ours. If you're wondering if we're in this for the long haul, we're spending $1 billion right now to transform the old Fort Monmouth Military Base in New Jersey into a state-of-the-art production facility, revitalizing the local economy there. We also export American stories to the world. About the only place you won't find Netflix is China because they censor and block us. With Warner Brothers, Mr. Sarandos asserted, Netflix would create more economic growth and more value for consumers. He explained that Warner Brothers' assets are very different from Netflix's, including a century of iconic intellectual property, a world-class theatrical distribution business, and studios that produce and license TV shows to other networks and streamers. He stated plans to operate those businesses largely as they are today. While both companies have streaming services, he described them as complementary, noting that 80% of HBO Max subscribers also subscribe to Netflix. He promised more content for less, distinguishing this from a "Noah's Ark problem" of duplicative assets, as Netflix would acquire assets it currently lacks. He committed to investing in Warner Brothers, preserving it as one of Hollywood's five major studios, and supporting theaters by releasing movies with traditional 40- to 45-day windows, thereby growing the American entertainment industry. Mr. Sarandos emphasized that TV and film competition has never been more competitive than it is today, and this deal would not change that. Netflix's goal is to win "the moment of truth" – that's when Americans sit on their couch, pick up their remote control, and decide what to watch. That's where they compete. Today, consumers can easily choose between broadcast and cable, like CBS and so many other networks, and streamers like Netflix, Disney Plus, HBO Max, Peacock, Paramount Plus, and Tubi. This also includes deep-pocketed tech companies who are trying to dominate the television business: Google's YouTube, Amazon Prime Video, and Apple. If one looks at streaming alone, Mr. Sarandos continued, YouTube is the number one platform for viewing time. They have NFL football, an exclusive deal for the Oscars, and a deal with the BBC to produce original programming. Some of their creators have bigger budgets than typical Hollywood television shows. "YouTube is not just cat videos anymore; YouTube is TV," he declared. This, he explained, is why Netflix, despite all its success, accounts for only about 9% of TV viewing time in the U.S., which would rise to about 10% with Warner Brothers. This deal, he concluded, keeps one of Hollywood's most iconic studios healthy and competitive, and Warner and Netflix together will create value for consumers, more opportunities for the creative community, and more American jobs. He concluded by thanking the subcommittee and looking forward to questions. Mr. Campbell then addressed Chairman Lee, Ranking Member Booker, and the subcommittee members, thanking them for the opportunity to discuss Warner Brothers Discovery and their decision to accept an offer to merge their studios and streaming business with Netflix, while continuing their previously planned separation of their news and sports television networks into a new company, Discovery Global. He highlighted that the iconic Warner Brothers studios have created some of the country's most recognizable television and film productions, from *Citizen Kane* to *Minecraft*. Just last month, their films received 30 Academy Award nominations, tying their own record set two decades ago. Discovery, he explained, grew largely from the cable revolution of the 1980s, delivering a variety of content on cable networks such as the Discovery Channel, Animal Planet, Food Network, and HGTV. As the media landscape evolved, Discovery expanded its focus on digital media, delivering high-quality content across a range of platforms in addition to cable. In 2022, Discovery merged with WarnerMedia, which was then being spun off from AT&T, to create Warner Brothers Discovery. This history, he noted, exemplifies the dramatic and dynamic changes experienced in the entertainment landscape over the past 100 years. Today, those changes are only accelerating as consumers access video content through numerous paid and ad-supported services. Consumers are experiencing a flood of options, including expanded social media platforms, digital and interactive games, and dozens of other entertainment options that compete for their interest and attention. This increasingly complicated media landscape has also resulted in considerable consumer frustration, as the abundance of streaming options has made it more challenging for consumers to find the content they want. Advertising revenue, he added, is also less stable due to marketplace changes, such as the move to on-demand streaming services and the rise of social media. At Warner Brothers Discovery, Mr. Campbell stated, they have continuously sought to innovate and respond to the changing marketplace. The 2022 merger gave them the ability to identify and align common business strategies. They found alignment with their combined collection of sports, news, and other cable networks, and saw a different collection of business opportunities with their studios and streaming business. They concluded that the company would be best served by separating the studios and streaming division from news, sports, and entertainment networks such as CNN, TBS, and HGTV. Last summer, they announced plans to split into two companies, Warner Brothers and Discovery Global. The separation strategy was designed to position each company for long-term success by enhancing the strategic flexibility and sharpening the competitive focus of each entity. That decision, Mr. Campbell said, prompted others in the industry to look anew at the company and its world-class collection of media franchises, brands, and properties. Last fall, they began to receive various outreach and offers, including most significantly from Paramount SkyDance and Netflix. Their board responded with a full review of the company's strategic options. Paramount SkyDance, Netflix, and another company made a variety of offers that their board considered carefully and fully. Ultimately, after careful evaluation of the competing offers, their board unanimously determined that Netflix's offer was and remains the best opportunity for their company. In this proposal, Mr. Campbell explained, they saw a compelling and unique opportunity to combine their existing separation strategy with the strengths of Netflix. This vertical merger permits Netflix to expand its nascent movie and television production capabilities with the addition of the Warner Brothers studio assets. It gives their production capabilities access to Netflix's popular and consumer-friendly streaming and distribution platform. The merger also permits them to continue their planned separation of the global networks business, which they continue to believe is in the best long-term interest of the company. At the same time, the board continues to review and evaluate additional offers as they have been received to determine if any are superior to the Netflix offer, consistent with the board's obligations under law. He concluded by expressing excitement about the future of the combined company with Netflix and looking forward to providing consumers with thrilling and entertaining content across all their storied media properties for many years to come. He thanked the subcommittee and anticipated questions. Chairman Lee then began the question period. He addressed Mr. Sarandos, noting that Netflix currently produces and distributes its own scripted long-form, full-length feature films, TV shows, documentaries, and series. Mr. Sarandos confirmed this, stating it was a small portion of their content library. The Chairman also pointed out that Netflix plans to invest roughly $20 billion annually, apart from the merger, to increase production and licensing, and asked why Netflix sought to acquire a competing content producer and streaming platform. Mr. Sarandos explained that Warner Brothers is both a competitor and a supplier, and Netflix's history and his own professional background are about adding more value to consumers through broadened choice, which requires substantial production and licensing. When asked if "supplier" referred to licensing arrangements, Mr. Sarandos confirmed that Netflix licenses Warner Brothers' television series, movies in various windows, and original and licensed series. The Chairman then questioned whether YouTube invests in original content to the same magnitude as Netflix. Mr. Sarandos stated he couldn't speak to YouTube's specific economic impact but noted they don't share risk with creators in the same way. He added that Netflix competes with YouTube for major deals like the Academy Award and NFL football games, indicating YouTube's significant content spending. The Chairman probed further about YouTube's content, noting it primarily shares advertising revenue with users who produce their own content, often shorter, non-feature-length films. He asked if most regular YouTube content is free and requires no subscription or login, unless for YouTube Premium. Mr. Sarandos, while acknowledging complexity, said he couldn't fully explain YouTube's model due to a lack of transparency. However, he maintained that YouTube is a subscription service with an ad-free option and offers "the exact same content," arguing they compete for the same content, viewers, ad dollars, and programming, citing a YouTube creator's movie, *The Iron Lung*, which grossed $17 million. Still seeking clarity on "exact same content," the Chairman asked if YouTube content typically involved the Screenwriters Guild or studio production. Mr. Sarandos referred to YouTube's CEO, Neal Mohan, who stated that YouTube creators are now acquiring Hollywood studios and producing competitive content, calling it "TV." He emphasized that 50% of YouTube engagement occurs on living room TVs, not mobile devices, and on that screen, it's a "zero-sum game": watching YouTube means not watching HBO Max, Netflix, or CBS. Thus, he contended, they compete for the same pool of content, viewers, ad dollars, and subscription dollars. As his time expired, the Chairman briefly sought to understand if the competition was merely for screen time, like any other daily activity, rather than for truly equivalent full-length, studio-produced content on regular YouTube. Mr. Sarandos quickly replied that while transparent data on YouTube programming types is unavailable, the content is "increasingly the exact same programming," offering *Saturday Night Live* as an example of content viewed on YouTube after its live broadcast. Senator Schiff thanked the Chairman and the witnesses, expressing his appreciation for the opportunity to hear from them. He began by addressing Mr. Campbell, stating that many of his concerns regarding both the proposed Netflix merger and the potential Paramount merger focused on the impact on the workforce, consumer choice, and cost. He noted that most media consolidation has led to layoffs and job losses rather than lower consumer costs, asking if the same should be expected here—specifically, a round of layoffs or increased consumer prices. Mr. Campbell responded emphatically, "No," to both concerns. He explained that Netflix's offer was appealing because, as Mr. Sarandos noted, Netflix lacks the film studio, film distribution, and television studio infrastructure that Warner Brothers Discovery possesses. Based on negotiations, he believed Netflix intended not only to keep those operations intact but to invest further in them, including continued production on their lots in Burbank and elsewhere. On the consumer side, Mr. Campbell added, 80% of HBO Max subscribers already have Netflix. He stated that the transaction would allow Netflix to offer the two services together at a discount and integrate Warner Brothers' and HBO Max's content onto Netflix's platform, which he lauded as the best technology platform available, superior to Warner Brothers' own, making the deal "very pro-consumer." Senator Schiff then turned to Mr. Sarandos, acknowledging their prior discussions. He asked for Mr. Sarandos's thoughts on the impact on the theater industry, noting his personal enjoyment of theatrical experiences. He mentioned concerns that Netflix's business model might lead to a reduction in theatrical exhibition windows from the industry standard of 45 days to as little as 17 days, asking how long Mr. Sarandos expected theatrical exhibition to be and how that might change. Additionally, regarding jobs, Senator Schiff observed that while other studios saw increased domestic production since the pandemic, Netflix appeared to be an outlier with more production moving overseas. He acknowledged Netflix's new facility in New Jersey but questioned the shift of Netflix's production overseas and how the merger would affect it. Mr. Sarandos thanked Senator Schiff and addressed the questions in reverse order. He explained that some overseas production is "local-for-local," creating Korean content for the South Korean market, for example, but stressed that the vast majority of Netflix's content spending results in production in the United States. He pointed out that many studios, including Disney, have moved productions, such as the *Avengers* movies, to London due to production incentives. In response, he has worked with New Jersey to create a competing incentive, leading to the construction of a large studio there. Since that incentive passed, 11 projects—seven initially slated for the UK—were brought back to New Jersey. He affirmed that Netflix is leading production in the U.S. and intends to continue growing it. Regarding theatrical releases, Mr. Sarandos confirmed that the Warner Brothers slate of movies would continue to be made and released with the traditional 45-day window, operating the studio largely as it is today. He acknowledged previous skepticism about his stance on theatrical windows due to Netflix's prior business model, but emphasized that Netflix would now own a "great" theatrical distributor that grossed $4 billion at the box office last year. This theatrical business, he stated, is factored into the acquisition price, and Netflix intends to maintain and invest further in it, supporting jobs and growing U.S. production. This, he asserted, will keep the theater business intact and production in the U.S. growing, adding that Netflix's New Jersey initiative would "change the course of production in the US completely." Senator Schiff then used the opportunity to briefly promote his own bill for a film tax credit to counter overseas incentives, to which Mr. Sarandos offered his strong support. Senator Hawley thanked the Chairman and welcomed the witnesses. He began by asking Mr. Sarandos about labor impacts and inquired about Netflix's current number of US-based employees. Mr. Sarandos estimated "about 10,000," and for production roles, he noted it's a fluid number but estimated about 155,000 production jobs on their originals at any given time, offering to provide a more exact figure later. Senator Hawley then asked Mr. Campbell about Warner Brothers' US production jobs, who estimated them to be in the "8,000 range." Senator Hawley then asked Mr. Sarandos for his projection on the amount of domestic production two years from now if the merger goes through, specifically whether it would be static or decline. Mr. Sarandos unequivocally stated that Netflix's domestic production would increase, explaining that most of their production is already domestic and would continue to grow. He mentioned their recent forecast to grow content spending to $20 billion, covering both licensing and producing, noting that licensing also compels production. The Senator then presented data on US production, indicating a "pretty much straight downward" trend since 2021, with a "massive shift in production overseas to other foreign jurisdictions." He asked Mr. Sarandos for a commitment to increasing domestic US production and jobs. Mr. Sarandos responded, "Absolutely," stating it's part of Netflix's business plan and long-term operating DNA. He respectfully clarified that the chart shown represented Los Angeles, to which Senator Hawley countered that while it was Los Angeles, the data was "reflective" and indicated that overall US production was "not going up" and that jobs had "moved." He highlighted this as a significant concern for labor and requested to submit a statement from the Teamsters—who, along with "pretty much every segment of labor," shared major concerns about the deal—into the record. He noted that labor groups worried about cuts to residuals (payments) and production jobs. Senator Hawley then sought specific commitments from Mr. Sarandos, asking if he would commit to using union labor for all domestic shoots. Mr. Sarandos confirmed, "We do use union labor." When pressed for a commitment to continued use for all domestic shoots if the deal went through, Mr. Sarandos replied, "Senator Hawley, this is the most unionized industry in America, and we use union labor on all our productions." When Senator Hawley reiterated, "I'm looking for a commitment," Mr. Sarandos said, "You have my commitment." Next, Senator Hawley asked for a commitment to a 45-day or longer theatrical release window for major Warner Brothers films. Mr. Sarandos affirmed, "Senator Hawley, I just said that I would do that under oath. So, yes." Senator Hawley then asked for a commitment to fairly compensating workers with residual payments. Mr. Sarandos began to explain, "Top-of-market payment has been Netflix's business philosophy since we started operating over 20..." but Senator Hawley interrupted, stating that Netflix had been routinely accused of "compressing and reducing residuals," a major labor objection. He challenged Mr. Sarandos to address this and commit to "full residuals." Mr. Sarandos attempted to explain that it was a "very complicated answer because we prepay." Senator Hawley interpreted this as "usually a way of saying no," and again sought a "yes." Mr. Sarandos maintained, "Senator, I understand that this is not a yes or no answer because—" which Senator Hawley again interjected, "It kind of is, though." Mr. Sarandos clarified that he and Netflix were "going to sit down with the unions for new contract talks that start actually in three days." Senator Hawley expressed disappointment, calling it a "complicated, long no" and reiterating his hope for a "yes." Mr. Sarandos then asked if Senator Hawley was requesting a commitment to "residuals in the traditional form," as he didn't believe that applied in many cases. Senator Hawley concluded by saying Mr. Sarandos had "some work to do on that" and hoped he would "reconsider his position." Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Thank you, gentlemen. I appreciate you being here. Mr. Sarandos, when we met, I indicated to you the big concern I have is what the impact is on the creative community. This is something rooted in a practice we started years ago at Netflix: creating competitive buying teams internally. For example, if you are a writer and you bring a project to us, we have a family team, a comedy team, and a drama team. Mostly because of one of two reasons: either you have the "Noah's Ark" problem where you bought a company that has the exact same sets of assets that you do, so all you do is cut everything in half and look for the profit, squeeze it out. That is not what we are doing; we do not have any of these assets that we are buying from Warner Brothers. The other problem usually is a company that does not have particular expertise in that business, and they wind up just sucking cash out instead of putting cash in.
