The topic shifted to Netflix's position on the deal, specifically whether it requires any divestiture or other remedy to address anti-competitive concerns. The question was if that was still their position, to which the reply was, "That's our position, but it's going to ultimately be up to the Department of Justice." The discussion then turned to the term "litigating the fix" among antitrust lawyers, a technique where a deal is taken almost to completion, and then a "fix" is proposed voluntarily to demonstrate magnanimity or a pro-competitive stance, even if no problem was initially acknowledged. The question was posed: if no fixes are necessary, would they avoid litigating the fix here? The response was, "I'm sorry, Senator. It's too premature to talk through it." The Senator then asked if they had pre-litigated the fix, noting that such arrangements are sometimes conceived and arranged privately before a deal's consummation, known to those negotiating it. The response was, "There's been no discussions about that that I'm aware of." The conversation moved to Netflix and Warner Brothers being among the largest buyers or acquirers of creative labor in the industry. The statement was agreed with, as the company spends little on sports, so most of its programming budget goes to creative content, unlike other networks that might spend significantly on sports. When asked about planning to spend $20 billion a year in production and if any other producer spends more, the response was that on the entertainment side, they spend roughly $12 billion. They noted that it's a very competitive industry, with Disney spending more, and Paramount and Universal spending in that same general ballpark. Acknowledging that this is an enormous sum of money, a question was posed about the post-merger scenario: assuming no hiccups and the merger proceeds, wouldn't it be fair to assume there would be fewer independent studios, or fewer studios generally, competing to hire writers, directors, actors, and other production professionals? A clarification was sought, leading to an explanation: pre-merger, there are two companies with separate revenue streams. Post-merger, they would be under one ownership, combining their production costs and outlay plans. Therefore, it was reiterated, wouldn't there be fewer studios competing for talent? The reply clarified that with this deal, they intend to keep Warner Brothers Studio, both television and film, operating exactly as they do today, meaning no fewer studios. As an example, it was stated that a potential Paramount deal would collapse from five to four studios. The Senator acknowledged this plan, recognizing it keeps the five studios intact, but expressed that the company’s history suggests they are not content with the status quo, observing market trends, technological changes, and shifts in market power, implying no guarantee that the current plan would continue indefinitely. The Senator light-heartedly referenced the company's origins in a video store. The Chairman noted he had gone over time and yielded to Senator Booker. Senator Booker began by asking Mr. Campbell about a report that Mr. Sandos met with President Trump on November 24th and discussed Netflix's bid to buy Warner Brothers at length. He inquired if Mr. Campbell or Warner Brothers Discovery CEO David Zaslav had met with President Biden to discuss Discovery's acquisition of WarnerMedia from AT&T in 2022. Mr. Campbell replied he was unaware of any such meeting. Asked if he or David Zaslav had met President Trump to discuss the current transaction, he confirmed they had not. Senator Booker then questioned the appropriateness of meeting with the President to discuss a transaction. Mr. Campbell stated that in such situations, all parties often reach out to anyone in government with an interest in the transaction, so he didn't consider it unusual. Senator Booker then brought up Skydance, which closed a deal with Paramount last year, concurrently with paying President Trump $16 million to address his grievances about a 2024 "60 Minutes" interview with Vice President Kamala Harris. He asked if that struck Mr. Campbell as improper. Mr. Campbell responded that it was not a decision they were involved with. Pressed for his personal opinion under oath, he said, "It was unusual, yes." Senator Booker expressed his belief that antitrust laws should be applied objectively, following the facts. However, he noted they were in an "unprecedented" or "unusual" environment where private organizations, including universities, law firms, and corporations, are making deals with the President. This includes contributions to his ballrooms, providing him with information leading him to buy stock in those corporations, and offering pro bono services, all seemingly to curry favor with a President exercising "unusual sway" over these deals. He asked if this was a concern. Mr. Campbell replied, "We are staying focused on the regulatory process." Senator Booker acknowledged this but argued that many are missing the larger erosion of democracy, citing past presidents who viewed anti-trust issues as an erosion of free markets and democratic ideals. He declared they were in a "constitutional crisis" with a President "pocketing literally hundreds of millions of dollars" from those whose companies' destinies he influences. He reiterated the report that Mr. Sandos met President Trump for over an hour on November 24th, less than two weeks before Netflix won the bidding war for Warner Brothers, and that within days, the President bought a significant amount of their stock. He stated that the reporting indicated much of the conversation covered Netflix's bid and that Mr. Sandos left with the impression President Trump wasn't opposed to the deal. Senator Booker asked what the President said about the proposed transaction. Mr. Sandos clarified he could not speak to the reporting directly, but confirmed he had met with the President a few times to discuss the state of the entertainment industry generally, including the production economy, runaway production, incentives, tariffs, and protecting American jobs. He believed it was proper for the President to engage with industry leaders. When pressed if the merger deal was discussed at all, Mr. Sandos admitted the President asked for a general overview of what was happening with the deal, but stated it was a very small part of the meeting, not its specific purpose. Asked if he felt President Trump wasn't opposed to the deal, Mr. Sandos replied he felt the President was interested in protecting American jobs, labor, and industry. When asked if he thought the President's intentions were noble and objective, he said the President, in his experience, has been nothing but interested in protecting and creating American jobs. Senator Booker asked if it was appropriate for the President to be involved in a Justice Department merger review, given his stated intent to be involved in decision-making about the merger. Mr. Sandos expressed confidence that the case would stand on its merits and be handled by the Department of Justice as the proper channel, without commenting on the President's involvement. Senator Booker rephrased: was it appropriate for the President to buy stock in a company undergoing a merger review by the Justice Department, in which he says he will be involved? Mr. Sandos declined to comment on the President's personal finances. Senator Booker then pushed further, noting his own sale of Netflix stock upon entering Washington and asking if that was appropriate. Mr. Campbell responded that any such trading would be "definitely worth questioning" and that no one with insider information should be trading stock. Senator Booker then asked if Mr. Campbell would agree that the President, if he bought stock, would also be acting inappropriately. Mr. Campbell reiterated that he did not think anyone with access to private inside information should be trading in the stock. Senator Booker asked if merger reviews should be political, to which Mr. Campbell firmly replied no, believing they should be overseen by the Department of Justice to protect consumers. Senator Booker then stated that a core challenge facing the country is society-impacting mergers being handled by a Justice Department overseen by a President who "seems to take personal tribute." He cited examples of legal settlements where the President and his family receive direct payouts, the President buying stock days after private conversations with leaders, and the President insinuating that these decisions are for him, not an independent Justice Department. He described this as a "corrupt political system" where there's a lack of transparency regarding political contributions from corporations to politicians influencing the process. Mr. Sandos interjected, assuring Senator Booker there was no request or offer related to the deal in their conversation. Senator Booker accepted Mr. Sandos's word, acknowledging his integrity, but emphasized a larger problem: the staggering growth of corporate power in America, creating unconscionable wealth disparities. This power, he argued, is exercised in a way indicative of crony capitalism, with individuals gaining access to the President, giving him millions, while he oversees decisions affecting the nation's culture. He described the moment as "distraught" with growing corporate power and consolidation, where corruption is no longer hidden, and even witnesses acknowledge the "unusual" or "unacceptable" nature of events like the President trading stock and overtly claiming sway over decisions. He spoke of fear from parties not just for self-interest, but for society spiraling towards concentrated wealth and corporate power influencing politicians. He criticized politicians on both sides trading on insider information, making profits from such moments, stating that it "should anger every American." He expressed gratitude to the witnesses for coming, recognizing it wasn't easy, and placed the current context of corruption not on them but on a nation that should rightfully be angry. He shared a personal story about his father, a young Black boy in a segregated theater inspired by a movie about Manhattan, who later became IBM's first Black salesman in his region, moving his family to New Jersey. His father taught him to "watch art, watch the artists." He lamented that his dad grew up in a different capitalist system where companies like IBM had allegiance not just to shareholders but also to workers and communities, with fewer income disparities, and corporations weren't considered people by the Supreme Court, nor could they influence politics as they do now. He thanked the Chairman and affirmed his trust in the witnesses' statements, but reiterated his worry about society and the context in which the merger would be evaluated by actors he deemed not independent, who "brandish their corruption with bravado." He concluded by calling for a halt to these "disastrous" trends, clarifying that this burden doesn't fall on the two companies trying to "imagine something better." He announced future questions for the record, stressing the importance of gathering information, and expressed distrust in the administration's evaluations. He ended with a prayer that the witnesses' hopes for the merger would come to pass, referencing a New Jersey poet and worrying about the loss of poetry and art, and its impact on future generations seeking inspiration, like his father. The Chairman then light-heartedly noted that Netflix isn't in poetry yet, but "poetry streaming" could be a new thing if anyone could find it. He thanked the witnesses for coming, acknowledging the good information exchanged, and announced that the record would remain open for a week before closing, at which point the hearing was adjourned. The witnesses offered thanks.

FULL INTERVIEW: Dylan Patel Says We’re Still Underestimating AI

TBPN(48.1K) ·43:42 · 2026-02-03
Dylan Patel John Coogan Jordi Hays
> "I think the big challenge is that chips are just really unreliable...
TL;DR The primary bottleneck for AI infrastructure is rapidly shifting from energy supply to the limited capacity of leading-edge chip manufacturing (TSMC and memory), while AI hardware companies like Nvidia and Google are diversifying their chip offerings to meet the...

TL;DR The primary bottleneck for AI infrastructure is rapidly shifting from energy supply to the limited capacity of leading-edge chip manufacturing (TSMC and memory), while AI hardware companies like Nvidia and Google are diversifying their chip offerings to meet the fast-evolving, specialized demands of the AI market.

The Future of Data Centers: Space vs. Earthly Constraints

  • Space Data Centers Feasibility: While launch costs are decreasing (e.g., Starship) and heat dissipation is manageable with large radiators, the reliability of chips in space presents a significant hurdle.
      • Chip Unreliability: Chips are inherently prone to failure; Nvidia GPUs can experience 10-15% initial failure rates in terrestrial data centers, requiring immediate human servicing.
      • Servicing in Space: The absence of human technicians means servicing requires either highly advanced robotics (an additional engineering challenge) or substantial on-board redundancy, significantly increasing complexity and cost.
      • Cost Fallacy: Although solar power in space is effectively "free," power accounts for less than 10% of a data cluster's total cost. The remaining 90% associated with maintenance, reliability, and logistics remains a major unaddressed cost in space.
  • Long-Term Potential: A bet exists on 1% of global data center capacity (1 gigawatt) being in space by 2028, requiring roughly 150 Starship launches. While ambitious, it highlights the perceived long-term potential despite current reliability challenges.
      • Satellite Advancements: Newer Starlink V3 satellites offer 100x more bandwidth and increased power output (aiming for 100 kilowatts per ton), advancing the technological capability for in-orbit compute.

AI Hardware Evolution: Specialization and Diversification

  • Nvidia's Strategic Pivot: Nvidia is undergoing a "vibe shift," moving from a general-purpose GPU approach (e.g., H100/H200) to specialized chips designed for specific AI workloads.
      • New Offerings: This includes the upcoming CPX chip for prefill, video, and image generation, as well as the newly acquired Grok chips.
      • Rationale: This diversification is a hedge against the rapid and unpredictable evolution of AI, aiming to engineer solutions across multiple points of the "Pareto optimal curve" to capture diverse market needs.
  • Google's TPU Proliferation: Similarly, Google is diversifying its TPU roadmap, with Broadcom and MediaTek developing distinct TPUs tailored for different requirements (e.g., high flops/low memory vs. high on-chip memory). A third TPU project is also underway.
      • Objective: To cover the entire spectrum of AI workloads, addressing varying demands for training, inference, numeric precision, and memory configurations.
  • Cerebras and Inference Acceleration:
      • Value Proposition: Cerebras offers significant speed-ups for time-sensitive, long-running inference tasks (e.g., AI agents), where customers prioritize speed over cost (willing to pay 10x for 10x faster completion).
      • OpenAI Partnership: OpenAI’s 750-megawatt deal with Cerebras targets the "absolute cutting edge, most price insensitive customers" for specific prompt types requiring rapid responses, complementing OpenAI's projected 16 GW by 2028.
  • Cross-Data Center Training: Google leads in multi-data center training, achieved by co-locating data centers within close geographical regions. The shift to Reinforcement Learning (RL) and agentic training, which requires less frequent and smaller data synchronization, now makes multi-data center training "completely reasonable" for other players.

Geopolitics, Supply Chains, and AI Supremacy

  • Shifting Bottleneck: The primary constraint on AI capacity is transitioning from energy supply (2024-2025) back to leading-edge semiconductor manufacturing (2027 onwards).
      • Energy Solutions: The energy sector has rapidly adapted, deploying tens of gigawatts annually (e.g., 30 GW projected next year) using diverse generation methods, overcoming initial infrastructure readiness issues.
      • Semiconductor Uniqueness: TSMC represents a unique, irreplaceable bottleneck. Unlike power generation, procuring a 3nm fab on demand is impossible. Memory makers have also not significantly expanded capacity since 2022, and building a state-of-the-art fab takes years due to extreme complexity (e.g., cleanroom air circulating every 1.5 seconds).
  • China Strategy Debate: The geopolitical dilemma involves balancing the risk of pushing China into aggressive actions (e.g., military, supply chain takeovers, or Taiwan) against the imperative to control critical AI technology.
      • Control Points: Opinions vary from completely banning chips and AI model access (Dario's view) to allowing chip sales (creating dependency on Western ecosystems like CUDA, per Ben Thompson) but restricting equipment.
      • Economic View (Dylan Patel): Selling equipment allows China to capture disproportionately more AI economic value. Providing AI model access (via APIs) allows the West to capture more of that value through services. China has historically resisted reliance on American tech ecosystems, preferring to develop its own.

AI's Economic Impact and Shifting Industry Beliefs

  • AI for Non-Coders: AI tools are democratizing development, enabling non-programmers (e.g., hedge fund analysts using Claude Code) to build complex financial models and perform data analysis without traditional coding expertise.
  • Market Underestimation: Despite strong belief in AI, many institutional investors (hedge funds) still hold conservative estimates for AI's market impact, with Dylan Patel arguing their figures are often too low.
      • Massive Projections: Few currently believe AI startup revenue will exceed $100 billion by year-end, or that Anthropic could reach $300 billion by decade-end, or that OpenAI will need 16 GW of power, yet these are presented as achievable due to the immense economic value creation.
  • Oracle's Communications: Oracle's recent public statements reassuring investors about secured financing and OpenAI's financial health were criticized as "terrible comms," highlighting market anxieties amid negative sentiment towards OpenAI and the strong performance of competitors like Anthropic.
  • Jensen Huang's Duality: Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang possesses two personas: a PR-savvy public figure adept at generating enthusiasm, and a "business killer" deeply knowledgeable about every aspect of the supply chain and deal negotiation, capable of terse, precise responses in high-stakes situations.

Meta's AI-Driven Growth and Strategic Play

  • Algorithmic Revenue Growth: Meta is significantly benefiting from AI, with recent earnings showing a 9% increase in ad CPMs driven by double-digit improvements in their ad algorithms, despite a challenging consumer spending environment.
  • Wearables and AI Assistants: Meta's "galaxy brain take" is a long-term bet on AI-powered wearables becoming dominant, with Meta aiming to out-execute competitors like Apple and Google in this space to deliver integrated AI assistants.
  • Content Consumption and Platform Dominance: AI will generate highly personalized content, increasing digital product usage and engagement on Meta's platforms. Meta aims to win by being the premier platform for this AI-generated content.
  • Aggressive Investment & Talent Acquisition:
      • Strategic Licensing: Meta has licensed Midjourney's code, data, and models in a deal estimated to be over $1 billion.
      • Talent Poaching: They have aggressively recruited top talent, including numerous Google Search personnel, through similarly high-value deals.
      • Compute Sourcing: Meta is even acquiring compute capacity from smaller, "long-tail" cloud providers to meet its massive and rapidly growing AI demand, in addition to its large-scale deals.
  • Future Vision: The ultimate goal is to integrate AI assistants into wearables, enabling seamless real-world interactions for information retrieval, purchasing (e.g., identifying and buying products seen), and other commerce activities, effectively transforming how users engage with the digital and physical world.

Good to see you again. Good to have you. Data centers in space, what do you got? When are we going? Me, you, the International Space Station. Let's break it down. The space tourism industry is quite a fun one, right? Would you go? Would you do the Blue Origin thing where they blast you out past the Karman line? Good enough for Katy Perry? It's not good enough for you. What's going on? It's like you're in freefall, you're not actually counting down. I want to be going around for days. I want my bone density to start to atrophy, right? I truly want to feel the negative effects of space. It's not enough just to go back. I think I would do it. It's like 90 seconds, right? Yeah, but it's better than hanging out. All the cool stuff that astronauts do, like put water and then they're bubbling, and then you try and drink the water. So they'll be unplugging the GPU, plugging it back. Oh yeah, that's how you pay for your space tourism. You've got to go and service. 90 seconds of service, one 90-second trip at a time. People were wondering, TPUs, Nvidia going on the Starlink V5 or whatever gets up there. It feels like this will be something more like a Tesla silicon chip, an AI chip. Do you have any insight into what the process, if you wind up figuring out how to heat dissipate, if you wind up figuring out the costs, what might the chip look like? I think everyone freaks out, "Oh my God, putting stuff in space is expensive." But if you look at Starship launch costs, they keep falling, and you're fine. I think by the end of the decade, the cost of space launch will be fine. Heat dissipation, I mean, it's a challenge, but you just put a massive radiator, and it's fine. By the end of the decade, you'll be good. I think the big challenge is that chips are just really unreliable. So, how do you deal with a couple of things? Satellites can only be so large before they start needing a lot of support and structure, before they tear themselves apart. So when you look at the launches, these things are shooting out tiny satellites, and many of them. So you can't have a big fully connected cluster of chips. And then on top of that, how do you deal with any random error? On Earth, you have techs running around the data center, unplugging stuff, putting in spares, things like that. What do you do in space? You RMA it to the factory where they might unsolder it and resolder it, and then test it, and it works, and go back out. Sometimes it is just trashed. But that's the challenge to me. I feel like maybe the pattern we should be looking at is how often the Tesla self-driving chips need to get serviced because that's the team that would probably be building or bridging the gap there. The Starlink satellites, sure, they go down, but the service works. You're just relying on some sort of 90% uptime, stuff's coming down, but most people in a Waymo, the chip keeps working, right? Most people in a Tesla self-driving, you don't hear about Tesla owners being like, "I love FSD, but I'm constantly in the shop getting my custom silicon chip unseated and re-seated." Well, it's also a function of the complexity of a chip. If a chip is twice as fast and the bit error rate, how often a bit flips, is the same, then it's erroring out twice as often. But let's say the chip is 10x as big. So when you look at a Tesla FSD chip, very good, very efficient, still relatively inexpensive and cheap compared to a big old GPU or TPU or whatever. Those things are extremely large. Again, if the error rates are the same, then it fails 10x more, but in fact, the error rates are a bit higher because they're pushing these things to the absolute limit. Whereas Tesla does have some level of, well, first of all, the Tesla car has two chips, a sort of redundancy already built in. Maybe you do that on the satellite, but then that's more power. President Trump Participates in a Bill Signing, Feb. 3, 2026

The White House(2.9M) ·35:39 ·193.6K views · 2026-02-03
Donald Trump
President Trump signed the Consolidated Appropriations Act to immediately reopen the federal government and fund operations through the fiscal year, highlighting its role in cutting wasteful spending, bolstering national security, and implementing fiscally conservative reforms.
TL;DR President Trump signed the Consolidated Appropriations Act to immediately reopen the federal government and fund operations through the fiscal year, highlighting its role in cutting wasteful spending, bolstering national security, and implementing fiscally conservative...

TL;DR

President Trump signed the Consolidated Appropriations Act to immediately reopen the federal government and fund operations through the fiscal year, highlighting its role in cutting wasteful spending, bolstering national security, and implementing fiscally conservative reforms.

Bill Signing and Key Contributors

  • Act Signed: President Trump signed the Consolidated Appropriations Act, which reopens the federal government and funds the majority of operations for the remainder of the fiscal year.
  • Purpose: The President described the bill as a "great victory for the American people," presenting it as a fiscally responsible package that cuts wasteful spending while supporting critical programs.
  • Acknowledgements: The President thanked numerous individuals for their work on the bill, including:
      • Speaker Mike Johnson: Praised for doing an "incredible job."
      • Representatives/Senators: Tom Cole, Ronnie Jackson, Buddy Carter, Stephanie Bice, Ken Calvert, John Carter, David Joyce, Steve Womack (with condolences for his late wife), Robert Aderholt, Susan Collins, Cindy Hyde-Smith, Bill Hagerty, Lindsey Graham, Mario Diaz-Balart, Katie Britt, John Barrasso, Shelley Moore Capito.
      • Staff: Hal Duncan (Associate Director of Legislative Affairs), Susan Rosser-Averson, Betsy McDonald.

Policy Achievements and Fiscal Impact

  • Ending "Woke Programming": The bill officially ends taxpayer subsidies for "radical far-left woke programming" on NPR and PBS.
  • Foreign Aid Reduction: Slashes nearly $10 billion in "wasteful foreign aid spending" and replaces USAID with a new "America First Opportunity Fund" to provide assistance serving American interests.
  • IRS Program Cuts: Cuts funding for "abusive and weaponized IRS programs," with a Republican Senator noting $10 billion was stripped from the IRS in the bill. The President also referenced his own experience as a "victim" of alleged IRS harassment and leaks.
  • Military Funding & Shipbuilding: Fully funds the military, includes a pay raise for service members, and makes "historic investments" in the American shipbuilding industry.
      • Steel Production Milestone: The U.S. produced more steel than Japan for the first time in over 30 years, signifying a resurgence in the industry.
  • Deportation & Border Security: Continues funding for deportation flights removing "dangerous illegal aliens." The President claimed the border is secure with "nobody coming across" illegally for nine months, though this conflicted with his later statement about 25 million people entering.
  • Melania Trump Initiative: The bill includes a "Melania Trump Foster Youth to Independence Initiative" within the Department of Housing and Urban Development, aimed at supporting foster youth transitioning out of the system.

Economic and Safety Outcomes

  • Economic Growth: The President cited a 5.6% GDP, claiming it could have been 7% without a previous 42-day shutdown, and that this bill prevents further economic loss.
  • Healthcare Reforms: Includes significant healthcare reform, EBM reform, and efforts to reduce drug prices through a "most favored nations" policy, aiming for prices "tied with the lowest price anywhere in the world."
      • Expanded Access: Extends telehealth services and addresses maternal mortality, with a focus on rural healthcare.
  • Crime Reduction:
      • Washington D.C.: Murders are reportedly down 80-97%, making it a "very safe city."
      • Memphis: Crime is down 77%.
      • New Orleans: Crime is down 68% in three to four weeks.
      • Minnesota: Crime is down due to federal removal of "thousands of criminals."
      • National Claim: Crime is at its lowest since 1900 nationally, despite what the President called an "open border stupid policy."
  • Taxpayer Benefits: Anticipates tax return increases of at least $1,000, no tax on tips or social security, and an interest deduction for loans used to purchase American-made cars.

Broader Policy Debates and Controversies

  • Election Integrity: The President advocated for federal government intervention in elections, suggesting "nationalizing elections" if states demonstrate "horrible corruption" or "rigged, crooked elections" in areas like Detroit, Pennsylvania, Philadelphia, and Atlanta. He also emphasized the need for voter ID.
  • Immigration Policy (Sanctuary Cities): Senator Lindsey Graham vehemently rejected Senator Schumer's suggestion of requiring search warrants for ICE, arguing it would hinder deportations. Graham also called for ending "insane policy of sanctuary city" and plans to bring a vote to the floor.
  • International Engagements:
      • Iran: Negotiations are ongoing, with Iran reportedly seeking a deal.
      • Colombia: A productive meeting with President Gustavo Petro focused on counter-narcotic efforts and sanctions.
      • Venezuela: The U.S. is "getting along very well with the leadership" and will work with them against guerrilla and terrorist groups.
  • Epstein Files: The President addressed the resignation of Peter Mandelson over Epstein links, claiming "Wolf" (a reporter) and Epstein conspired against him, and framing the Epstein issue as a "Democrat problem."
  • Hillary Clinton Deposition: Expressed mixed feelings about Hillary Clinton's impending deposition related to Epstein, comparing it to his own past legal challenges.
  • Savannah Guthrie's Mother: Expressed concern over her missing mother and offered federal assistance.

Notable Quotes

"This bill is a great victory for the American people. Instead of a bloated and wasteful, omnibus monstrosity full of special interest handouts, we've succeeded in passing a fiscally responsible package that actually cuts wasteful federal spending while supporting critical programs for the safety, security, and prosperity of the American people." — President Trump "America is back. The the hat is appropriate. It's the right right slogan." — Speaker Mike Johnson "We stripped $10 billion from the IRS in this bill that you just signed." — A Republican Senator "If we have to get a search warrant to get 15 million people out, Schumer is telling me he doesn't want them out. It's the most ridiculous idea... So, Mr. President, stick to your guns." — Senator Lindsey Graham

What’s the Future of Vertical SaaS in an AGI World? Jamie Cuffe, CEO of Pace

Sequoia Capital(130.0K) ·51:56 ·7.8K views · 2026-02-03
Jamie Cuffe Lauren Reeder Pat Grady
Pace, led by CEO Jamie Cuffe, is disrupting the $400 billion BPO market, starting with insurance, by replacing traditional human outsourcing with AI agents that handle end-to-end mission-critical processes.
TL;DR Pace, led by CEO Jamie Cuffe, is disrupting the $400 billion BPO market, starting with insurance, by replacing traditional human outsourcing with AI agents that handle end-to-end mission-critical processes. Through a strategy of deep customer partnership, forward-deployed...

TL;DR

Pace, led by CEO Jamie Cuffe, is disrupting the $400 billion BPO market, starting with insurance, by replacing traditional human outsourcing with AI agents that handle end-to-end mission-critical processes. Through a strategy of deep customer partnership, forward-deployed engineering, and a focus on transforming industry economics, Pace achieves 50-75% cost savings and significantly higher accuracy for its clients.

Pace's Vision: Transforming the Services Market with AI

  • The Agentic Process Outsourcer (AI-PO): Pace's core thesis is a fundamental shift from human-based BPO (Business Process Outsourcing) to AI-based outsourcing, starting with the insurance industry.
      • This move aims to replace "human touch and human judgment" with AI agents capable of nuanced, end-to-end process execution.
  • Target Market & Opportunity:
      • Insurance Focus: Insurance is highly operationally intensive, lacks integrated software, and relies heavily on emails and PDFs due to numerous intermediaries and no common API – making it an ideal starting point for AI agents.
      • Market Size: The total BPO spend across Banking, Financial Services, and Insurance (BFSI) is nearly $400 billion globally, with insurance alone representing $70 billion. This is comparable to the entire global cloud software market.
  • Economic Disruption: Pace aims to transform BPO economics:
      • From a 10-15% gross margin business to an 80% gross margin business.
      • Replacing hundreds of thousands of human workers with hundreds of thousands of AI agents supported by a small team of insurance experts.
      • A product-led flywheel where every improvement in the product directly increases gross margins as Pace takes on harder, more complex tasks.
  • Long-Term Ambition: Inspired by Constellation Software's aggregation of niche vertical software, Pace seeks to build a platform that aggregates and transforms the economics of niche vertical services across BFSI and beyond.

Building and Deploying Mission-Critical AI Agents

  • End-to-End Automation: Pace builds AI agents to execute any standard operating procedure (SOP) completely end-to-end, rather than segmenting AI into tiny, isolated tasks.
      • This addresses the "human judgment" and numerous edge cases that previously made BPO work difficult to codify deterministically.
  • Human-in-the-Loop Strategy:
      • Minimal Intervention: The vast majority of workflows are processed by AI end-to-end with no human in the loop.
      • Onboarding & Improvement: Pace's operations team is primarily involved in the onboarding process, labeling examples to achieve initial 90% accuracy, then enabling a self-improvement loop to reach the 99.5%+ accuracy required for critical workflows.
      • Progressive Difficulty: As AI masters simpler tasks, the operations team shifts focus to the next most difficult workflows.
  • Strategic Workflow Selection:
      • Pace targets workflows already outsourced to traditional BPOs at scale, as these typically have:
          • Well-codified SOPs.
          • Existing accuracy checks (internal QA, sampling).
          • Easier change management (spinning down a BPO service vs. retraining a W2 workforce).
      • Focus is on high-volume use cases promising the biggest ROI.
  • Typical Engagement Path:
      • Starts at the "top of the funnel": submission intake (new risk data extraction, business logic, underwriting platform integration) or claims intake (First Notice of Loss).
      • Expands down the policy servicing lifecycle (endorsement processing, policy administration, billing) and full claims processing (payout, QA).
  • Transformative Metrics: Pace delivers significant improvements:
      • Cost Savings: 50-75% cost savings for customers in a short timeframe.
      • Speed & Scalability: AI works 24/7, enabling faster business closing and handling seasonal spikes (e.g., hurricane claims) before backlogs form, eliminating the need for scaling up human BPO workforces.
      • Observability: Provides transparent insights into task status, agent reasoning, and bottlenecks, allowing customers to redesign workflows for optimal efficiency.
      • Enhanced Accuracy: AI agents can achieve "way more accurate" results than humans on complex, multi-rule tasks (e.g., a 300-page document with hundreds of rules), ensuring consistent quality that scales beyond any "best BPO rep."
  • AI Technology Wish List:
      • Improved web agents specifically for complex CRUD operations on diverse insurance administration panels (which differ from common web agent demos).
      • Advancements in Reinforcement Learning (RL) and reinforcement fine-tuning for end-to-end agent decision-making, leveraging Pace's gradable outputs and reward function potential.

Building Trust and Ensuring Customer Success

  • 100% Pilot Success Rate: Pace prides itself on every pilot being successful and progressing to production.
  • Core Value: "Closing the Distance":
      • Deep Domain Expertise: Pace's team possesses deep understanding of the insurance industry, speaking the customers' language from day one.
      • Forward-Deployed Engineering (FDE): This key strategy involves placing highly skilled engineers (often former founders or technical commercial individuals) directly on-site with customers.
          • Role: FDEs do "whatever it takes" to ensure customer success, from understanding use cases, prompt tuning, and creating eval sets, to system integration and even shipping code directly into Pace's product codebase.
          • Feedback Loop: This creates a tight feedback loop, rapidly translating customer issues into product improvements and reducing deployment times.
          • Team Balance: Pace maintains an 80% engineering team to productively leverage FDE insights.
  • The "Trust" Imperative:
      • In a market where customers recognize AI's potential but also its risks, Pace focuses on selling "trust as much as anything else."
      • Core Value: "Be the Rock": Pace aims to be a dependable partner for its customers, mirroring how insurers serve their clients during difficult times.
      • Strategic Rollout: Pace partners with top-tier, most trusted insurers first, building credibility and results that speak for themselves before expanding through the market.
  • On-site Implementation:
      • Engagements start with understanding AI opportunities from the COO's office.
      • Focus on real production data from the outset, with clearly defined (often challenging) success criteria.
      • Existing SOPs (often 50-100 pages) are converted into "Agent Operating Procedures" within Pace's product, leveraging various tools for long-context retrieval, rule-based reasoning, and integration with customer systems (APIs or web agents for non-API systems).
      • This approach has consistently made Pace the fastest vendor to achieve PSC (Production-Ready State) and full production for many clients.

Company Culture & Founder's Insights

  • Core Value: "Set the Pace": This value embodies urgency, speed in delivering customer value, internal velocity, and intentional progress towards the broader mission.
  • Solo Founder Advantages: While challenging in early, existential stages, a solo founder can, once direction is clear, build a highly engaged and stronger team by distributing roles and decision-making traditionally held by co-founders (e.g., values, GTM hires, product decisions).
  • Advice for AI Founders:
      • Embrace the Fun: Building with AI is immensely enjoyable, allowing for rapid development of workflows that were previously extremely difficult to codify in traditional software.
      • Become a Forward-Deployed Engineer: Jamie Cuffe recommends this as the best training ground for founders, as it forces close engagement with customer problems, seeing solutions through to production, and developing both building and selling skills.

Notable Quotes

"if you build, you know, for the ability for these AI agents to take any standard operating procedure and be able to run that process end to end, that's the direction that we're sort of taking at at pace." — Jamie Cuffe, CEO of Pace "the goal of what we're going for is how can we take that from you a 10% gross margin business to an 80% gross margin business where instead of having hundreds of thousands of people it's hundreds of thousands of AI agents and a small team of insurance experts." — Jamie Cuffe, CEO of Pace "We have a one of our other values is be the rock which is basically like our customers are there for their customers. They're insured on their hardest days and we are there for our customers every single day." — Jamie Cuffe, CEO of Pace "I think the role that you guys or Harvey or Sierra plays in some ways is just saying, "Hey, trust us. We're going to make sure you're good, right? Like we're going to make sure that this new world of AI is to your benefit, you know, and it's going to make your business better and we're going to do whatever it takes to make that happen." — Pat Grady, Host, Sequoia Capital
I think if you try to shoehorn an AI into these tiny little boxes and then add a code layer around it, you are missing the point of what can be done with AI today. If you build for the ability for these AI agents to take any standard operating procedure and run that process end to end, that is the direction we are taking at Pace. On this episode of Training Data, we sit down with Jamie Cuff, the founder and CEO of Pace, to explore how AI agents are quietly revolutionizing insurance and what that means for the future of work in high-complexity, highly-regulated environments. Jamie shares why the real breakthrough isn't just automating tasks, but architecting AI to handle nuanced end-to-end processes previously thought to require a human touch and human judgment. We dive into building trust with some of the biggest and oldest companies in the world, why forward-deployed engineers are becoming a secret weapon for AI companies, and how AI replacing BPOs could fundamentally rewire entire sectors of the economy. Enjoy the show. Jamie, thank you for joining us. Thanks for having me. It's great to be here. We would love to dig right into what you're building. I'm curious, you started a company last year. What are you building and why? Pace is the agentic process outsourcer for insurance. What that means is we have seen over the last couple of decades a big shift from onshore to BPO work, and our thesis is that over the next decade, we're going to see a big shift from outsourcing to outsourcing to AI. So we focus exclusively on insurance carriers, helping them automate a lot of the critical back-office operations that traditionally are being outsourced to BPOs. Can I ask about the path that brought you here? Top of your class at Princeton, investor at Sequoia, rising star at Retool, insurance BPOs—it's not obvious that that would have been the next step on the journey. Can you explain how you got there? It's a circuitous path, and what's funny is I actually started there. I grew up around insurance my whole life. I was born in London, grew up between New York, London, and Bermuda, which are all capitals of insurance. The throughline there is my dad actually ran operations for a reinsurer and then a Lloyd's coverholder in London. So I always knew about this problem, but starting my company out of college, I didn't immediately run towards that until I got to Retool. At Retool, a ton of our customers came from financial services. From the outside, many people think of Retool and think startups, but Retool is really attacking a lot of this bigger market around custom software. The places where there's most custom internal tooling are the businesses that are most operationally intensive, and financial services firms are a lot of those because they don't have a lot of great off-the-shelf software. So what we were seeing was a lot of very large legacy insurers were using Retool, all the way through to the most tech-forward ones, like Ethos and Vouch, and businesses like Progressive and Berkshire Hathaway. At that point, being in a forward-deployed engineering capacity, you get a front-row seat to seeing a lot of the problems these companies are handling. What was really cool is a lot of times people would build Retool applications that would help streamline some of their back-office operations. So I got to see a lot of the problems I think my dad was also dealing with in his business: policy administration, claims submission intake, a lot of the core workflows that are critical to insurance operations. At the time, with software, you can make a little dent in BPO spending; you can make it 10% better. I think that was a lot of the promise of OCR and RPA and some of these prior technologies. But what happened after the ChatGPT moment, and when I was lucky to be working on a lot of Retool AI and some of the new products coming out of Retool, you saw this opportunity to completely change the economics of that industry. Instead of augmenting outsourced services with software, you could actually go and completely replace the vertical service. Insurance is a particularly good starting point for that because the lingua franca of the industry is emails and PDFs. The main reason for that is there are a lot of intermediary players, and there's no common system on which they all work. With the lack of an API, the alternative is email, PDFs, and phone calls. Now, AI agents truly have the ability to bridge that gap. What gave rise to a lot of BPO work—manual data entry handling of these documents and emails—is now also what perfectly positions this for AI agents. So we see that as a really good starting point for the opportunity to build the agentic process outsourcer across many industries longer term. Jamie, how do you build a better business than the last generation of BPOs, even though you're taking over the same workflows and a lot of the same spend that's going on today? People often ask what keeps me up at night, and that's the main thing. We are very lucky right now to be working with incredible customers. We have the opportunity to build a very large company; just being a BPO, there are multiple tens of billions of dollar public companies that are running at 10-15% gross margin with tens or hundreds of thousands of people doing this work. Obviously, those are great businesses and exceptional teams. But for us, we think there's a much, much bigger outcome. When I talk with our team about what we're shooting for, building a multi-billion dollar BPO but not changing anything about the economics of the industry, that's the failure case. The goal of what we're going for is how can we take that from a 10% gross margin business to an 80% gross margin business, where instead of having hundreds of thousands of people, it's hundreds of thousands of AI agents and a small team of insurance experts. I think that is a much, much larger company. The last step is how we do that not just in one vertical, but across many verticals. Those are the two things I think about. People might say, 'Push gross margins to later,' or 'It's not that important.' I think people have a lot of perspectives on gross margins. I think it is important, and I don't think it's important to necessarily have from day one, but you need to have a path to it. People talk a lot about flywheels, and the flywheel that we want to create is every time we're making improvements in the product, the gross margins go up. That's the big thing that we're pushing on: even if we're lower gross margin today, as we take on harder and more complex tasks, how are we constantly seeing that number shift up over time? As you're building with agents and starting to plug into these email and document processing workflows, I'm curious what you're seeing is working in practice. We've talked to folks from OpenAI here, doing fine-tuning. They have all the data, they have amazing infrastructure. We've seen companies with much lighter processes on the back end. What actually works for you in practice? A lot of the types of workflows that we focus on are these mission-critical processes that have been outsourced, and they want you to do that work end to end. The prior generation, pre-AI, when tackling these problems, yes, extracting data from unstructured documents is great, but it's just one part of the problem. It's extracting it, reasoning over that, applying human judgment, and then also being able to write back into those internal systems, truly solving what previously could only have been done with humans. One way you could think about this is to build one of these workflow DAG-kind of builders and infuse AI nodes in different places where you can do document extraction or email sending back and forth. Part of the reason BPO work exists is it was so hard to codify that work deterministically. The reason is there was this human judgment component and lots of edge cases that came up. If you try to shoehorn an AI into these tiny little boxes and then add this code layer around it, you are missing the point of what can be done with AI today. I think that product stance is relatively short on AI. If you build for the ability for these AI agents to take any standard operating procedure and run that process end to end, that's the direction we're taking at Pace. So, do you have a human in the loop right now or no? For the vast majority of our workflows, there's no human in the loop. It's AI processing end to end. We do have our own operations team that we're building out for the most complex workflows. The way we see the role of human-in-the-loop is that we have our operations team to do it. We can escalate to our customers, but primarily where that is used is in the onboarding process. As we're getting customers up and running, we want to be able to label a bunch of examples and make sure that the agent is starting out of the box at maybe 90% accuracy, and then how do we get it up to the 99.5% plus that our customers require in these critical workflows? That's where we see this human labeling component, and then a self-improvement loop that helps us get there. Over time, we reduce the amount of human in the loop and move that operations team to work on the next most difficult task. When you engage with an insurance carrier and they have X number of workflows that they're currently outsourcing or that could be outsourced to a traditional insurance BPO, I presume you can't do all of them today. Which can you do today, and how do you engage such that you pick off the workflows that are really applicable to AI today, and then how do you grow over time to take up more of those workflows? We tend to focus on use cases that are already nicely outsourced. So, they have to already be using a BPO at scale. The reason for that is it's already codified as a standard operating procedure. There's already a way to check for accuracy because this is usually being reviewed by someone internally or at least QA'd on a sampling basis. The last part is, as you think about change management, it's much easier to spin down a BPO service than it is to retrain or move a W2 workforce. So, we start with the operations team BPO, the large carrier. We tend to focus on the highest volume use cases where we think there's the biggest ROI. You look at the line item for BPO: where are they spending the most, and where can they get the most ROI from AI? Usually, that means we start at the very top of the funnel, like submission intake, where they're getting in new risk, extracting that data, running their business logic over it, and then writing back into their new business underwriting platforms. The same can happen on the claims side, such as first notice of loss or claims intake, getting that information into their internal systems. Once you do that, customers ask you to work down the policy servicing life cycle to endorsement processing and other policy administration tasks, all the way through to billing. On the claims side, the same thing, from claims intake all the way through to payout and quality assurance. So that's usually the path we take. What's a typical before-and-after situation? Human BPO and then Pace AI BPO. What metrics change when they go from before and after? It's interesting in the BPO world. I think a lot of where you start is, 'Okay, there's a new technology that can now help me save a lot of my costs.' With some BPOs, I think the NPS is quite low, and it's a massive line item for a lot of our customers—eight figures plus of spend. Because it often starts with cost, and that's big, for some of our customers, we've been able to achieve 50 to 75% cost savings in a very short period of time working with them. But usually, what happens after that is they realize there are a number of other things that are actually much more important to them. Usually, these are things that previously just couldn't be done with BPOs that now, with AI working at superhuman speeds, you have the capability to do. An example of that is if you think about a submission intake process, usually that carrier receiving that submission is not the only one receiving it. If you can get back much faster, you can actually close business faster. The same thing, we think about scalability. BPO work is not perfectly linear over the course of the year; it's highly seasonal. You can think of a gen one enrollment period or hurricane season, like we're in right now. If there's a massive spike of hurricane claims, you have to spin up a bunch of other BPO workforce to do that work. Now you can have AI working for you 24/7 to crank through that backlog before it even becomes a backlog. Those are a few of them. The last thing is observability. A lot of this BPO work in the past has been a black box. It was very hard to see what was getting done, what stage each task was in, or where the bottlenecks might be. Some of this is just good old-fashioned software workflows. Now that you're able to do this work with agents and literally see exactly the task being taken, the reasoning, and the why, you can surface a lot of that information back to customers so they can actually redesign their workflows, not just do the same stuff that they were doing with their BPO. I'm curious as you work with these massive companies with mission-critical core business workflows, such as processing claims or payouts. What does it take to actually help them succeed? On our end, we see studies from MIT saying 95% of AI pilots fail, and then we also see our portfolio companies not failing. What do you see, and how do you make sure that you see a lot of companies not failing? Yes, exactly. I think the 95% number hit the headlines, and everyone was like, 'Oh my gosh, what's going on?' The truth is, getting AI working in production is definitely not a done deal when you walk in. There are certainly challenges that we've seen across the industry, with other vendors or building in-house, where they haven't been successful. So for us, what we really focus on is one of our core values: closing the distance. We've been lucky that every single one of our pilots has been 100% successful and gone through to production, and I'm very proud of that. How did you pull that off? That's not normal. I think the main thing is we really believe in forward-deployed engineering. I think that might be a hot take, and I'd be curious for your thoughts on this, but my thoughts are that we're very much taking the customer-back point of view. When we go in there, we're on-site with our customers, we're flying to them. We deeply understand the insurance industry, and we are only focused on these workflows. We have a ton of insurance experience on our team. So immediately, as we go into these customers, and they talk to us, they say, 'Okay, they're speaking our language. They understand what we're doing.' But the next thing is we have to do the hard work. Often, that's doing the hard work upfront, not necessarily once you're live in production with your seven-figure-plus contract. It's working with a customer as a partnership to prove out that this can work. This is even more important in AI, where there were a lot of exciting demos early on that maybe didn't pan out. So we focus on really helping our customers get to value and get to something in production as quickly as possible. What that means is for our forward-deployed engineering team—this is a team I was lucky to build out at Retool—I think at the time, deployed engineering, after Palantir, Retool had maybe one of the most scaled deployed engineering teams. The profile that really works for deployed engineering is basically former founders. It's engineers who want to be commercial, or commercial people who are technical. The critical thing is just to do whatever it takes to make the customer successful. So it's understanding the use case. It's helping them when they need help with an AI model; we help them prompt tune and make that successful. It's diving into the operations data and figuring out how to create a really clean eval set for them. It's helping them get integrated into their internal systems. It's all the hard work that actually makes these pilots successful. How do you—one thing I'm curious about—because we've seen this in a bunch of other domain-focused AI application companies: Do you hire AI people and teach them insurance? Do you hire insurance people and teach them AI? Do you just hire athletes, and they learn as they go? How do you think about the intersection of technical skills and domain knowledge? What do you look for? I think the critical thing is you have to hire both on the team and sit them closely together alongside your customers. That way, you get this constellation approach where everyone learns from each other. For us, that means hiring people directly from the insurance industry who have worked on exactly these mission-critical operations at top-five carriers at scale, and then sitting them right next to a former YC founder who has worked on an AI company at the cutting edge of building AI agents. Then, they come together to get this done for the customer. Our experience has been that there aren't that many people already ramped up on AI agents. In the insurance world, we've been lucky to find incredibly forward-thinking people who are excited about helping usher in this change. I think part of that is people resonate with the thesis; many have worked in the BPO world and they see, 'Okay, this is where the world is going.' I've been incredibly impressed by people we've been able to get from both backgrounds and their ability to pick this up quickly to get the full set of skills you need. Back on the topic of forward-deployed engineers, is that a temporary phenomenon because the capabilities coming out of the AI world are not quite there in terms of what we need to deliver a full, reliable solution to customers, or do you think 10 years from now you'll still have forward-deployed engineers on every account? I think I'll answer this by flipping the script to you for some of your thoughts. I actually think you and I both agree on the end state. In the short term, I think it's hard to go wrong getting more and more deployed engineers to work with our customers. The main reason is we have this flywheel both within our customers—helping them get successful with the product and then going on to larger and larger use cases where we can really expand into this very large set of budgets they have around BPO spend. We also have an internal flywheel: our forward-deployed engineers can work inside of our product and work with our customers, but they can also ship code into our codebase, and they do. That's something we test in interviews, on-site, and throughout their work. What's really important is having this very tight feedback loop of working with the customer. You see an issue, and you immediately go back and fix it. It's not like creating a ticket and passing it to somebody else who maybe does it, and then maybe you get it back to the customer. You get that done really, really quickly. What should happen over time, or what we're already seeing, is deployment times are going down. The amount of work that deployed engineers are doing is becoming much, much higher leverage because we're pushing that back into software workflows. So, in the short term, I'm very pro continuing to build with our customers and build early with forward-deployed engineering. But I agree with you that I think the long term is that software-codified workflows are ideal. We enable our customers to build inside of our product, and it's not all just forward-deployed engineering. We might get the first one or two up and running, and we have customers where we've turned around, and now they have 9 or 10 in production because they have a business analyst who has been building out additional workflows. That's very much the way that we think about building the product. The critical thing is when you think about a team, you have to make sure that the layers and where you're investing in the team match up with that. So if your forward-deployed engineering team is like this and your engineering team is like this, you're going to have a problem where you're not really investing in the product. For us right now, basically 80% of our team is engineering, and then we have forward-deployed engineering at the very top, and then we have this go-to-market team. As long as you keep that in balance, we're going to be able to really get that flywheel going. But I think the timelines are maybe the big thing that I'd be curious for your thoughts on. For me, I think it's okay for us to continue doing this for a long period of time because it just makes our customers successful. Maybe we can check back in 10 years as we're going public. I actually think it's a great story to have in the public markets, seeing that you've got this big forward-deployed engineering team, and you're just constantly figuring out how to make them higher and higher leverage so they can do more and more. I think we're pretty well aligned on this. Our observation on this market has been, maybe to overstate it a bit, if you think about bottoms-up distribution versus top-down distribution as two ends of the spectrum, you want to be pegged out at one end or the other. You don't want to be stuck in the middle. Bottoms-up ChatGPT is a bottoms-up distribution product. Open Evidence is a bottoms-up distribution product. These are not comprehensive solutions, but they are wonderful tools that people see, love, and figure out what to do with. On the top-down end of the spectrum, Harvey is a solution for law. Sierra is a solution for customer communications. You guys are a solution for insurance, right? I think if you're going to go the solution route, we're at a moment in time in this market where what you're selling to your customers is trust as much as anything else. They can see the magic of AI. They want to believe in the potential, but they can also see the issues and where it can go wrong. I think the role that you guys, or Harvey, or Sierra play in some ways is just saying, 'Hey, trust us. We're going to make sure you're good. We're going to make sure that this new world of AI is to your benefit, and it's going to make your business better, and we're going to do whatever it takes to make that happen.' I think forward-deployed engineers in that context not only really help to give customers confidence, to your point, but it also creates that feedback loop into the organization so that you're not theorizing as to what problems you can solve. You're actually there where the rubber meets the road, constantly going deeper and deeper into their workflows. I think that's spot on. The trust point is so important, particularly in our industry. Insurance is the business of trust. We have another one of our values: 'Be the rock.' 'Be the rock' or 'be the rock?' 'Be the rock.' Okay, got it. 'Be the rock.' The idea there is our customers are there for their customers; they're insured on their hardest days, and we are there for our customers every single day. That's what we want to do. I love it. This industry is built around trust. A lot of what we think about is working with the most trusted brands, the very top insurers, or the top end of the market, building that brand of trust and credibility, and the results that speak for themselves. Then, looking to expand through the market. I think, similar to Sierra and Harvey and a number of other companies, they've done that really, really well: you go and work with the top customers in that industry where you can get the highest volumes, the highest ROI. They're the ones that stand the most to gain. Make them really successful, and then the product speaks for itself as you go to your next set of customers. We've gotten two values out of you so far: 'Close the Distance' and 'Be the Rock.' What else? What are the other company values? So, we're two for two on good ones. What else do you have? We only have one more. Keep them short. The last one is my favorite, which is 'Set the Pace.' You both know me really well, and I think I have a pretty high level of urgency and, perhaps, impatience at times. We feel very, very lucky to be in the position that we're in. It is such an amazing time to be building a business. I'm sure you all see this from your vantage point as well, investing in businesses. We are so lucky to be working with the companies that we're working with and to be charting this path. I think it's ours to go and make this future a reality, so I feel 'Set the Pace' really encapsulates that. Naming the company Pace is all about speed, and that's a lot of the value that our customers are seeing, but it's also what we want internally. I think it's also intentional—a step at Pace towards our bigger mission. We're moving as fast as we can to get there. Nice. Tell us about that bigger mission. I'm curious where you go once you've gone deep in insurance. There's a lot of room to expand, but your ambitions are much bigger than that. Maybe it starts when I was lucky to be an intern here. One thing I learned that summer was that Andrew Reed's favorite company is Constellation Software. I don't know if that's still the case. I'm pretty sure technically we'd have to say that his favorite company is Figma. Figma, yeah. Okay. Or Robin Hood or CLA. We can rerun this to say his favorite non-Sequoia portfolio company in the public market—not investment advice—is Constellation. For those who don't know about Constellation, what is really exciting about that company, until I think last week, was run by this incredible founder Mark Leonard, who built this company for 30 years. His vantage point was, 'Let's build a business that can span many different verticals.' What they did in software was basically aggregate all of these niche vertical industries that, in and of themselves, were not scaled enough to be legendary long-term businesses. But at scale, in a federated model that they have, it's an incredible business that has compounded massively over the last couple of decades in the public markets to, I think, one of the largest companies in Canada, like 70, 80 billion dollars. There's a really interesting opportunity right now to do the same thing that was done in software, but for the services market. As we've talked about, the services market is orders of magnitude larger. If you look at BPO spend in just banking, financial services, and insurance—which are the markets that we see immediately in front of us—it's about 400 billion, a little bit under 400 billion, about the same as global cloud software every year. 70 billion of that is in insurance. So basically, the full BFSI BPO spend is the same as all cloud software. If we can be a platform on which you can aggregate all of these niche vertical services across BFSI and maybe beyond longer term, there is just a huge opportunity there. I think the most interesting opportunity is that it's not just about aggregating them; it is also about transforming the economics of the industry. This is one of the things that I think is interesting about Pace or other domain-specific application layer AI companies: a lot of the markets that people are going into are sneaky big, much bigger than you might realize, because they're not household names, they're not B2B SaaS products. The other thing that I think is interesting, and you alluded to this earlier, but I want to hit on it again—this idea of going after the BPO spend. You have a clean interface with your customer because there's already an interface between the customer and the BPO. You have concretely defined objectives because they have to define those to be able to interact with the BPO. You have built-in eval because when that work comes back, they're checking it somehow to make sure that it's actually right. A lot of times, they're doing champion-challenger models with different BPOs.
