I think that strikes me as an important aspect of what you're building too. I think that's exactly right. I mean, a lot of our customers are really tuned into the fact that AI has this opportunity to dramatically transform their business, particularly in areas where there is this high volume of highly repetitive tasks that require human judgment. You look at your BPO, and that's exactly where that is. The fact that there are already standard operating procedures that we can model in our product, and then the ability to do the accuracy checking of the built-in eval, also do the change management afterward much faster so that customers can really get hard cost savings, hard ROI savings, is really great. It's also helpful as we go to market with our customers that there's a baseline in place. Transparently, sometimes that baseline is not as high as we think it should be. The average accuracy rates in BPOs are like making 5 to 10% error rates. That's kind of where most people feel it is. But for some of our customers, we've seen it's actually much, much higher. The main reason is if you're doing, when people talk about accuracy with AI, they're usually like, 'Can the AI be as accurate as a human?' We think in a lot of the use cases we're working on, it could be way more accurate. The reason is, let's say you're doing a claims QA or a policy QA. You've got a 300-page document, maybe a big claims file, and you've got hundreds of rules. If you're looking at this thing at the 15th hour on a Friday and you're trying to figure out how to apply these hundred rules to this policy document, that's a really hard task for any person to do. Chances are, you're going to miss something on page 298 of this 100-page rule list. That's something that AI is really good at and can do that same thing at the same quality every single time. So the consistency you can have, you can really scale your best BPO rep. That's the promise. I think anybody who's ridden a Waymo in San Francisco appreciates the benefit of a driver that always has perfect attention, has seen every corner case a million times. I think that we have the existence proof that a properly trained AI can do a lot of jobs better than most people, and I think applying that to all these other verticals makes sense. Tell us a little about what it looks like concretely when you're on-site with a customer: what are some of the things that you're hearing from them? How do you get implemented, and then what are the metrics that you track at the end to make sure that you were successful? We've talked at a high level. I'm curious to get some specific examples. Absolutely. A lot of our engagements start with the office of the COO, really understanding where the opportunity for AI lies and coming together on what I hesitate to call a proof of concept. I actually think POCs get a bad rap and aren't really the right thing to do in AI. It's basically about getting them to success as quickly as you can. What we focus on is not dummy or demo data, but how we can actually go in and make them successful. So we pick something to work on, and then what's really critical is you define the success criteria upfront: where do we want to get to? Sometimes we encourage customers to give us their hardest success criteria. We want to show them that we feel confident in our product, and we're up for that challenge. Defining success criteria upfront is very important. Then, it's about having a great partnership, and we're super lucky with that with a lot of our customers. We fly on-site, we work with them, we take their existing standard operating procedures. These are usually documents that are 50 to 100 pages long, with 60 different steps. There might be a highlight in this document like, 'You've got to get this thing out from here, and then you've got to enter it into this admin panel,' with a little red box around it. It's crazy. Are there shadow SOPs? If you just implemented the standard operating procedures exactly as they're given to you, would that actually give them what they want, or are there shadow rules that are not codified in the document that you would figure out over time? Like, people are actually doing things a little bit differently than what the procedures say. I think we're lucky that because we work mostly on BPO workflows, they had to be pretty well codified because you have to hand these off to folks. But there is always a little bit of a gap there. That's part of what, when you close the distance to the customer, you figure out really quickly. Going back to it, basically we take these standard operating procedures and get them into what we call agent operating procedures. In our product, that means it's almost like a Notion-like document where you can write out all the steps that you need to do in natural language and then use various different tools along the way that specifically enable these agents to do everything they would need to do in the insurance industry. A good example is we have a lot of tooling for long context retrieval and extracting information from really challenging document sets. We have a tool for human reasoning over lots of rules. We have a tool for writing back into a lot of the mission-critical vertical systems of record. Similarly, a lot of our customers deal with systems that don't have APIs. Either they haven't been built out yet, or they might not even own that end system because it's some intermediary. Think of a broker working with a carrier portal. There, we actually just use web agents to be able to write back to this. That's the critical part that a lot of the forward-deployed work drives: building out these tools with our customers to be able to get these agents live. The critical thing is doing whatever it takes to get them live. If there's an API, we'll use the API. If there isn't, we're going to use the web agents. We're going to make them successful. The last thing is getting live into production from there. We've been lucky with a lot of our customers. We're working again in the context of their most mission-critical use cases. In a lot of cases, we have been their fastest company ever to POC and their fastest company ever to production. That's just going on-site and spending time with them. As you build these agent workflows, technology has come a long way over the last even 12 months here, but I'm curious what's on your wish list for things that you wish were easier or what you want to come next. I think we've definitely benefited a ton from the scaling curve of reasoning models, particularly for our very complex documents that we handle. That is not a 100% solved problem, but for us, a lot of the document extraction stuff, we are able to get much better than human reliability. The next frontier I think, really for us, is web agents. So, for any folks listening from the big labs here, we'd love the best possible web agents; please send them our way. We are very lucky to be a company running a lot of those workflows and have a lot of great use cases in insurance. The canonical task is basically doing CRUD operations on insurance admin panels. I'll be honest, they look very different from booking a restaurant or booking a flight, or a lot of the web agent demos that we see. So, a lot of our work is just making those successful, and web agents would be really great. The last one is thinking about longer term: the opportunity for reinforcement learning (RL) and reinforcement fine-tuning in what we do. Because of the way that we've structured our product, and because we are long on AI and what it can do, longer term, our product is set up in a way where it's not a workflow diagram builder where the AI agent only has context for that individual block that it's in. You would never—the analogy I draw here is—if you had your best person on your team, you're not going to put them in this box and say, 'Okay, you need to categorize these documents and not have any sense of what happened before or after in the workflow.' So, what we've really built into the product is the AI being able to make the decisions end to end and follow these agent operating procedures. What that means longer term is it really sets us up well to benefit a lot from RL because the agents are working from input all the way through to output in a way that is highly gradable, where you can see the accuracy of the work that's being done. There's an expected output that you can grade against and create a reward function. It makes it very, very easy to actually do RL for each of these individual workflows for our individual customer at scale. So, I think if you think about the long term of the product, it's not codifying all this stuff into determinism to try to make the agent successful. It's really giving the agent this perfectly modeled world that it can work in and being able to do RL end to end across all the tool calls. So, I think that's one of the big wish lists and areas that we see the world going. What has surprised you about building with AI, or what advice would you have for other founders who are building in AI or thinking about doing so? I think two things have surprised me. One is how much fun it is. This is such a great time to be building, and there's all this stuff. People talk about being able to build faster with AI, and certainly that's the case. We're lucky; we use a lot of tools like Cursor, Cloud Code, and Codex that help our team be really successful. But even more than that, there's all this stuff that was really hard to codify in software that is now really easy to codify with LLMs. We've been able to just build workflows that traditionally I think would have taken decades to build the software, and all the knobs and dials and requirements to get those live in production because you can offload a lot of that to the agent. So, it's a really fun time to be building. The second thing I think is really important, and maybe it's some advice for founders, is to go be a forward-deployed engineer. Ideally, come do a Pace, but I really do think that is the best training ground for becoming a founder because you go and spend a lot of time with customers, you see their problems, and you help them get it into production. That's really at companies: you're either building or you're selling, and forward-deployed engineers do both. So, that's my big advice. If you're thinking about starting a company, it will get you close to problems. It'll get you working on the right stuff, and you'll build the right skill sets to build the company. I was very lucky at Retool. I was very fortunate. I think both David Scher, our CEO, and Brian Shrier, who we're lucky to be working with again on Pace, were very kind to take a big bet on me early on at Retool, to figure out how we build out our post-sales motion, how we take a lot of our new products from zero to 100. I think that really set me up to start a company in a way where it's never easy, but it has felt much more tractable this time than the first time I started a company. I'm very grateful for that, and I encourage other folks to find that path and find people like that who will back you again and again. I feel very fortunate for that. Awesome. Shall we do some hot takes? Let's do it. Okay. What was your hot take from interning at Sequoia? What did you learn, other than Andrew Reed's love of Constellation? I'm not sure if you remember this one, Pat, but we were out on the back patio in the 2800 office, and I think you asked me a similar question: What did you find most surprising about Sequoia? There's a sentiment that people don't really like solo founders generally as an investment. I'm very thankful to my co-founder for that in my last company. But once you have figured out exactly where you're going and it's all execution risk—for Pace, everything is execution risk now. It's so clear what we're doing; it's just executing. There are a lot of benefits of starting a company as a solo founder with the right team that you build. We've been super lucky. Here is an executive briefing summarizing Nelson Peltz's discussion at WSJ Invest Live:

Executive Briefing: Nelson Peltz at WSJ Invest Live

TL;DR: Nelson Peltz, CEO of Trian Fund Management, discussed Trian's evolution from "activism" to "constructivism," emphasizing a strategic shift towards whole company acquisitions, particularly leveraging AI through new partnerships. He also shared strong views on the transformative power of AI, critiqued current corporate governance, and offered insights on political and media landscapes.

Trian's "Constructivist" Approach and Evolution

  • Defining Trian's Strategy: Nelson Peltz clarifies Trian's approach as "constructivism" rather than activism, focused on fundamentally improving company operations.
      • Goal: Increase sales much faster than historically, accompanied by earnings growth, by identifying new ancillary avenues and structural changes within companies.
      • Method: Avoids solely balance sheet solutions like leveraging up, stock buybacks, or hiving off businesses.
  • P&G Case Study: Cites the Procter & Gamble engagement as a prime example of successful constructivism.
      • Outcome: The stock rose from the $70s to the $160s, nobody was fired, and "peace broke out immediately" after Trian joined the board.
      • Result: Achieved through sales growth and structural changes, not financial engineering.

Strategic Shift to Whole Company Acquisitions (Trian 2.0)

  • Returning to Roots: Trian is transitioning back to its original model of buying entire companies, a strategy successfully employed in the 1980s and 90s (e.g., American National Can, Snapple).
      • Motivation: The private equity market has "fallen on its face," leading to more reasonable prices and productive deals, allowing Trian to implement plans quickly without boardroom "dance."
      • New Era (Trian 2.0/0.5): This represents a shift towards acquiring companies with a strategic plan, rather than solely responding to Wall Street bids.
  • Janus Henderson Partnership: Illustrates this new direction, involving a significant investment and subsequent agreement to buy the rest of the company.
      • Investment Details: Trian bought stock in 2021, and recently agreed to buy the remaining shares at $49, a similar multiple to their initial purchase in the low $20s.
      • Impact: Trian partners joined the board, a new CEO (Ali) was brought in, turning outflows into inflows and driving company growth.
  • Leveraging AI with General Catalyst: Trian partnered with Hermont (General Catalyst) to apply AI to asset management "in a very meaningful way" at Janus Henderson and potentially other ventures.

AI: Transformative Power and Potential Risks

  • Major Breakthrough: Peltz views AI as a significant breakthrough, potentially as impactful as the cotton gin.
      • Societal Impact: Expected to make society "way better off" and vastly more efficient, noting even his non-tech-savvy wife uses AI regularly.
  • Anticipated Challenges: Acknowledges there will be "pain" and a "rough period" due to job displacement.
      • Government Intervention Concern: Expresses worry about government putting the brakes on AI development due to fear of unemployment, potentially causing the U.S. to lose its leadership position.
  • Early Adoption: Humourously claims Trian has been "doing AI before AI even existed."

Corporate Governance and Shareholder Democracy

  • Critique of Index Funds: Identifies index funds, not proxy advisors, as the primary issue undermining shareholder democracy.
      • Problem: Index funds own approximately 25% of most companies (effectively 30-35% of votes) but "don't care if the stock goes up or down" as they earn a fee regardless.
      • Desired Change: Advocates for legislation to empower shareholders who genuinely care about stock performance (up or down) and enable direct engagement with them.
  • Disney Succession Planning: Critiques Bob Iger's handling of CEO succession at Disney.
      • Prediction: Believes Iger will use Josh D'Amaro's (new CEO) lack of entertainment background as an excuse to remain in a guiding role.
      • Trian's Experience: Sold Disney stock after losing the proxy fight; notes the stock significantly underperformed the market (would be near $200 if it kept pace, currently $105, Trian sold at $119).
      • Operational Concerns: During Trian's engagement, noted that management brought to meetings were all "staff jobs" with no P&L responsibility, indicating a disconnect from core operating businesses.

Views on Leadership, Media, and Politics

  • Donald Trump and Tariffs: Expresses mixed views on former President Trump's policies.
      • Agreement: Generally agrees with "most" of what Trump has done.
      • Disagreement: Believes Trump is using tariffs "the wrong way"—as a source of revenue rather than a threat to lower trade barriers for closer to free trade; hopes this is temporary.
  • Personal Life and Press: Humourously acknowledges his family's recent media attention, advising them to "stay the hell out of the press," while expressing well wishes for his daughter's marriage.

Notable Quotes:

Thank you, Nelson. Wow, bright lights. We've been talking about doing this for a while, so I'm excited to finally have you up here. Let's start with the big picture. Trian just celebrated its 20-year anniversary at the end of last year, a big milestone. You've obviously seen a lot over your career: some 40 engagements with companies, 20 boards, just four proxy fights during that time. How has the activism landscape evolved during those 20 years? I don't like the word activism. Let's start there. In our case, it's constructivism, and I don't say that with tongue in cheek. We don't go into these companies to fire people. We don't go into these companies to leverage them up and buy back stock. If your solution is only a balance sheet solution, then you don't need us there. You just call your local investment banker, and he can tell you how to change your balance sheet around. Our goal here always is to figure out how to get sales up much faster than they historically had, with earnings to go along with it, and have them look at new ancillary avenues for them to do that. Some of the bigger names we were involved with included PNG, which was the largest proxy fight ever and ended beautifully. Nobody got fired. We got on the board. Peace broke out immediately. It was unnecessary to have that proxy fight, and the stock went from the 70s to 60. So, we were happy, and I think anybody else was happy. We didn't do it by hiving off businesses, and we didn't do it by leveraging the balance sheet. We just got sales up, and profits went along with it. We changed some things structurally in the company, and that's our goal all the time. These are not personal issues. To follow up and build on that, I know you don't like the word activism. Fei Fei Li, World Labs CEO: A Fortt Knox Update

Fortt Knox(10.7K) ·10:58 · 2026-02-03
Fei Fei Li Jon Fortt
> "This is a civilizational technology...
TL;DR Dr. Fei Fei Li, CEO of World Labs, asserts AI's status as a pervasive "civilizational technology" impacting every aspect of life, underscoring the critical need for diverse global participation and localized understanding, while identifying spatial intelligence as the next...

TL;DR Dr. Fei Fei Li, CEO of World Labs, asserts AI's status as a pervasive "civilizational technology" impacting every aspect of life, underscoring the critical need for diverse global participation and localized understanding, while identifying spatial intelligence as the next major frontier beyond current language models.

The Pervasive Nature of AI

  • Transformational Speed: While AI's potential was always recognized, its breathtaking speed of development over the past decade, especially the last few years, has surpassed expectations, leading to widespread industry adoption and societal discourse.
  • Civilizational Technology: AI is a foundational technology akin to the invention of chips, influencing healthcare, agriculture, education, robotics, energy, and sustainability, impacting every aspect of human life and work.
  • AI as "New Compute": Dr. Li explains that just as chips are the hardware foundation for computing in virtually every device (from light bulbs to cars), AI represents the "new compute," meaning any system reliant on chip-based computing will eventually depend on AI.

The Imperative of Global & Localized AI Development

  • Universal Participation: It is crucial for the "entire global population" to participate in AI development, not just a variety of voices, given the technology's profound impact on shaping versions of the world.
  • Localized AI Models: There's a vital need for AI to genuinely understand specific languages, cultures, and ways of life to ensure inclusivity and prevent large segments of the global population from being excluded.
      • Example: The "Typhoon" project by SCB 10X in Bangkok, creating AI models in native Thai language, demonstrates this localized approach beyond reliance on English or Mandarin.
  • Market-Driven Demand: Governments, regions, and markets worldwide are actively recognizing and driving the demand for localized AI, seeking to integrate specific cultural contexts and shared experiences into AI systems.

Spatial Intelligence: The Next Frontier for AI

  • Beyond Language Models: While language models are significant, Dr. Li, whose career began in computer vision, identifies spatial perceptual intelligence as the next major frontier for AI.
      • Human Intelligence Parallel: Human intelligence is fundamentally spatial, perceptual, and embodied (e.g., daily actions like making breakfast or driving), extending far beyond verbal communication.
  • World Labs' Focus: World Labs, founded two years ago, is dedicated to developing "next-generation frontier models" for spatial intelligence.
      • Capabilities: These models aim to reason, understand, interact with, and generate 3D/4D worlds.
      • Applications: Key areas include simulation, robotics, creativity, design, education, healthcare, and manufacturing.
  • Active World Modeling: This approach goes beyond passive image reasoning, enabling agentic, computed, and proactive interactions within the physical world, which language alone cannot fully describe (e.g., the nuances of cooking pasta).

Real-World Applications & Gaming

  • Gaming and Interactive Experiences: This sector is highlighted as a particularly exciting market for spatial intelligence applications.
  • Marble by World Labs: The company released its first model and prototype, "Marble by World Labs," allowing game developers to create games and innovate with these new tools.
  • Empowering Creativity: The early adoption by developers for smaller-scale games demonstrates the immediate potential for empowering creativity and innovation within the gaming industry.

Notable Quotes

"This is a civilizational technology... every aspect of our life to every aspect of our work will be impacted by this technology." — Dr. Fei Fei Li "AI is the new compute. That means any place, any piece of device, equipment or scenario that depends on chip computing on a chip, it depends on computing with AI." — Dr. Fei Fei Li "It's so important that at this point our community our society understand the profoundness of this technology." — Dr. Fei Fei Li "Beyond language model, I see the next chapter, next frontier of AI in spatial intelligence." — Dr. Fei Fei Li "So much of the physical world's process whether it's is initiated or or or or interacted with human or not human is beyond the description of language." — Dr. Fei Fei Li
Dr. Fei-Fei Li, thanks for sitting down for Fort Knox and CNBC. Thanks for inviting me. They call you the godmother of AI because you have done so much foundational work in shaping this reality that the business world is moving into today. Is it working out the way you expected? Yes and no. First of all, no one can singularly own any field. The transformation and revolution we are seeing in modern AI is a result of generations of scientists and technologists. For those of us who have been in this field long enough, we obviously believed in the power of AI and how incredible this science could be. It's a civilizational technology, but the breathtaking speed of development in the past almost 10 years, and especially in the past few years, has been just beyond many of our wildest dreams. Looking at the entire industry embracing it, and also society talking about AI from many different perspectives and dimensions with strong emotions, is something that still feels a little surreal, but also gives me the weight of responsibility. You've done a lot of work to make sure a broad range of people and perspectives have input into how AI is being developed and the opportunity to work with it. You've talked about women in AI, for example, and I wonder if you can sketch out for me what you see the stakes being. In the past we've talked about representation of different perspectives in literature and in media, but now we're building pretty literally versions of the world that are going to influence how the real world works. Why is it important to have a variety of voices there? It's not just a variety of voices; it's important to have the participation of the entire global population in AI because this is a civilizational technology. Whether we're talking about a healthcare application or agriculture, education transformation, robotics, energy, and sustainability, every aspect of our life and every aspect of our work will be impacted by this technology. People ask me what AI is and how to think about its reach and scope. It's not an analogy; one example I use is to think about where chips can be. We think about chips as if they're large, big energy consumption chips. That's not true. Almost every light bulb these days will have a tiny chip. Your fridge has a chip, cars have chips, airplanes have a lot of chips. Everywhere there is a chip, from the tiniest to the large ones, there's compute because a chip is the hardware foundation for computing. AI is the new compute. That means any place, any piece of device, equipment, or scenario that depends on computing on a chip, it depends on computing with AI. This is how profound this is. The stake is everything. Of course, it will come in different stages, but it is so important that at this point, our community, our society, understand the profoundness of this technology. One of the ways I've been thinking about this: a few months ago, back in the summer, I had a chance to go to Bangkok for an event that we did at CNBC. An old friend of mine from high school, Casim Thorn Pipachai, was working with SCB 10X on a project called Typhoon, which uses AI models in native Thai language as opposed to relying on English, Mandarin, etc. After visiting there and talking to my friend from high school who has been working on this project, it got me thinking about the implications of localized AI differently. If it really genuinely understands your language, your culture, your way of life, and if it doesn't, if you don't have a role in building that out, who gets left out? Who gets included? Is there enough conversation about how much language and culture influences what AI understands and who potentially gets left out if that's not built in? I do travel around the world and talk to people of all walks of background, governments from Europe to APAC regions to the rest of America. I think people, countries, and regions are absolutely aware of the need for localized AI. I know that we're focusing and talking about language models, which is very important. Your example is a language model. AI is not just language models, but even just focusing on language models, this need for localized AI is very important for people. The culture, the context, the shared experiences, the situational nuances for people in different parts of the world and for people coming from different backgrounds or different sectors, from artists to manufacturers, it's absolutely important. I think the market is driving this. It's not just a top-down, "we think we must do this" approach; I think the market and the people are asking for it. I'm seeing a very healthy interest from many regions and many governments in wanting to do that. You mentioned AI is not just language. That's a good segue to World Labs. You're very focused on not just text prompts and responses, but immersive, three-dimensional, physics-influenced interactions between things. I imagine there are textures involved. You could even probably do cooking simulations at a certain point. What is that going to enable, you expect, five, 10 years from now? What kind of parallel might you draw to the image work that you did years back? My career started in computer vision, which is a subfield of AI, just like some of my colleagues' careers started in natural language processing, which eventually, that field and the rest of AI, led to GPT-like technology. Having worked in vision-based AI all my career, I know that spatial perceptual intelligence is a lynchpin of intelligence from an evolutionary as well as human perspective. We don't just talk. We wake up in the morning hugging our children. We make breakfast for them. We drive them to school. We go to work ourselves. We order a cup of coffee and know how to drink it. So much of our daily life, as well as our civilization's work, is spatial, perceptual, and embodied intelligence. Beyond language models, I see the next chapter, the next frontier of AI, in spatial intelligence. This is why we founded World Labs barely two years ago. We are focusing on creating the next generation of frontier models that is going to help us to reason, to understand, to interact, and to generate 3D/4D worlds that would empower applications in simulation, robotics, creativity, design, education, healthcare, manufacturing, and many, many horizontal use cases of spatial intelligence. Because it's the equivalent of image capture and what that can do for teaching a system how things move, what happens when they bump into each other, whether something shatters or is resilient—all of that you can't describe with just language. No, you can't. First of all, it goes beyond just using an image to reason because that is still more passive. With world modeling and spatial intelligence, it can be agentic; it can be computed; it can be more proactive for embodied agents. Just the examples I give: even if you're making a meal, a simple meal of making pasta, that whole process—one could imagine using language to describe, let's say, 15 or 20 minutes of that process, but it's still a lossy representation. The nuance of how you cook the salt, how you put the pasta in the water, what the pasta does in the water, is impossible to use language alone to describe. So much of the physical world's process, whether it's initiated or interacted with by human or non-human, is beyond the description of language. One last question for you. I know you have a lot to do and see, and a lot of people to talk to here today, so I appreciate the time. One particular application of what you're developing in World Labs that jumps to mind is gaming. There's a lot of profit, innovation, and teamwork to be had there. How much should we expect some of that work to be manifested in quicker, more effective development in spaces like that as a way of spreading its influence? You're totally right. Gaming and interactive experiences is one of the markets that we're very excited by. Last year, about two months ago, as you know we're still in early February, we released our first model and a prototype of our product called Marble by World Labs. Already there are so many gaming developers using these tools and having fun, showing us the kind of right now still smaller level games they're making. It just excites us that we're already empowering that kind of creativity and innovation among game developers. Excited to see what happens next. Dr. Fei-Fei Li, thank you for your time.

Why The Laws of Startup Physics Have Changed | Ben Horowitz Interview

Invest Like The Best(38.0K) ·62:09 ·9.6K views · 2026-02-03
Ben Horowitz Patrick O'Shaughnessy
Ben Horowitz believes the U.S.
Here is an executive briefing summarizing Ben Horowitz's interview on "Invest Like The Best": TL;DR Ben Horowitz believes the U.S. tech sector is exceptionally healthy, driven by an entrepreneurial culture and the transformative power of AI, which is redefining company building...

Here is an executive briefing summarizing Ben Horowitz's interview on "Invest Like The Best":

TL;DR

Ben Horowitz believes the U.S. tech sector is exceptionally healthy, driven by an entrepreneurial culture and the transformative power of AI, which is redefining company building and offering solutions to previously intractable problems. He emphasizes that America's leadership in AI is crucial for its global standing, advocating for technology over policy solutions and stressing action-oriented culture in organizations.

The AI Revolution & America's Strategic Advantage

  • U.S. Tech Sector Strength: The American tech sector is robust, competitive, and boasts an outstanding entrepreneurial culture, which other nations struggle to replicate due to regulatory and cultural challenges. The broader economy is also improving, benefiting from lower energy prices, reduced regulation, and a more favorable tax code.
  • AI's Transformative Potential: AI is poised to impact every aspect of society, offering potential solutions for major challenges from auto deaths to cancer. Its rapid adoption is facilitated by existing internet infrastructure, allowing businesses to integrate AI without significant new infrastructure builds.
  • Policy Risks to Innovation: Ben Horowitz warns that ill-conceived government policy poses the greatest threat to America's technological leadership, citing past proposals to outlaw AI or restrict GPU sales. He asserts that Technology Solutions are Superior to Policy: effective technological innovation (e.g., vaccines, safe nuclear power, policing technology) generally outperforms policy-driven interventions in solving complex problems.

Shifting Laws of Company Building & Talent Dynamics

  • Changed Market Dynamics: The emergence of AI is altering the "laws of physics" for company building and investment.
      • Everything is Up for Grabs: While established software giants remain formidable, AI makes it easier for new entrants to "make their own" solutions.
      • Hyper-Accelerated Growth: AI products are achieving unprecedented revenue growth rates, significantly faster than previous tech generations (e.g., Cursor reaching $1B in "no time").
      • Catch-Up Potential: Unlike traditional software, deep pockets and access to data/GPUs can now enable rapid market entry and competitiveness in large model development (e.g., Elon catching up with major AI models). This changes competitive barriers and valuation methodologies, as market sizes could be astronomically larger (e.g., $5 trillion vs. $50 billion).
  • "Alchemistic" AI Talent: There's a severe scarcity of top-tier AI researchers with practical experience building large models, particularly those from leading labs like Google, Facebook, or OpenAI. This "alchemistic" knowledge, not taught in academia, drives astronomical valuations for such talent ($100M-$1B for a researcher).
  • Inequality and Opportunity:
      • "Kobe Bryant Effect": AI amplifies the existing trend where top performers achieve outsized wealth and influence due to global reach, a phenomenon Ben calls the "Kobe Bryant effect."
      • AI as an Equalizer: Despite the concentration of wealth, AI also acts as a profound equalizer by democratizing access to "super intelligence" via smartphones, offering opportunities like world-class education (e.g., an advanced tutor for every child).
      • Unpredictable Job Market: Historically, automation creates new, unforeseen job categories while eliminating old ones. Predicting AI's precise impact on the labor market and job creation is difficult and often oversimplified.

a16z's Mission: Expanding the Tech Frontier

  • Strategic Imperative for America: Ben views the current AI era as a shift comparable to the Industrial Revolution. He believes America's continued leadership in technology is essential to maintain its economic, military, and cultural influence. a16z aims to play a critical role in fostering the next generation of American tech companies.
  • Founding Principles & Evolution of a16z:
      • Disrupting Traditional VC: a16z was founded to create a "better product for entrepreneurs," contrasting with a VC industry historically focused on LPs and relying on established reputations.
      • Empowering Founders: The core idea was to provide founders with the confidence, knowledge, network, and advice needed to succeed as CEOs.
      • Marketing Strategy: Breaking from traditional VC secrecy, a16z adopted an open, communicative approach, including naming the firm after its founders to signal long-term commitment.
      • Scaling Venture Capital: The firm evolved to scale beyond traditional VC models, developing specialized investment teams (e.g., bio, crypto) each with a "platform" to support portfolio companies through various growth stages (multi-product, multi-channel, multi-geography).
      • Cultural Alignment: a16z intentionally avoids private equity AI rollups, as its mission is about building new things and growth, rather than optimizing existing assets through cost-cutting or layoffs.
  • Defining Culture by Action: Influenced by Bushido, Ben emphasizes that Culture is a Set of Actions, Not Ideas. Corporate values are meaningless without specific, enforced behaviors. Examples include punctuality for entrepreneur meetings (once fined $10/minute), thoroughly explaining "no" investment decisions, and strictly prohibiting public criticism of entrepreneurs.

Technology for Societal Impact: The LVPD Case Study

  • Unique Context: Ben highlights the Las Vegas Police Department (LVPD) as an ideal testbed for advanced policing technology due to its unique structure (elected sheriff, not mayor-controlled), focus on community policing, and exceptionally high murder clearance rate (94%).
  • Technology Deployment & Results: Ben has funded LVPD's initiative to become the most high-tech police force.
      • Drones & AI Cameras: A drone program ensures deployment within 90 seconds of a 911 call or gunshot, streaming live video to officers. AI cameras enhance surveillance and identification.
      • Dramatic Safety Improvements: Since the program's inception, crime is down over 50%, and police shootings of suspects are down nearly 75%. This is attributed to intelligence making dangerous situations dramatically safer for all parties involved (suspects, citizens, and police).
  • Broader Implications: The initiative has also restored pride in policing, leading to a higher standard for recruits in Las Vegas, a city with a high concentration of veterans. This demonstrates how technology can address systemic issues and yield positive societal and cultural impacts.

When Ben Horowitz and his partner Mark Andre came into the venture capital industry, it was very different than it is today. You can argue that it is them, more than almost anyone else, who has reshaped this industry and matured it so much ever since. Andreessen Horowitz has become one of the most important institutions—not just investors, but institutions—in the private investing landscape, having achieved a scale that no one thought was possible in venture, which was always supposed to be this small, tiny niche corner of the world. This conversation is a bit unique relative to some of the other ones that Ben has had more recently. I tried to understand the shaping forces and influences in his life and the ways that he thinks America most needs to change. He's taken this as his life's mission to build a firm that affects outcomes in the country, not just in a small niche part of the market, but very broadly. We even had discussions in this conversation about his work with, say, the Las Vegas Police Department, which he's tried to infuse with technology to lower crime rates across the system. I hope you enjoyed this great and wide-ranging conversation with Ben Horowitz. A fun place to begin, Ben, would be your take on the state of the country. What does it feel like to you in 2026? I know part of your mission is to directly impact the trajectory of the country. We'll talk about that a lot. But begin with what the landscape, the playing field, looks like to you today. I think the tech sector is very, very healthy. America's competitiveness is very, very good. The entrepreneurship culture is outstanding, and that's the main thing I look at from my lens. I go all over the world, and everybody wants Silicon Valley—"How can we have Silicon Valley in the UK? How can we have it in France?" The thing that's lacking, I would say, is that they have a lot of the ingredients. They have great talent, they've got great universities. They definitely have a worse regulatory environment in the EU, an increasingly bad regulatory environment for entrepreneurship. But there's a cultural challenge where succeeding, doing something larger than yourself, making the world a better place—those aren't things that young people feel society values. And so, the likelihood, if you're building a company, of getting people to work for you and dedicate their life to a mission like that is just not that great. Whereas in the US, it's amazing. I think the economy is in much better shape than people realize and are starting to see that. We've done a lot of things to stimulate it. We've got lower energy prices, much less regulation, and a more user-friendly tax code. That's all starting to kick in now. From our perspective, the bigger thing is that AI is going to impact everything. There's almost no problem you can think of that you can't go, "Well, we have a real shot at solving that with AI." From the big problems in the US like auto deaths—we've got an AI solution for that. Cancer—we have an AI solution for that. The fact that we've got a technology where we can address everything is a real new phenomenon, and all that's going to kick in in a fairly major way over the next 12 to 24 months. Why do you think 12 to 24 months is a time frame worth mentioning that some of this stuff will start to be felt more broadly? It's all starting to take effect now, and it's got to roll out, get deployed. Deployments of technology in the past have taken a long time. You had to build out the infrastructure to do it. For cars, you needed things like roads and traffic lights and all that kind of thing. For the internet, you needed fiber in the ground, and people to have smartphones, and you needed to do a lot just to get going. The internet is here. So, if you want to use AI, if you want to apply it to your business, you just do it. There is no infrastructure that needs to be built to adopt the thing. What could most interrupt this good trajectory that America is on where we are building solutions using technology? What are the biggest risks? I think policy. One of the things my father said to me was a bad government, no matter how many smart people you have, no matter how great a culture you have, no matter how great the country is, can ruin the whole thing. Venezuela was the fourth richest country in the world, and then communism took over, and that was that. If you look at how little comes out of so many of these countries in Europe that have so many smart people, and then the ones that went into communism—there are so many genius Romanian entrepreneurs, John von Neumann, and the number of great genius scientists that came out of Hungary, this little country, and then it was just gone once the communists took over, completely nothing from inventing everything to nothing overnight. I think that can absolutely happen here. We could outlaw AI. There were pretty aggressive proposals. The last Biden administration executive order said that you could not sell a GPU without federal government approval. That was a real executive order, and it got reversed. But we were that close to being basically out of the global chip game. So it is fragile. By the way, technology solutions work much better than policy solutions. That's the other thing. Policy solutions are very hard to make anything work. If you think about COVID, we could tell everybody to stay in their house. That had some extremely bad side effects and turned out not to work that well. Or we could invent a drug that cures it or a vaccine that works. It's just hard to have a policy solution, like all the policy stuff on climate change. Europe actually reduced emissions and all that, but it didn't do anything because China didn't reduce emissions. But if you build a technology, a really safe nuclear fission or nuclear fusion facility, that would have a big effect. And I think in general that's true. Defunding the police did not make anybody safer; technology does. So, if you really want to change the world, if you really want to make it a better place, I think you can build a solution for darn near anything. If you want to change the world for the better, it's never been a better time to be an entrepreneur. I was with a local restaurateur yesterday here in New York, one of the best, for a couple hours, having him describe how he is planning on using AI tooling to improve everything about his restaurant business. How do you think about the way all of this is changing the potentially large, attractive businesses that you want to invest in? For example, with restaurants, Toast is a great company; there are many great companies that have been built in and around restaurant software businesses. It seems like this restaurant owner is going to be able to have his own spun-up operating system specific to him, not going to need any of that stuff. How is this changing the way in which you view investment opportunities? On the positive, everything is up for grabs. I think people are overreacting to that in the stock market and so forth. If you look at existing software companies, people think, "Oh, they're all dead." Well, some of these guys are extremely hard targets. It's not that easy to take out Salesforce or SAP. You would be surprised, even with AI, how much heavy lifting that is. Having said that, it is true that a lot of these things, yes, you can just make your own, you can do it yourself. It's going to be a lot easier. The number of possible interesting companies, I think, went up a lot. The other thing we're seeing is that the products work so much better than any technology products we've seen in the past that revenue growth is so much faster for these AI companies. There are many such cases of companies coming out. Cursor, which is ostensibly an IDE—what was the biggest IDE before Cursor? I don't know, but it wasn't big, and it took probably 12 or 15 years to get to that revenue level. They went over a billion dollars in revenue in no time. So that's super interesting. I would say from an investing standpoint, the laws of physics of company building changed, which is going to affect investing in what's currently an unknown way. If you look at the one thing you knew if you'd ever built a software company is you cannot throw money at the problem. You know, "What's a man-year?" "700 IBMers before lunch." That phenomenon everything was built on, because you knew if somebody built a great product and it took them three years and they did it with a small team, Google's not going to hire 2,000 engineers and catch them. It's just not going to happen. That was a law of physics. Now, if you have the data and you have enough GPUs, you can solve damn near anything. We've seen that with Elon catching the big models in no time. He just took a lot of money and a really good data center design and some smart engineers. He's in the game; he got in the game very fast. That would have never happened in the past. The markets also seem to be much, much bigger than anything we've ever seen. So it would cause you to think about valuations and long-term value and other sorts of things in a different way than we have in the past. On the one hand, it's like, well, when you calculate the long-term value, what if this market wasn't $50 billion? What if it was $5 trillion? And then on the other end, what if somebody could catch you? These are just concepts we've not dealt with. So how would the conversations feel different to me if I came in? You've got all these great investors working at Andreessen Horowitz. What is the nature of the conversation amongst your teammates as they're debating this sort of stuff versus four years ago or something? Where does it feel most materially different internally? I would say one of the most different things is when you look at AI researchers, it is really a different kind of thing. If you haven't been at Google or Facebook or OpenAI or Anthropic, and somebody gave you hundreds of millions of dollars to try and build a giant model, and you weren't one of the main people, then you probably don't know how to do it because you can't learn it in school. You can't learn it in school because it's a little bit alchemistic in nature. It's a little bit of an art. And so if you've never done it before, the chance of on your very first try of building some kind of large model that it's going to work well isn't that great. Now, people are coming up to speed more; there are more companies, people are learning it. But that's why you got to this, which from the outside world probably looked absolutely bananas, that, "Well, why is somebody paying a hundred million dollars for an AI researcher or a billion dollars for an AI researcher? That's the craziest thing I've ever heard." Well, what if there were only 40 of them in the world? And you have a $4 trillion company. Yes, then it kind of changes the math on it a little bit. I think that's sort of where we were because it's the first time we've had a need for a technologist that academia couldn't produce. That is probably one of the bigger things that changed in the conversation, like, "Who are all these people?" We track all of them and know what they're doing, but it's very different. Visit workos.com to skip the unglamorous infrastructure work and focus on your product. Everyone talks in venture about the power law. The thing underneath the power law is a sort of inequality. It seems like so many of the things that are happening are just massive multipliers on the trend of inequality in every way: the billion-dollar researcher, the size of the biggest companies, the wealth of the people creating those companies. I would argue that inequality is a feature, not a bug, of the American system. But I'm curious for you to riff on the nature of growing inequality and the good and the bad associated with that. What's happening in AI is an extension of the Kobe Bryant effect. A basketball player when James Naismith invented the game, there was a limited amount of money you could make because you basically played the game in front of the people who could show up for the game, and that was it; that's the whole market. Whereas once you add television and the global audience and these kinds of things, you can get to a much bigger scale. You can be LeBron James; you can become a billionaire, and that just was not at all possible before. I think we first saw that with the internet where, "Okay, now I can build a product and I can get to global distribution very fast, then all of a sudden I can become extremely rich." And then AI is another layer on top of that. "Okay, now take that same product and make it just a more valuable thing." So whoever invents that is whatever the internet company was, plus-plus, and so that's going to make them even richer. That's the bad part of it. I think the good part of it is it's starting out day one completely democratized. Anybody gets access to very powerful AI. Anybody who has a phone—and most people in the world at this point have smartphones—now has super intelligence in their phone. So that's a big equalizer of the opportunity in a lot of ways. I don't think we've ever seen a bigger opportunity equalizer than AI. Every child can have a super advanced, amazing tutor teacher. So great education is accessible to all now. So I think it's an equalizing technology. There's some drive in inequality, and this is another thing I learned from my father. He said, "Look, son, life isn't fair," and that's extremely good advice because it's just not going to be fair. No matter what government or anything tries to do, it's not going to be fair. And the problem is, if you create a system that tries to correct that, it doesn't make things more fair. It just transfers all the power to the person running the system. And that's what happened with Stalin, that's what happened with Ceaușescu, that's what happened with Pol Pot, that's what happened with Mao. It's not an accident that every single system like that went bad because it really ends up just being a power transfer. When you think about what you want, you'd like everybody to have a chance. Don't give me no chance; give me some chance. Now, it may not be as big a chance as the other guy. It may not be a perfect chance. But if I have the desire, if I've got some capability, give me a chance to be something, to make my imprint on the world. And a system like that is going to end up with a lot of inequality. By the way, all systems end up with a lot of inequality. But you can try systematically to give everybody an opportunity, and I think AI does a really good job of that. One of the memes that's very popular today is that you have a couple years to get some capital, or you're going to be a part of the permanent underclass is the phrase that is used on Twitter. I certainly agree that now everyone has the best lawyer, accountant, adviser in their pocket, and that's amazing. But what do you think about this notion that because of that, we need less labor? It's going to be harder if you don't have some capital to begin with to accumulate capital and break in. I don't necessarily believe that. I'm just curious what you think about challenges we'll face because of AI society. I don't really think that's right. I don't think the door is going to close behind you. I think opportunities tend to multiply when you open up a new door and open up a new way of doing things. We saw that with crypto. So many people who made money on crypto were people who literally didn't have much to start with. They just got into the technology early, and then they parlayed it up. If you have something that grows really fast, that's actually the opportunity for somebody with a little bit of capital to make a lot of money because it doesn't take much. If you bought Bitcoin for a nickel, you did really well, and all you needed was a nickel. That's the nature of these things that go hyperbolic. Particularly if you create something. I also think the labor market stuff, people are acting as though it's very predictable when it's not at all predictable. If you look at the history of the world and automation—and this is what it is, it's an automation technology—we've been automating things since the agricultural days. In those days, I think 95 or 96% of all jobs in the US were agriculture. Almost all those jobs have been eliminated, and the jobs we have now the people doing agriculture wouldn't even consider jobs. So the idea that we could imagine all the jobs that are going to come, sitting here, that AI is going to enable, I think is low. I think the need for more creativity jobs is going to go way up, and the need for jobs to process work for the creatives will probably go down in some ways, but I'm not even sure about that. We've had AI going, right? ImageNet was what, 2012, and then natural language stuff and BERT and all that was 2015, and then ChatGPT was 2022. Where's all the job destruction? Why hasn't it happened yet? And why are you so sure it's going to happen next? And why are you so sure no jobs are going to be created? I don't think it's nearly as predictable as people are saying. How would you describe the nature and scope of your ambition over the next 10-20 years? One of the things that I learned from a mentor, a great CEO by the name of Andy Grove. He was the CEO of Intel, and he famously did the major pivot of them out of the memory business into the microprocessor business. Maybe the greatest tech CEO we've had. One of the things he said, which in a way is very obvious but I think is also profound, is that if you're the leader in the industry, then the growth of the industry is dependent on you. It's up to you to expand the market; nobody else is going to do it. So when I think about the firm, I think of it a lot in those terms. The reason America is America, and there are many narratives on this, but I think the factual one is that we won the industrial revolution. We really did. We had Henry Ford and we had Thomas Edison. We had great entrepreneurs. They built great technology. The technology led to a military lead, led to an economic lead, led to cultural dominance. None of that was by accident. Had we not had all those inventions, had all those companies, which led to everything from winning World War II, we just wouldn't be—we'd be some other thing. We wouldn't be America. So, we're there again. This is the equivalent change of the industrial revolution in terms of how everything works: governments, societies, businesses. And we're either going to be the leader of that technology, the provider of that technology, or we're not. And if we're not, we're not going to be the economic superpower, the military superpower, the cultural influence, the standard of the world that we are now. At least I think that would be bad. I think America's been good for the world and good for giving people a chance, like we talked about before. So our role in that, trying to be humble with the role, but our role is from a policy standpoint, from a funding standpoint, from a helping people build standpoint, to make sure that the next set of great companies comes out of America or allied nations. A core ambition is to do our part in helping that. I want to ask about some of the ingredients to do that well. But just as a quick sidebar on Andy Grove, his book is incredible. Everyone should read *High Output Management*. What specifically did you learn from him? What did you see him do that impacted the way that you think or behave? I'm so overly influenced by him, it's hard to even pin it down. For *High Output Management*, I actually wrote the new foreword for it, which I actually think is the best thing I ever wrote, was a foreword to *High Output Management*. The reason I wrote that foreword was that it was my favorite book, and I wrote *The Hard Thing About Hard Things* which was basically intended to be the updated version of it. But the thing in *High Output Management* that he did so well that I tried to do my own version of is the concepts of management are easy. You need an eighth-grade education maybe to understand management. It's not like physics; it's pretty simple. But the psychological part of it is extremely difficult, particularly for a young person to be able to do. It's super confrontational. You're having to look through the conversation you're having to the entire organization. You really have to be Confucian at times. The good of the whole supersedes the good of the individual, and all these things are really complicated to do. His big influence on me was me trying to not only absorb that but then tell it in a more up-to-date, modern way. I went to visit him. He had this award on the wall, which was literally "Manager of the Year for the Santa Clara facility of Intel" from, I don't know, 1992. I said, "Andy, we're the biggest CEO in the world, why did they give you the manager of the year award for the Santa Clara facility?" And he goes, "Oh, man. Santa Clara always scored the lowest quality scores, the lowest score on everything at Intel. So I was just like, 'I'm going over there and talk to them.'" So I go over there, and he said, "I brought a roll of toilet paper and I put it under my desk, under my chair. I said, 'When are you going to get this facility up to code?' And they just started in with all this nonsense. And I reached under my chair and put all the toilet paper up. I said, 'Clean up your crap and tell me when you're going to be up to code.'" And in two months, they were up to code, and they were always the highest-rated facility thereafter, just on that. So they gave him Manager of the Year for that. When did you first experience the lessons that drove his success, this confrontational, psychologically difficult aspect of management yourself? How would you encourage other people to get a taste of it? You can't just read about it, obviously. What happens to founders is you invent something. Now I've got to build a company. You don't know what you're doing, and you make mistakes. Then those mistakes really cost the company, and you lose confidence, and that leads you to hesitate. That hesitation is what causes the failure mode. So then either the company's indecisive, or they get very open. All these guys get so open to input from their team and their executives, but the team doesn't have the full context. Only the leader has the context. So even if they're smarter than you, you still likely can have better judgment because you have all the knowledge. But they defer, and if you defer to people who work for you, that creates a weird political situation because people jump into the vacuum, like, "You're not making the decision, I'll make the decision." And then that feels political to everybody else. So that's the pattern people run into. You really have to build up enough confidence in them to have that confrontation. The hardest version of this, by the way, is the reorg. Because a reorg is basically redistributing power to make the company work better, to have communication be better, to not have as much conflict. But what's going to happen is somebody who's really good, who you've had for a long time, is going to lose power, and they're going to be pissed. And if you compromise the organization so they can maintain their power, then you've just redistributed power from the people doing all the work to the executives, and that's a catastrophe. So it's always that kind of thing where people don't want to have that confrontation. They don't want to tell that person, "Look, the organization's here. You helped us get here, but you either have to be happy in this new role, or it's going to be a wrap." When you're young and inexperienced, it's going to hurt to tell him that, but I don't know if it's going to help me to do this reorg because I'm not experienced enough to know that. I've never done that before. So I'm going to go with the known avoid-hurt to the theoretical avoid-hurt, and that's when you wreck your company. That's the pattern, and I always do my best to lend them my experience on that. You were lucky that when you started Andreessen Horowitz, you and Mark had both had tons of operating experience, both together. I still didn't know what I was doing as CEO. Fair enough. And he didn't know what he was doing either. His ideas now, if you ask Mark about management now, he's so different than how he actually did it. And it actually makes him mad if you talk about it too much because he's like, "I got such bad advice. They told me to hire all these guys." How do you think he's most different? What would he say is, or what do you observe to him to be, the most different? I just think he's way more in control of his emotions. Mark is a super emotional person, and he's just way more in control of it than he was then. Just in terms of the personality. He used to be like zero or 100, right? So he would be full of emotion, like, "What are we doing?" Or, "I'm just not going to say anything," but nothing in between. Something I know the least about your firm is the first, I don't know, three days, three months, three years. And I'd love to hear about how you thought about the business right as it was getting started. Of course, I'm going to come back to what it is now and those ingredients you mentioned for having the impact you want to have. But Silicon Valley, Wall Street, these are institutions that make America great. Lots of people listening have ambitions to do this sort of thing, and I'd love to hear the very, very early primordial case study of what it was like and what kinds of conversations you were having and what your initial ideas were. So venture capital, first of all, you have to understand the context of it. There hadn't really been new top-tier venture capital firms. The last one before we started that you would say is top-tier was probably Benchmark, which ostensibly started in 1995, but it didn't really because all those guys came from another firm called Merrill Pickard, and that firm was from the 80s. There hadn't really been a new one from the 80s. If you looked at why, every VC was reputation-based. To be top-tier, you had to have invested in Apple and Cisco and Google and Yahoo and all the great companies. You can't from a standing start get to that. If you're not top-tier in VC, you're not going to last because in a super hot period, everybody makes money, but the best entrepreneurs will only work with the top-tier firms because that's how you're going to recruit great engineers, that's how you're going to get follow-on money. Everything comes out of that. So you'd never take money from a Tier 2 if you could get it from Tier 1. That's why the Tier 1s always have better returns. So we knew we had to be Tier 1, but we had that problem. The idea that we had was, well, venture capital is a great product for LPs, but it's not a great product for entrepreneurs. So, if we could build a better product for entrepreneurs, then we could win. And that was the original framework. The idea that we had for the product for entrepreneurs, because we had been entrepreneurs, was around what you and I had been talking about, which is, if you're a founder who wants to run their own company, you're not getting much. You need so much. You don't have the confidence, you don't have the knowledge, you don't have the know-how, you don't have the network. What if we built a firm that was designed to give you enough confidence, power, network reach, advice that you could actually be a CEO? And so that was the whole idea behind the firm originally. The second idea we had, which was the other thing, was that VCs didn't ever market themselves at all because if you're all based on your investing track record, it's best that it's just magic. Why say anything? Keep that a secret. So they weren't talking. And so when we went out and talked, everybody covered it. So instantly everybody knew we had this product. Where did the germ of that specific idea come from, to be fairly loud relative to what others do from the very beginning? It's funny because Mark and I were talking about it. He said to me, "Why don't VCs market?" And actually, the original thing went all the way back to the first class of VCs, which were the investor revolution VCs: JP Morgan, Rothschild, Goldman Sachs, et cetera. They were the ones financing these things. And it turned out that these guys were financing both sides of World War II, and so they really didn't want any publicity because that would have been an extremely bad thing. To a large extent, that just carried over all the way through Arthur Rock and all these things, and then the reputation thing clicked in, and it was working, so there was no need to do it. We got a lot of criticism when we did it. Our LPs would say, "The other VCs say, 'You guys are egomaniacs. You name the firm after yourself. You're marketing it like this.'" And it was so funny because the reason we named the firm after ourselves is when we tried to raise money in 2009, which is right on the edge of the financial crisis, the big objection from LPs was, "Well, you guys are really good entrepreneurs. You're just going to leave this thing and go build another company, and then we're going to be stuck with the fund." And we couldn't get them off of that. And so then I had the idea. I was like, "Well, why don't we just name it with our names, and then they know we're safe." And that worked.
