If you think about the period of takeoff of the firm in 2009 up until you reach, let's call it, cruising altitude, when was cruising altitude, and what was the most difficult part about getting it from takeoff to that point? Well, the first thing is we really didn't know that much about investing. Mark and I had done some angel investing, but neither of us had any venture capital experience. Credit to Sequoia, credit to Greylock and Kleiner, and all the guys who were around at that time, they just had years and years of doing it. We made more than our fair share of investing mistakes, missing things we should have done, and then doing things that we shouldn't have done. But missing things that we should have done was probably the bigger one. The other thing is that how we thought about the profile of the investor was wrong. We so over-indexed on our idea that we had to help the founder become a CEO that we made it a requirement that you couldn't be an investor at Andreessen Horowitz if you hadn't founded and/or run a company. That was a very good attitude and set the culture of the firm in a lot of ways and had good things that came from it. But I would just say that most CEOs aren't as interested in investing as they think they are, and also most CEOs aren't as good at helping somebody else learn the job. So those two things ended up not quite correct. We made some adjustments. Fund one just went really well because we hit the scene hard. It was a small fund. We did Skype, we did Slack, we did Okta. Stripe was in there. There were just too many good things in a $300 million fund for that thing not to blow the doors off. Fund two wasn't as good as one. By the time we got to three, that's when we had the contention among, "Oh, we really don't have the right profile for GP here." There was a while where we thought that was going to be a terrible fund. It ended up being a great fund because we had Coinbase and DataBricks and Lyft and GitHub, but that one was scary for a while. Coming out of that, we knew what the firm needed to be, and so I think it settled down after that. It wasn't such a startup; it was like, okay, we got across that chasm. But then the bigger thing was, we always had this idea about "software is eating the world," which Mark articulated really well in his 2011 piece. So we always felt like venture capital firms needed to be able to scale, and that the other firms would have trouble scaling because of the way they worked, the way they shared control. So that could be an opportunity for us, but we hadn't figured out how to do it yet. Then starting with the bio and the crypto fund, I started to get to the organizational picture of how we would be able to address every market of technology. But with investing teams that weren't 20 people—that doesn't work. So you need an investing team of four or five people, but you can't address the whole technology market with five people. So you have to have multiple teams. Having multiple teams in a venture capital firm was a little bit of a novel idea, particularly when each team has a platform that helps the founder build the company. So we began it really in earnest with the crypto fund, I think around 2018, and now the whole firm is organized that way. If we zoom now to today and back to what you said, that the scope of your ambition is big, as the leader, to be the one to help expand the market, what are the components of doing that? What does the system need that it doesn't currently have that you might be able to provide? One is the capital markets have changed dramatically with not much help. I went public at 18 months old with $2 million in trailing revenue. That wasn't a good idea. But companies used to go public routinely with $50 million in revenue. It was fine. Now nobody's going public with less than a billion, right? You get to go public, or something like that, and you're kind of small if you don't have that. So you need a lot more out of the private markets than VCs are built to do, and so that's one of the things we have to think about. Another one is the companies in the portfolio. They used to leave you at $100 million in revenue; they're going public, they're off to the races. Well, that's not true anymore. So what do you need when you get to be $200 million, $300 million in revenue? You need to be multi-product, you need to be multi-channel, you need to be multi-geography. So as a venture firm, we need to help them, and as a venture industry, we need to help them do that. Like, "How do I get to Japan? How do I get to South America?" Most venture firms don't provide much along those lines. So we have to step up to those ideas if we're going to have companies in the portfolio at that stage. Do you hope that over time your firm, and maybe some others like it that have become these big institutions in venture, go on to be like the Blackstone, Apollo-type companies that are big, publicly traded, enduring businesses? A big, huge wave among venture capitalists is private equity AI roll-ups. It's a good business idea, a really good business idea, which is, just like the spreadsheet created the original private equity business, AI is creating a new private equity business where you can buy any existing company, optimize it with AI, and it'll be more valuable. That's a good idea. It's a good thing to invest in. It's not something we're going to do for two reasons. One, it's the cultural opposite of who we are. We're about building new things, growth, believing in the entrepreneur. Price doesn't even matter. As long as the thing succeeds, you're going to do well. Private equity is like, entry price is key. I had a great dinner with Marc Rowan, who's a super genius who runs Apollo, and he was like, "Entry price, entry price, entry price." We never even think about that. We think about it, but it's not first and foremost at all, thinking about containing cost and this and that and the other. That's just not what a good venture capital frame of mind is. So culturally, I didn't want to mix those two things. But more than that, I just didn't want to be in a business where the way you make money is you figure out how to optimize an existing thing and lay off people and that kind of thing. We're about new technology companies building the future, taking things forward, and I'll leave that to the other smart guys in the industry. What, if any, trade-offs feel like they might exist at this scale as you continue to scale, as you consider all these different people you're trying to serve? The investors internally, the LPs, the founders—nothing's perfect. What are the trade-offs to the path that you've chosen? I think with any scale of organization, you really have to over-pay attention to culture, or the culture will drift. We probably spend more work on that than any venture capital firm. You're not allowed to join unless you sign the culture document. I spend an hour with every single employee teaching them the culture. It's that level of investment. And then we really try to enforce it hard when we can, and we have pretty good consistency, but that is hard to maintain as you grow. Can you teach me more about culture? You've written a book about it. You've built them. You've studied some very interesting cultures that you wrote about in the book. If you had to teach a seminar on what a culture is in the first place, and then how to design one given what you do and who you are, and then how to make sure people live by it. Let me give you a small but probably the most important insight, which is from Bushido, the way of the warrior, from the samurai. A culture is not a set of ideas; it's a set of actions. If you define your culture as a set of ideas—integrity, do the right thing, we have each other's backs, or any of these ideas they call corporate values—it's actually just a bunch of platitudes; it doesn't mean anything. The culture has to be defined in terms of the exact behavior that you want that supports that idea. What do you have to do to actually be that thing that you want to be? And it's the little things. How responsive are you to your colleagues? What's the SLA on returning a Slack message or an email? Do you show up to meetings on time? Not everybody has those ideas, but if you want that idea, you've got to manifest it through something else. For example, we have an idea about you have to respect the entrepreneur. Well, what is that behavior? One, you can't ever be late to a meeting with an entrepreneur. I used to fine people $10 a minute in the beginning of the firm to reinforce it. And then you have to get back to an entrepreneur. If you say no, you have to say no. You have to explain why you're not investing, and it has to be clear. We're going to survey that entrepreneur after you say no to make sure that you said no and that they had a good experience. So that's a behavior. If you try to make yourself look good by making an entrepreneur look bad, you're fired. So if you get on X and say, "Oh, he's selling dollars for 85 cents"—no, no, no, no, no. We're dream builders. We're not dream killers. Forget that. Somebody wants to do something larger than themselves, build a company, make the world a better place. We're for that. We don't give a damn what the idea is, or if Sequoia funded them or whatever. We love that. That's who we are. So the behavior is the culture, is the actual thing, and that gets you the idea as opposed to the idea and then figuring out how you're going to behave. So that's probably the main thing on culture. Can you say more about the influence your dad had on you? You mentioned that lesson of "nothing's fair" or "life isn't fair." Tell me about your dad. He was what's known as a red diaper baby. My grandparents were communists. They went to secret meetings. They had cards. My grandfather was fired during the McCarthy era from being a junior high school teacher for being a communist. And he grew up a communist. He started out on the left. He was editor of a very famous New Left magazine called *Ramparts* magazine, which he was editor of, and he was involved with the Black Panthers with Huey Newton and the Oakland chapter Eldridge Cleaver. He sort of dropped out of politics and he re-emerged, I guess, probably eight years later, on the right. He really understood the ills of communism and socialism, which helped me a lot. One of the things that he said to me that always stuck with me, he's like, "Son, go to the library, pick any book on socialism. There's hundreds of books. And in that book, I guarantee you, you will find page upon page, chapter upon chapter, of how to divide the wealth. You will not find a single sentence on how to create it, how to make it." And I was like, "Oh, wow. That's not a very good system, is it?" I learned a lot about systems thinking from that, which ended up being, I'd say, very helpful to me as CEO. He wasn't a new age father. He wasn't like that. In the old days, your father wouldn't even talk to you till you got to be 12. And then you get these little snippets of wisdom. One of the ones I actually put in *The Hard Thing About Hard Things*, but I had three kids, I was young, and I remember it was 102 degrees, the air conditioning was broken, the kids were going crazy. One of them poured a whole bottle of apple juice, a gallon of apple juice, into the rug. Apple juice was steaming out of the carpet. But I'm just sitting there looking like I was going to die. And my father looks at me and he goes, "Son, you know what's cheap?" I said, "What?" He goes, "Flowers. Flowers are cheap." I said, "Okay." He said, "You know what's expensive?" I said, "No, what?" He said, "Divorce." And he had been married four times, so he knew what he was talking about. As you look out today in the world, I'm curious what things are captivating you most and maybe even most inspiring you. You have such an interesting perch; you get to see so much at the frontier. What's going on in coding now is quite phenomenal. We went through this period where, "Okay, AI can write code, cool. Okay, you can write code with a lot of security holes, fine." But I think over the winter break, it turned a corner where really, really good programmers were going, "Whoa, oh god, this helps me." I just became a hundred times more productive, and I can't remember any kind of technology where all of a sudden you wake up and the whole world just changed like that. And that's happening on a pretty regular basis, I would say. Then we spent a bunch of time with people in Hollywood who are using AI. I think AI will help you make movies both better and at much lower cost because you can shoot a scene and then have the AI do a variation of that scene. That's very, very good. So you don't have to do, if you're an actress, you have to shoot a scene like 15 or 20 times. Wouldn't it be nice to shoot it three times and then you just take the pieces you like and make it what you want? So I think it's a little underestimated as a tool for creatives. I think that's true in music, too. I was a young person when hip-hop started, and the huge criticism was, "This is not music. They're just taking music and they're remixing it, and they're rapping over it, and it's a bunch of nonsense. It's a novelty." But it was postmodern art, and I think we're going to get into postmodern art with what people will be able to do with AI and music. That was one of the most exciting times in music. The invention of the new art form is when it gets really exciting. What specific people in hip-hop have had the largest impact on you personally, and how? Nas is a very good friend of mine, and he's definitely had a big impact. Just the lens at which he sees the world is so different and interesting for me. We're both very big fans of Rakim, who is kind of like the John Coltrane of rap. Rakim had one of his first big songs called "My Melody." Nas and I are listening to "My Melody," and the first line is, "Turn up the bass, pull up a chair, hand out a cigar, I'm letting knowledge be born. I'm my name." And so he puts it on, hands out a cigar. He pauses it and goes, "Ben, why is he handing out a cigar?" I go, "I don't know why." Then he plays a line, "I'm letting knowledge be born," and he says, "It's a birth, Ben. He's passing out cigars at the birth of knowledge." I was like, "Oh, I've listened to that song a thousand times. I never heard that." I can't tell you how many times he sees or hears something that's there that I don't see. So having somebody I can talk to who has a completely different perspective of all things in life is invaluable. It was interesting; we did the Coinbase deal together, and he had called me two weeks prior to us really seeing that, because he wanted to learn about Bitcoin. I explained to him how it worked, and he was very interested. Then, when I was talking to Chris Dixon, who was working on the deal, I asked, "Tell me about the guys." He replied, "Well, one of them, Fred, is really into hip-hop." I thought, "Okay." So, I brought Nas over to my house, saying, "Have him come over to my house. There's a boxing match on Saturday." I had Nas come over, and that's how we got that deal. He's a big influence on me personally. As a leader and a writer, storytelling is important to me, and I think he's one of the great storytellers of all time, a super genius on that. Is there a CEO comparable to Nas? In the 90s, there was a class of guys like Jay-Z, who famously said, "I'm not just a businessman, I'm a business, man." Massive franchises were born, and these guys became incredibly successful in the business world. That felt more industrialized, almost like a whole process. But with Nas, even his latest album, it still feels like *Illmatic*—it could have come out then or now. There's this weird timeless quality he still possesses, and it's the same with someone like DJ Premier. Do you know anyone else like that in another domain? He seems like such a unique person relative to his peers. Perhaps Jensen Huang. Jensen has a very defined view—agree with it or not—of who he is and what the company is, and that has spanned across eras. It's still the same thing, right? It played in gaming, it played in Bitcoin, it plays in AI, but it's still Nvidia. He never thought he had to change the name of the company. He's gotten better over the years, but in a weird sense, it never felt like he's trying to be current, much like Nas never feels like he's trying to write a hit. Can you tell the story of the work you're doing with the Vegas Police Department? I'm asking about this because it's super interesting, and it feels like an interesting, different example of what the application of this constellation of new technologies might allow for in terms of improved efficiencies. It's such an interesting case study. A couple of things about the Las Vegas Police Force were intriguing to me. The biggest one was that they were different from other police forces in the country because they're a big metropolitan area not run by the chief of police, but by the sheriff. The reason that's important is the sheriff is an elected official and does not report to the mayor. So they never got caught in the big political movement to defund the police, and they were one of the only cities that didn't reduce the police budget or anything like that. They stayed intact, and they're also, interestingly, the one that I knew never militarized; they do community policing, and you can see it in the numbers. The murder clearance rate in Las Vegas is the highest, meaning they solve 94% of murders. San Francisco's clearance rate is about 75%, Chicago's is in the 30s, and the national average is below 60%. I asked Sheriff Kevin McMahill why their murder clearance rate was so high. He said, "Ben, when somebody is murdered, there's always somebody who knows who did it. They just don't talk to the police. But they talk to us because we're part of the community. They know us." I thought, "Wow, that's a great environment to see if this new technology worked." I knew about all the public safety technology because we invested in it through American Dynamism. I said, "Look, we're going to become the highest-tech police force in America, hopefully the world, and I'm just going to fund it." So, we've got a drone program, a prepared 911 system, and Flag Safety AI cameras. If an emergency or 911 call comes in, or if a gunshot goes off, a drone will be deployed within 90 seconds. That drone video feed will be instantly available on every police officer's phone in the vicinity. Since we started the program, crime is down over 50%, and the shooting of suspects by police is down close to 75%. Everybody is safer. The most surprising thing to me about the technology deployment is this: when you talk to the police, they say descriptions cause half the violent confrontations. I asked what they meant. They explained, "Somebody carjacks a car; there's a baby in the back seat. We get a description of the car: 'It's a 2004 blue Hyundai.' Well, it's really a 2008 green Hyundai. But we pull a guy over in a 2004 blue Hyundai, and that person has had bad experiences with the police, and now he's got a gun in the car, and all of a sudden, we've got an incident where an innocent citizen gets harmed or a police officer gets shot." With AI cameras, we know precisely which car it is. We also know there's a baby in the car. So, we're not sending one guy with a gun to see if that's the suspect; we're sending a whole squad, and we're apprehending them safely. Policing is inherently dangerous, but intelligence makes it dramatically safer. I'm a huge believer in this technology for making everyone safer: suspects, criminals, citizens, police—everyone. The other knock-on effect is that it's put the pride back into policing. We used to have a big problem in Vegas where nobody wanted to be a police officer, so we were lowering the standard. Now the standard is really high. Between the super state-of-the-art drone center and these amazingly futuristic and cool-looking Cybertrucks driving around, everyone wants to be a police officer now. Las Vegas happens to have the highest concentration of veterans in the country, so there are plenty of super qualified people to choose from. They all want to be police. It's all gone really well. The last question I ask everyone is the same: What is the kindest thing anyone's ever done for you? A mentor of mine, a fellow by the name of Ken Coleman, was a big executive at Silicon Graphics. When I was a sophomore in college, I got an introduction to him, and he gave me a job as a summer intern. Without that job, I don't know that I ever would have made it to Silicon Valley or had that whole experience. So I would say that was probably the highest impact; he didn't have to do that. It may interest you that that is the most common answer across 500 of these: someone who took a bet when they didn't need to. Ben, it's been a pleasure to finally do this with you after a couple of years of watching and learning from you. Thank you so much for your time. Thank you, Patrick. It was fun.
